

# CyberSocium: Theoretical Foundations of Cybersocial Economics and the Architecture of a Decentralised Socio-Economic Form

## Foundational Article and Technical Specification of GyberExperiment

---

**Version:** 2.0
**License:** Creative Commons Attribution-NonCommercial 4.0 International / GPL-v3 (code sections)
**Authorship:** Gybernaty Research Collective
**Date:** 2026
**Classification:** Interdisciplinary research — political economy, distributed systems, institutional design, cryptoeconomics, social theory

---

## Abstract

This document lays the theoretical foundation of a new interdisciplinary field — **Cybersocial Economics** (*CyberSocial Economics*) — and presents the technical specification of **GyberExperiment** as its first empirical realisation.

We live in an era in which more than five billion people carry in their pockets computing devices whose aggregate power exceeds by orders of magnitude the entire computational technology of the twentieth century. Information technologies have integrated into the fabric of social relations, dissolving geographical, cultural, and now financial-economic boundaries. Yet the institutional infrastructure governing global resources remains a product of the industrial era — centralised, opaque, oriented toward the concentration of capital and power in the hands of a shrinking circle of beneficiaries. Recurring global crises, mounting inequality, and the systemic fragility of existing economic mechanisms signal that none of the prevailing socio-economic formations is capable of providing sustainable and rational management of the resources of a civilisation whose technological power has long demanded precisely such management.

In this work we: (a) formalise the concept of the **CyberSocial Corporation** (CSC) as the fundamental economic unit of a new type — a decentralised, autonomous, self-governing structure belonging to all its participants; (b) introduce the **Principle of Minimum Individual Participation** (PMIP) and demonstrate that, given a sufficient number of participants, it makes projects of arbitrary scale realisable; (c) describe the **mechanism of Socio-Economic Selection** (SES) — an evolutionary process in which projects compete not for investor capital but for the interest of society; (d) design the **MacroeconomicDAO** system as an instrument of direct public governance of economic processes; (e) present the complete architecture of GyberExperiment — the infrastructure that realises the theoretical constructs described herein through blockchain technologies, distributed computing, cryptographic protocols, and open-source software.

The theory of CyberSocium describes the regularities of the **cybersocialisation of the economy** — the historical process of transition of the global socio-economic mechanism toward distributed peer-to-peer forms controlled directly by society. This process is as objective and irreversible as industrialisation, and is equally in need of new theory, new infrastructure, and new institutional design.

**Keywords:** CyberSocium, CyberSocial Corporation, decentralised autonomous organisation, cryptoeconomics, Socio-Economic Selection, distributed governance, open-source economics, MacroeconomicDAO, peer-to-peer economy, Principle of Minimum Individual Participation

---

## 1. Introduction: Statement of the Problem

### 1.1. The Era of Super-Communications: Context and Scale

More than five billion people on planet Earth carry in their pockets computing devices whose aggregate power exceeds by orders of magnitude all the computational technology of the twentieth century. According to the International Telecommunication Union (ITU), by 2025 the number of mobile internet users had surpassed 5.4 billion — more than 67% of the world's population [1]. The aggregate volume of globally generated data reached 120 zettabytes per year (IDC Global DataSphere, 2023), with more than 80% of these data generated directly by users — people, not machines.

This is not the era of supercomputers — it is the **era of super-communications**. Every computing device is, in the first instance, an extension of the human being — a communication instrument that expands, accelerates, and transforms relations among members of society. Manuel Castells, in his foundational work *The Information Age: Economy, Society and Culture* (2000), defined this process as the formation of the "network society" — a social structure in which key economic, political, and cultural functions are organised around networks of information processing [2]. Two decades later, his thesis has not merely been confirmed — it proved to be conservative.

Information technologies have integrated into the very fabric of social relations. They dissolve geographical, cultural, and now financial-economic boundaries. The process that anthropologists call "time-space compression" (Harvey, 1989 [3]) has reached a qualitatively new level: today a developer in Nairobi and a researcher in Helsinki can work in real time on the same software product, with their collaboration coordinated automatically through distributed version-control systems, requiring not a single bureaucratic intermediary. Yochai Benkler, in *The Wealth of Networks* (2006), described this phenomenon as **commons-based peer production** — a model of value creation that fits neither the market nor the hierarchical paradigm [4].

The scale of this transformation is remarkable. According to GitHub Octoverse (2024), more than 100 million developers are registered on the largest open-source development platform; the aggregate volume of open code created by volunteers without traditional economic incentives exceeds the code produced by all the commercial corporations in the world. The volume of on-chain transactions in decentralised networks reached $15 trillion per year (Chainalysis, 2024). The number of decentralised autonomous organisations (DAOs) has surpassed 13,000, and the total assets under DAO management have reached $30 billion (DeepDAO, 2024).

Yet the institutional infrastructure governing global resources remains a product of the industrial era — centralised, opaque, oriented toward the concentration of capital and power in the hands of a shrinking circle of beneficiaries. This gap between the **technological capabilities** of society and the **institutional reality** of resource governance is the central problem that this document addresses.

The exclusive goal and exclusive opportunity of society is the most effective unification of its participants in the interests of society itself. The process of development of the human social mechanism is defined as the continuous development of relations among people — from primitive exchange to complex financial instruments, from local markets to global platforms. Each new technological leap expanded the circle of participants in economic interaction and increased the complexity of the forms of coordination among them. Today we stand on the threshold of the next leap — the transition from platform capitalism [5], in which intermediaries extract rent from coordination, to **Cybersocial Economics**, in which coordination is performed by protocols belonging to the participants themselves.

### 1.2. The Crisis of Existing Socio-Economic Institutions

If the technological dimension of the problem — the era of super-communications — represents a story of expanding possibilities, the institutional dimension is a story of mounting crisis. The gap between the technological potential of society and the institutional capacity to realise that potential manifests across several dimensions.

**Concentration of economic power.** According to Oxfam (2024), 1% of the world's wealthiest people own more than 40% of global wealth, and the 26 wealthiest individuals own as much as the poorest 50% of the world's population (3.8 billion people). Thomas Piketty, in his foundational work *Capital in the Twenty-First Century* (2013), demonstrated that inequality is not an anomaly but a structural property of the capitalist system: when the rate of return on capital exceeds the rate of economic growth (r > g), inequality inevitably intensifies [30]. Daron Acemoglu and James Robinson, in *Why Nations Fail* (2012), showed that extractive institutions — those that concentrate power and wealth in the hands of a narrow elite — are the primary cause of economic underdevelopment [31].

CyberSocium offers not a redistribution of existing wealth (a political solution) but the creation of institutional infrastructure in which concentration is impossible by design: Hybrid Voting Power limits the influence of large token-holders, while PMIP creates a mechanism for financing projects without the requirement of large capital.

**Opacity of governance.** The global financial crisis of 2008 demonstrated that even the largest financial institutions (Lehman Brothers, AIG, Bear Stearns) could operate with critically dangerous risks invisible to regulators and market participants. Nassim Nicholas Taleb, in *The Black Swan* (2007), theorised this as the problem of "hidden risks" — the opacity of systems makes them fragile to unforeseen events [32]. Axiom A2 (transparency) is a direct response: in the CSC, all financial flows are recorded on-chain and available for verification by any participant. Opacity is physically impossible — in contrast to traditional institutions, where it is secured by corporate rights to secrecy.

**The crisis of democratic governance.** Traditional representative democracy faces a crisis of legitimacy: according to the Edelman Trust Barometer (2024), trust in governments in developed countries stands at less than 50%, and in major corporations at less than 40%. Colin Crouch introduced the term "post-democracy" (2004) to describe a situation in which formal democratic institutions persist, but real power is concentrated among economic elites [33]. Helen Langman and Mansour Moya, in *Surveillance Capitalism*, note that digital platforms do not merely extract value from user data but also shape users' preferences — which calls into question the very possibility of informed democratic choice.

The four-class DAO taxonomy of CyberSocium proposes a governance model in which: (a) decisions are taken directly by the interested participants, not their representatives; (b) competence is accounted for through differentiated DAO classes and Hybrid Voting Power; (c) transparency is ensured by on-chain voting; (d) AI tools compensate for the cognitive overload of direct democracy (Axiom A8).

**Data appropriation as a form of economic extraction.** Shoshana Zuboff, in *The Age of Surveillance Capitalism* (2019), showed that the business model of the largest digital platforms is based on the appropriation of "behavioral surplus" — data generated by users beyond what is necessary for the provision of the service [10]. These data are monetised through advertising, predictive models, and behavioural manipulation. The user is not a customer (they do not pay for the service) but a *product* (their data are sold to advertisers).

Axiom A3 (data sovereignty) eliminates this model at the architectural level: in DSP, data are stored on users' devices and encrypted; no server in the ecosystem has access to raw user data (section 6.1). This is not a political solution (regulation such as GDPR) but a *technical* one — the data-extraction model is physically impossible within the architecture of CyberSocium.

**Inefficiency of resource allocation.** According to ILO (International Labour Organization) estimates, approximately 60% of the world's working-age population works in the informal economy, lacking access to institutional financing, social protection, or participation in economic governance. Venture capital, which is the primary mechanism for financing innovation, is geographically concentrated (>50% of global VC investments in Silicon Valley) and thematically biased (preference for software projects over hardware or social innovations). This means that a vast portion of human potential and creative ideas systematically fails to receive the resources for realisation — not because they are unwanted by society, but because they do not fit the selection criteria of centralised financing institutions.

PMIP addresses this problem through a mechanism of decentralised resource allocation: a project receives financing not from VC or a grant committee, but directly from the interested community. The sole criterion is social relevance SR(p): if a sufficient number of people are interested in the outcome of a project (|SIC(p)| ≥ threshold), the project receives resources. This eliminates both the geographical and the thematic bias of traditional financing.

### 1.2a. Structure of the Document

This document is organised as follows.

**Section 1 (Introduction)** formulates the problem: the gap between the technological capabilities of society and the outdated institutions of resource governance. We examine the problem of data ownership, the historical tendency toward decentralisation, and the evolution of economic governance systems.

**Section 2 (Literature Review)** maps the intellectual traditions at whose intersection the theory of CyberSocium stands: the political economy of the digital age, institutional economics and the theory of common-pool resources, mechanism theory and mechanism design, cybernetics and the theory of complex adaptive systems, and cryptoeconomics. We identify the limits of each of these traditions and establish the necessity of a new synthesis.

**Section 3 (The Theory of CyberSocium)** presents the original theoretical contribution: the axiomatic foundation (A1–A8), the formal definition of the CyberSocial Corporation (CSC), the formalisation of the evolution of governance forms, the Principle of Minimum Individual Participation (PMIP), the mechanism of Socio-Economic Selection (SES), the IPI model, and the formal model of CyberSocium as a complex adaptive system.

**Section 4 (Architecture of GyberExperiment)** translates theory into technical specification: the tokenomics of GBR, the architecture of DSP, GyberNet, GyberComputer, MacroeconomicDAO, the legal wrapper DUNA, and the AI department (AiC).

**Section 5 (DAO Taxonomy)** details the four-class model of decentralised decision-making: Social DAO, Code DAO, Commerce DAO, and Economic DAO.

**Section 6 (The Applied Ecosystem)** describes the specific implementation components: DSP, GyberNet, GyberComputer, G-Plan, and auxiliary projects.

**Section 7 (Comparative Analysis)** compares GyberExperiment with existing theoretical frameworks and practical projects, identifying its unique contribution.

**Section 8 (Discussion)** analyses the theoretical and practical implications, limitations, threat model, and open research questions.

**Section 9 (Roadmap)** presents the four-phase implementation plan: from foundation to maturity.

**Section 10 (Conclusion)** summarises the findings and articulates a vision of the future.

**Sections 11–12** contain the bibliography and appendices (glossary, smart contract addresses, summary of axioms).

### 1.3. Information Security, Ethics, and Data Ownership

Even those of us who have no direct connection to computer science and electronics are personally involved in the process of generating enormous quantities of data — all manner of valuable information that is readily exploited for all manner of manipulation and is equally readily monetised.

The scale of this process is striking. Each smartphone user generates on average 1.5 GB of data daily [16]. Cumulatively, this forms a flow of data that traditional economic theory is unable to describe adequately: data are neither a classical commodity (they are non-rival and reproducible at zero marginal cost) nor a public good (they are exclusive — controlled by those who own the servers).

The majority of large IT companies treat user accounts as corporate property, and the enormous quantities of data continuously generated by users as a fully-fledged product of the company. From a technical standpoint, this is correct: the accounts created by users are formed and stored on the companies' own servers. Approximately 99% of the internet content known to the general public is stored on closed proprietary servers that are physically secured no less well — and more likely considerably better — than the most heavily guarded military facilities, and all of this is, of course, maintained at the expense of the network's users themselves.

The fundamental technical concept of the internet is simple: it is a collection of computers connected by a network. In reality, the internet consists of people sending one another physical signals through a computer network, with the computer transforming those signals into information of the required type for us. Without people the internet would not be alive; it is obviously inconceivable without society, because it is the living result of society's activity at a real moment in time, and cannot exist without it.

From an ethical standpoint, data created by users constitute at the very least their personal intellectual property and personal data, and the appropriation of these data by obsolete economic institutions by virtue of temporary technical circumstances is a glaring fact that signals the lag of the humanistic development of modern society behind the development of its technology. When one adds the fact that 99% of the internet runs on open (free) software products and fundamentally on open Linux servers, the absurdity of the capture of internet space by obsolete economic institutions becomes evident.

The trading of user data is, in our time, an already established, commonplace, and entirely legal phenomenon. Companies develop lengthy user agreements that almost nobody still reads, and in which they obtain from users various kinds of consent — such as consent to the processing of personal data — which gives them the legal right to appropriate the data of millions of users worldwide, to analyse them, and to trade in them. Meanwhile, the multimillion-strong community of actual users remains excluded from the management and control of their own data, which in the long term leads to the erosion of the fundamental principles of the freedom of personal information, the security and control of personal data, and the freedom of society, and in every way retards and suppresses the process of the historical socio-economic development of society.

The GDPR (General Data Protection Regulation), adopted by the European Union in 2018, was the first serious attempt at a legal solution to this problem [17]. However, GDPR remains a palliative solution: it regulates the use of data but does not eliminate the structural cause of the problem — the centralised storage and processing of data. As Aaron Swartz noted in the "Guerrilla Open Access Manifesto": the problem lies not in the policies of specific companies, but in the architecture of systems that makes the concentration of data technically inevitable and economically advantageous [18].

It is evident that, from an ethical perspective, the user is the sole owner of all rights to the content they generate, including metadata and all other data types, to say nothing of the inviolability of personal correspondence, interests expressed online, and so forth. Of course, many technical products that use user data provide users with convenient, useful, and interesting features, but it is clear that all such features must be enabled and disabled at will, that the mechanism of their operation must be transparent, must ensure the security and control of user data, and that this must be confirmed by the open source code of the product.

Equally critically important is individual flexibility and the modifiability of services. The user must have full control over the portion of the overall system that they personally use, and the possibilities for modifications introduced by the user must be unlimited within their personal portion of the system. Individual possibilities for extending the system must also be unlimited — the system must be extensible in all directions.

### 1.4. Decentralisation and Open Source as a Historical Tendency

Computer science began its development as a closed field of technology, accessible only to corporations and governments. Computers were expensive, cumbersome machines available primarily to large technical institutions and corporations. We may roughly define this as the initial stage. It is followed by a stage of accelerated development of information technologies — which may be defined as the period in which the computer becomes accessible to a larger number of developers. At this time, competition in software development expanded and two principal competing currents emerged in the world of software development: proprietary and open.

Richard Stallman founded the free software movement in 1983, formulating four fundamental freedoms: the freedom to use a program, to study and modify it, to distribute copies of it, and to distribute modified versions [19]. Linus Torvalds, in 1991, created the Linux kernel, demonstrating that a distributed community of volunteers is capable of producing a software product that surpasses in reliability and scalability the products of the largest corporations. Eric Raymond, in his essay "The Cathedral and the Bazaar" (1999), theorised this phenomenon, contrasting two models of development: the "cathedral" (centralised) and the "bazaar" (distributed) [20].

As a result of surmounting these abstract stages of development, we observe an unprecedented surge of activity in OPEN SOURCE development, an improvement in the quality and usability of open-source software products, an expansion of their scope of application, and a significant growth in the number of users. OPEN SOURCE increasingly contributes to the emergence of revolutionary technologies. We can observe an entire evolutionary cycle of global socio-economic relations, directly connected with and nourished by the global OPEN SOURCE community and its ideas, whose general vector is the open and comprehensive development of software and of society as a whole.

Parallel to the evolution of open-source software, the decentralisation of information technologies developed — a process that evolved harmoniously in the depths of the internet community, periodically announcing itself through the emergence of powerful decentralised technological mechanisms resolving particular problems of society:

- **BitTorrent** (2001) — decentralised data distribution, demonstrating that a peer-to-peer protocol can be more efficient than centralised infrastructure [21]
- **Bitcoin** (2009) — a decentralised electronic monetary system, solving the double-spending problem without a trusted intermediary [22]
- **Ethereum** (2015) — a programmable blockchain, transforming decentralisation from a protocol for transactions into a platform for arbitrary applications [23]
- **IPFS** (2015) — the InterPlanetary File System, proposing content addressing in place of location addressing [24]
- **FileCoin** (2020) — an economic layer on top of IPFS, incentivising data storage [25]

Combating such decentralised technologies is practically futile and at best results in a temporary impediment to the operation of services, which subsequently leads to their modernisation and stabilisation — that is, to their development. This empirical fact has deep theoretical significance: it demonstrates that decentralisation is not a design decision but a **historical attractor** — a direction toward which the system tends regardless of attempts at external opposition.

Another of the principal contemporary problems looming over society is the growth of centralised control over the dissemination of ideas that are relevant to society. Obsolete economic institutions, by virtue of their hopeless but still sufficiently powerful position, strive rapidly to refine their own mechanisms for the control and monitoring of society, in order to identify relevant, advanced, socially significant ideas and projects and to counter their activity before they spread through society. Together with the fact that the overwhelming majority of large resource-intensive projects are initiated and financed by those same obsolete socio-economic institutions, we have, for the most part, a world that reflects exclusively the interests of these obsolete institutions and is extremely reactionary toward any kind of revolutionary, fundamental change, and consequently — toward the development of society as a whole.

At the same time, and in part as a result of this, we observe the vigorous development of decentralised technologies, which has already taken shape as a powerful, global, distributed socio-economic movement that has already united fairly broad and educated masses of people. In essence, we are witnessing the process of formation of a new type of global creative intelligentsia, capable of uniting its ideas and interests with other social forces into a single ideology wholly directed toward the common good and development — to become the basis for the final formation of a new, advanced creative class, one capable at last of making the transition to the long-overdue global socio-economic changes adequate to the contemporary demands of global Civilisation.

This emerging class differs fundamentally from the traditional intelligentsia in the historical sense — one enclosed within academic or cultural institutions and dependent on the patronage of the state or capital. What is at issue here is a distributed global network of researchers, developers, engineers, economists, and thinkers, united not by geography, corporate affiliation, or national identity, but by three structural characteristics: first, a shared technological literacy that enables them not only to understand but also to create complex information systems; second, a commitment to the principles of openness, decentralisation, and transparency as fundamental ethical and practical orientations; third — and this is critically important — possession of practical tools for the direct realisation of their ideas without the mediation of obsolete institutions, ranging from cryptographic protocols and smart contracts to distributed computing networks, open-source governance systems, and mechanisms of decentralised financing.

Richard Florida described the emergence of the "creative class" as an economic force defining the development of post-industrial societies [47], yet his analysis remained within the framework of existing institutions — corporations, cities, universities. Florida's creative class creates value within the system and depends upon it. What we observe now is qualitatively different: a class is being formed that does not integrate itself into existing institutions but generates its own — decentralised autonomous organisations, open protocols, distributed networks of storage and computation, CyberSocial Corporations. For the first time in history, a significant mass of people simultaneously possesses the intellectual potential to design alternative forms of socio-economic organisation and the technical capacity to realise them — without permission, without mediation, and without control on the part of obsolete structures.

Today, a new global social consciousness is being formed — the principal organ of humanity's social self-governance. Although the era of "kings", with all its characteristic rigidly centralised mechanisms of control that restrained the education and development of society, science, and technology, has long since passed, and today we observe a vigorous flourishing of culture, education, science, and technology in the historical sense, and their penetration — as a consequence of the historically successive social transformations that have already been accomplished — into virtually all strata of society, the process of decentralisation of global mechanisms of economic interaction is still far from its final stage. However, the direction of this process is already determined — not by the will of individual leaders or organisations, but by the very logic of technological and social development, which cannot be stopped, but can only be temporarily impeded, which, as the history of decentralised technologies shows, invariably leads to their strengthening and perfection.

There is an evident tendency of the global socio-economic mechanism in its historical development to arrive at the most distributed peer-to-peer form, governed through cybersocial financial mechanisms acting upon the global socio-economic resource — that is, a mechanism in the hands of the public and controlled directly by the public.

As information technologies develop and as they penetrate the real sector of the financial-economic space, society acquires an ever-growing number of levers of substantial influence over the direction of global economic development. Technologically, the global economy stands on the threshold of large-scale transformations associated with society's transition to a new level of socio-economic interaction, with the direct use of innovative decentralised cybersocial economic mechanisms.

With the emergence of Blockchain technology, the possibility of constructing decentralised financial systems has opened up, and their development genuinely contributes to the emergence of opportunities for the construction of global self-governing cybersocial economic structures, expanding the possibilities of society's intellectual influence on the global economic process.

Whatever happens in the world, and whatever powerful forces influence the course of history, the organism of society invariably produces a cycle of evolutionary transformation — adaptive change. It becomes more perfect, and this process occurs, above all, not on the platforms of debates, financial-economic congresses, and other hypocritical venues, but in the minds of people — initially in isolated cases, then, gaining momentum, it strengthens within society and develops in a spiral. And, of course, all this development is accompanied and ratified by the appearance of revolutionary, fundamental, philosophical, legal, and technical documents and products, which are various moments of the single dialectic of the development of global socio-economic relations, systematising and advancing that development to the degree necessary for the transition to a new stage of development.

This document is an attempt at such a systematisation.

### 1.5. The Evolution of Economic Governance Systems

The history of economic governance represents a sequence of expanding circles of participation. Each stage of transformation is characterised by three parameters: **who** makes economic decisions, **what volume of resources** is coordinated, and **what mechanism** of coordination is employed.

**Stage 1 — Local subsistence economy (before the 18th century).**
Economic decisions are made by the head of a household or community. Coordination is limited to physical space: the market fair, the guild, the manor estate. Mechanism: tradition, personal relations, direct coercion. Circle of coordination participants: tens to hundreds.

**Stage 2 — National market economy (18th–19th centuries).**
Adam Smith described the market as a coordination mechanism in which the "invisible hand" replaces centralised planning [6]. Stock exchanges, joint-stock companies, and central banks emerge. The circle of participants expands to thousands and tens of thousands. Coordination mechanism: the price signal. However, Marx showed that this mechanism systematically generates inequality and the concentration of capital [7].

**Stage 3 — Global corporate economy (20th century).**
Transnational corporations coordinate resources on a scale exceeding the budgets of many states. Ronald Coase explained the existence of the firm through the minimisation of transaction costs [8]: intra-corporate coordination is cheaper than market coordination under certain conditions. Oliver Williamson developed this theory, demonstrating the role of opportunism and asset specificity [9]. Circle of decision-makers: thousands of managers, governing the activity of millions. Mechanism: hierarchy + market.

**Stage 4 — Platform economy (early 21st century).**
Digital platforms (Google, Amazon, Facebook, Apple, Microsoft) achieve scales of coordination comparable to those of states. Nick Srnicek, in *Platform Capitalism* (2017), showed that platforms extract rent from the role of intermediary without creating proportionate value [5]. Shoshana Zuboff, in *The Age of Surveillance Capitalism* (2019), described the extraction of "behavioral surplus" as a new form of exploitation [10]. Circle of participants: billions of users, but decisions are made by boards of directors. Mechanism: algorithmic coordination controlled by a corporate hierarchy.

**Stage 5 — Decentralised cybersocial economy (emergent).**
The CSC as the fundamental economic unit eliminates the gap between the scale of participation and the scale of governance. For the first time in history, millions of participants can directly coordinate economic resources without hierarchical intermediaries — through formalised protocols executed by smart contracts. Mechanism: algorithmic coordination controlled by participants through DAOs. The PMIP principle ensures the scale of financing, while SES ensures the quality of project selection.

This sequence is not coincidental. It reflects a fundamental law: as the technological capabilities of coordination grow, **the circle of participants in economic decisions steadily expands**, and **the role of hierarchical intermediaries contracts**. Each stage is characterised by a contradiction between expanded coordination capabilities and outdated governance institutions. The resolution of this contradiction requires a new institutional design — and it is precisely this that the theory of CyberSocium proposes.

```
Stage            │ Decision participants │ Resource scale  │ Coordination mechanism
─────────────────┼──────────────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────────────
1. Local         │ 10¹–10²              │ Local           │ Tradition, personal ties
2. National      │ 10³–10⁴              │ National        │ Price signal (market)
3. Corporate     │ 10³ (managers)       │ Global          │ Hierarchy + market
4. Platform      │ 10¹ (directors)      │ Global          │ Algorithm (corporate)
5. Cybersocial   │ 10⁶–10⁹             │ Global          │ Protocol (DAO + PMIP)
```

The formalisation of this evolutionary sequence and the demonstration of the inevitability of the fifth stage constitute one of the central tasks of section 3.

### 1.6. Methodology

This work employs a combination of several methodological approaches, determined by the interdisciplinary nature of the subject under investigation.

**The axiomatic method.** The theoretical foundation of CyberSocium is built on a system of eight axioms (A1–A8), formulated as normative design principles of the system. The axiomatic approach is not chosen arbitrarily: it ensures the logical coherence of the architecture and allows formal verification of the correspondence between technical decisions and theoretical principles. Each axiom is formulated, justified through references to existing theoretical traditions, and linked to specific technical implementation mechanisms.

**Formal modelling.** The key mechanisms (PMIP, SES, IPI, FRP) are formalised as mathematical models with clearly defined parameters, conditions of operability, and demonstrable properties. For the PMIP model, a number of theorems are proved (universal realisability, scale inversion, anti-fragility). CyberSocium is modelled as a complex adaptive system (CAS) in the terminology of Holland [11] and Kauffman [12], with an explicit definition of agents, resources, interactions, feedback loops, and emergent properties.

**Systems analysis.** The interactions among the components of the GyberExperiment ecosystem are analysed using methods of system dynamics (Forrester [13], Meadows [14]). Positive and negative feedback loops, leverage points, and potential regimes of instability are identified. This approach allows the prediction of system behaviour under various initial conditions and external perturbations.

**Comparative institutional analysis.** The architecture of GyberExperiment is compared with existing projects (MakerDAO, Aragon, Colony, Gitcoin, Nouns DAO) and theoretical frameworks (mechanism design, Ostrom's theory, cryptoeconomics). This analysis makes it possible to identify the unique contribution and to reveal borrowings and parallels.

**The constructive approach.** In contrast to purely descriptive studies, the present work not only analyses existing systems but also proposes a specific architecture for a new system. Each theoretical construct receives a technical implementation: axioms are implemented in smart contracts, mechanisms in protocols, models in algorithms. This approach follows the tradition of institutional design (*institutional design*) established by Hurwicz [15] and developed in mechanism design.

**Epistemological position.** We acknowledge that the theory of CyberSocium is at an early stage of development. Many of its propositions are hypotheses requiring empirical verification. GyberExperiment is regarded precisely as an experiment — a controlled intervention on a real system with the purpose of testing theoretical predictions. In this sense our approach is closer to the methodology of Lakatos [42] (a research programme with a "hard core" of axioms and a "protective belt" of empirically testable consequences) than to Popperian falsificationism.

### 1.7. Scope and Boundaries of This Work

To prevent misplaced expectations and to ensure the correct interpretation of results, it is necessary to explicitly define the boundaries of this document — what it contains and what it does not contain.

**What this work is:**

1. *A theoretical foundation.* The document defines an axiomatic system (A1–A8), formal mechanisms (PMIP, SES, IPI, FRP), and mathematical models (CAS, Hybrid VP, replicator dynamics) that constitute the theoretical foundation of a new class of socio-economic systems — CyberSocial Corporations (CSC).

2. *An architectural specification.* For each theoretical construct, a specific technical implementation is defined: smart contracts (UnitManager, Governor, GBRVotingWrapper, LPBurnInitiator), protocols (DSP, GPROD, DDP), infrastructure (GyberNet, GyberComputer), AI components (AiC), and legal wrapper (DUNA). The specification defines *what* is to be implemented and *why*, leaving the specific implementation details to technical documentation.

3. *An experimental protocol.* GyberExperiment is formalised as a controlled socio-economic experiment with explicit hypotheses (H1–H5), observable variables, counterfactuals, and falsification criteria (section 8.6).

4. *A positioning document.* Through the literature review (section 2) and comparative analysis (section 7), the document positions CyberSocium relative to existing theoretical traditions (institutional economics, mechanism design, cryptoeconomics, CAS theory) and practical projects (MakerDAO, Aragon, Colony, Gitcoin, Nouns DAO).

**What this work is NOT:**

1. *Not a token white paper.* Although the document describes the tokenomics of GBR (section 4.2), its goal is not the attraction of investment but the formalisation of economic mechanisms. The document contains no financial projections, promises of returns, or investment recommendations. GBR is treated as a utility token — an instrument of coordination within the ecosystem, not an object of investment (see the Howey analysis, section 8.4.2).

2. *Not technical documentation.* The document describes the architecture at the level of abstraction necessary for understanding the connection between theory and implementation. Detailed API specifications, deployment guides, Solidity NatSpec, and UI mockups are contained in the technical documentation: `docs/system-architecture/` (specifications 00–06), `integration-docs/` (integration specifications), and inline documentation in the CONTRACTS, GPROD, and DSP Client repositories.

3. *Not a legal opinion.* The analysis of the legal form of DUNA (section 4.7), the Howey test (section 8.4.2), and the compliance analysis (section 8.4) are informational in nature and do not substitute for qualified legal counsel. Participants in the ecosystem are obliged independently to assess the legal consequences of their actions in the jurisdiction of their residence.

4. *Not a completed theory.* CyberSocium is at the stage of formulation — analogously to the early works of Nakamoto (2008) on Bitcoin or Buterin (2014) on Ethereum. Many propositions are hypotheses subject to empirical verification. The document consciously adopts this position: an honest recording of the current state of knowledge is more valuable than premature claims to completeness.

**Target audience:**

- *Researchers* — economists, computer scientists, legal scholars, sociologists — interested in the formal analysis of decentralised socio-economic systems;
- *Developers* — blockchain engineers, full-stack developers, AI/ML engineers — implementing components of the ecosystem;
- *Participants in GyberExperiment* — all levels of MemberStatus — for an understanding of the theoretical foundations of the system in which they operate;
- *Regulators and legal professionals* — for the assessment of the legal architecture and compliance model;
- *Critics and opponents* — for constructive critique; the existence of formalised hypotheses and falsification criteria makes criticism productive.

**Versioning.** This document follows the principle of a living document: it will be updated as empirical data from GyberExperiment are received. Each version is recorded in Git with a complete history of changes. Current version: 2.0 (March 2026). Next planned version: 2.1 (following the completion of Phase I and the receipt of initial empirical data, estimated Q4 2026).

---
## 2. Literature Review and Interdisciplinary Context

The theory of CyberSocium does not arise in an intellectual vacuum. It stands at the intersection of several powerful intellectual traditions, each of which provides a necessary but insufficient apparatus for describing the phenomena we observe. The purpose of this section is to map these traditions, to demonstrate their contributions, to identify their limits, and to substantiate the need for a new synthesis.

### 2.1. The Political Economy of the Digital Age

Classical political economy, from Adam Smith to Karl Marx, analyzed the transformation of economic systems through the lens of productive relations, forms of property, and the distribution of surplus product. Marx, in particular, demonstrated that each mode of production creates a corresponding superstructure of institutions, and that the contradiction between the development of productive forces and outdated productive relations is the driving force of historical transformations [26].

The transition to the digital economy generates a new form of this contradiction, which may be called the **informational paradox of property**: the means of production of the digital age (data, algorithms, network effects) are created collectively by billions of users, but appropriated by a narrow circle of corporations controlling the infrastructure. This creates a gap between the social character of production and the private form of appropriation — a gap structurally analogous to the one Marx described for industrial capitalism, but realized at a new technological level.

Thomas Piketty in *Capital in the Twenty-First Century* (2014) demonstrated empirically what Marx had predicted theoretically: without institutional corrections, capital concentrates, since the rate of return on capital (r) exceeds the rate of economic growth (g), i.e., r > g [8]. In the digital economy, this tendency is amplified manifold: data, unlike physical capital, possesses properties of zero marginal reproduction cost and increasing returns to scale. A company that has accumulated a dataset of a billion interactions trains a model that attracts even more users, who generate even more data — a self-reinforcing cycle that radically accelerates concentration. The result: by 2024, the five largest technology companies (GAFAM) controlled a combined market capitalization exceeding $10 trillion — more than the GDP of most states.

Joseph Stiglitz, the 2001 Nobel laureate, in a series of works [9] demonstrated that information asymmetry — a situation in which one party to a transaction possesses substantially more information than the other — is not an anomaly but a structural characteristic of modern markets. In the digital economy, this asymmetry reaches an unprecedented scale: platforms know almost everything about users' behavior (geolocation, search queries, social graph, purchasing patterns), whereas users know neither what data are being collected about them nor how those data are used. CyberSocium addresses this asymmetry through Axiom A2 (transparency): all algorithms governing the ecosystem are public (AGPL-3.0), all transactions are verifiable on-chain, and all DAO decisions are documented in an immutable registry.

Contemporary authors develop the line of analysis of classical political economy as applied to the digital economy. Paul Mason in *PostCapitalism: A Guide to Our Future* (2015) argues that information technologies undermine the basic mechanisms of the market economy: when the marginal cost of reproduction tends toward zero, the price mechanism ceases to be an effective means of coordination [27]. Jeremy Rifkin in *The Zero Marginal Cost Society* (2014) develops the same thesis, predicting the emergence of a "collaborative commons" — a sharing economy in which the logic of free software (the freedom to study and modify a program) is extended to the entire stack of socio-economic interaction. Modular architecture, open code, and standardized interfaces make this technically achievable.

Shoshana Zuboff in the monumental work *The Age of Surveillance Capitalism* (2019) introduced the concept of **Surveillance Capitalism** — an economic system in which human experience is systematically transformed into data for predicting and modifying behavior [10]. Zuboff showed that the problem is not reducible to privacy: the issue is a new form of power, in which corporations accumulate "behavioral surplus" (*behavioral surplus*) — data generated by users beyond what is necessary to improve the product — and use it to create "behavioral futures markets" (*behavioral futures markets*). This model is fundamentally incompatible with Axiom A3 (data sovereignty) and Axiom A2 (transparency). CyberSocium proposes an architectural alternative to Surveillance Capitalism: data never leaves the user's device (federated learning), algorithms are open to audit (AGPL), and the economic model is based on creating value for participants, not on extracting value from their behavior.

Nick Srnicek in *Platform Capitalism* (2017) proposed a systematic typology of digital platforms [5]: advertising (Google, Facebook), cloud (AWS, Azure), industrial (Siemens MindSphere), product (Spotify), and lean (Uber, Airbnb). Each type extracts rent from control over an intermediary node — a point through which data and transactions must necessarily pass. Srnicek showed that platform capitalism is not "post-industrial" — it is a continuation of the logic of capitalist accumulation under new technological conditions. CyberSocium proposes a sixth platform type — a **coordination platform** (*coordination platform*), which does not extract rent from its intermediary role, but instead provides coordination infrastructure governed by participants through DAO and financed through PMIP. The difference is fundamental: a traditional platform maximizes engagement (user time = data = revenue); a coordination platform maximizes coordination (collaborative value creation = shared outcome).

Yanis Varoufakis, former Finance Minister of Greece and economist, in *Technofeudalism* (2023) went further, arguing that the platform economy represents not capitalism but **Technofeudalism** — a system in which platform owners occupy a position analogous to medieval lords, while users are digital serfs obliged to labor on the "digital manor" of the platform [99]. Corporate profits, according to Varoufakis, are generated not by the market (competition, pricing) but by rent — payment for access to infrastructure monopolized by the platform. This perspective radicalizes the problem and reinforces the argumentation of CyberSocium: if the problem lies not in the specific practices of specific companies, but in the structure of the information space, then the solution must be structural — a change in the architecture of coordination, not regulation of behavior within the existing architecture.

Yet none of these critical works offers a coherent institutional alternative. Mason describes the destruction of the old but not the architecture of the new. Rifkin predicts a "collaborative commons" but does not formalize the mechanisms of coordination. Zuboff diagnoses the disease but prescribes treatment within the existing paradigm (regulation, antitrust law). Varoufakis acknowledges Technofeudalism, but his alternative remains at the level of a political program. Benkler [4] theorizes peer-to-peer production but confines the analysis to informational goods with zero marginal costs. The theory of CyberSocium seeks to fill this gap — to propose a formalized architecture for a new socio-economic interaction, grounded in the principles of decentralization and self-governance, with mathematically justified coordination mechanisms (PMIP, SES), a formal axiomatic foundation (A1–A8), technical implementation (smart contracts, DAO), and a legal shell (DUNA).

### 2.2. Theory of the Commons and Collective Action

The central problem of any decentralized system is coordination: how can a multitude of autonomous agents collectively manage common resources without a central authority? This problem determines the fate of any cybersocial economics project.

Garrett Hardin in "The Tragedy of the Commons" (1968) formulated the classical argument against communal governance: rational agents, pursuing individual gain, inevitably exhaust the common resource [28]. Mancur Olson in *The Logic of Collective Action* (1965) reinforced this thesis by showing that the free-rider problem (*free-rider problem*) makes collective action impossible without coercion or selective incentives [29].

Elinor Ostrom refuted both of these pessimistic conclusions. In *Governing the Commons* (1990) she demonstrated through empirical material drawn from thousands of self-governing communities — from the irrigation systems of Spain to the fishing cooperatives of Japan — that communities can effectively manage common resources when eight design principles are satisfied [30]:

1. Clearly defined boundaries of the group and the resource
2. Rules corresponding to local conditions
3. Participation of users in rule-making
4. Monitoring by the users themselves
5. Graduated sanctions for violations
6. Accessible and inexpensive conflict-resolution mechanisms
7. Recognition of the right to self-organize by external authorities
8. Nested enterprises (*nested enterprises*) for large-scale resources

These principles, formulated for physical communities of hundreds of participants, acquire fundamental significance for the design of digital decentralized systems at the scale of millions. Each of Ostrom's principles finds technical realization in the architecture of GyberExperiment:

- Clear boundaries → cryptographic identity, access tokens
- Local rules → parameters of specific DAOs, adjustable by vote
- Participation in rule-making → Governor contracts, on-chain governance
- Monitoring → blockchain transparency, G-Plan, AI audit
- Graduated sanctions → reputation slashing, status reduction in MemberStatus
- Conflict resolution → FRP (Fork Resolution Protocol)
- Right to self-organize → DUNA (Wyoming), legal autonomy
- Nested enterprises → four-class DAO taxonomy (Social, Code, Commerce, Economic)

The critical distinction between CyberSocium and Ostrom's models lies in scale and enforcement mechanism. Ostrom studied systems in which compliance with rules is ensured by social pressure and face-to-face mutual monitoring. In CSC this is impossible: participants are anonymous or pseudonymous, distributed around the world, and not connected by personal relationships. Smart contracts solve this problem: rules are enforced automatically, transparently, and incorruptibly. Monitoring is provided not by social pressure but by cryptographic on-chain verification.

Formally, the relationship between Ostrom's principles and CSC's mechanisms can be represented as a scaling function:

```
Scaling of Ostrom's Principles (Scale Transition Function):

  Ostrom (local community):
    Enforcement: social pressure + personal relationships
    Monitoring: mutual observation (face-to-face)
    Trust: interpersonal, accumulated across generations
    Scale: 10²–10³ participants
    Adaptation: slow (consensus through deliberation)

  CSC (global system):
    Enforcement: smart contracts (automatic execution)
    Monitoring: on-chain verification + AI audit
    Trust: cryptographic (trustless by design)
    Scale: 10⁶–10⁹ participants
    Adaptation: rapid (DAO voting + cadCAD simulations)

  Key transformation:
    Trust(personal) → Trust(cryptographic)
    Monitoring(social) → Monitoring(algorithmic)
    Enforcement(community) → Enforcement(smart_contract)
    Adaptation(deliberative) → Adaptation(computational)
```

Douglass North, the 1993 Nobel laureate, extended the analysis of institutions beyond communal resources. In *Institutions, Institutional Change and Economic Performance* (1990) he defined institutions as "the rules of the game in a society, or, more formally, the humanly devised constraints that shape human interaction" [90]. North drew a fundamental distinction between *formal* institutions (laws, contracts, constitutions) and *informal* ones (norms, traditions, codes of conduct). He showed that economic growth is determined not by resources or technologies, but by the institutional environment — the set of rules that reduce uncertainty and transaction costs.

For CyberSocium, North's contribution has a threefold significance. First, CSC is an *institutional innovation* in North's sense — a new set of rules that reduces the transaction costs of coordination. Second, smart contracts represent a unique type of institution: they are simultaneously formal (code is law), automatically enforceable (requiring no third party for enforcement), and modifiable (through DAO governance). Third, North emphasized the role of "path dependence": institutions are difficult to change because existing institutions shape the interests and beliefs of agents, who then resist change. CSC addresses this problem through FRP (Fork Resolution Protocol): instead of modifying an existing institution (which provokes resistance), the system allows a fork — the parallel existence of alternative institutional designs with subsequent evaluation through SES.

Oliver Williamson, a student of Coase and the 2009 Nobel laureate, developed transaction cost theory, showing that the choice between market and hierarchy is determined by three factors: asset specificity, transaction frequency, and uncertainty [9]. Under conditions of high asset specificity and uncertainty, hierarchy (the firm) is more efficient than the market, since it reduces the risk of opportunism. CyberSocium proposes a third path, transcending Williamson's dichotomy: smart contracts reduce the risk of opportunism without hierarchy — code executes automatically, conditions are visible to all (A2), and violation is technically impossible, not merely legally prohibited. This means that CSC can coordinate activity with high asset specificity (software development, research, complex projects) at low transaction costs, which Williamson considered possible only within hierarchical organization.

```
Williamson's Dichotomy and Its Extension by CyberSocium:

  Williamson (1985):
    Market:    low specificity → efficient
    Hierarchy: high specificity → necessary (due to opportunism)

  CSC Extension:
    Market:      low specificity → still efficient
    CSC:         high specificity → feasible without hierarchy
                 (smart contracts eliminate opportunism)
    Hierarchy:   redundant in the presence of trustless enforcement

  Transaction costs:
    TC(market)    = search + bargaining + enforcement
    TC(hierarchy) = monitoring + bonding + residual loss
    TC(CSC)       = gas_fee + governance_overhead
                    (all three components compressed technically)
```

Acemoglu and Robinson in *Why Nations Fail* (2012) proposed a binary classification of institutions: *inclusive* (providing broad participation, protection of property rights, rule of law) and *extractive* (concentrating power and resources in the hands of a narrow elite) [91]. They showed that long-term prosperity is ensured only by inclusive institutions, whereas extractive ones lead to stagnation and collapse. CyberSocium can be interpreted as an attempt to create a *maximally inclusive* digital institution: access is open to anyone (A6, inclusivity), governance is decentralized (A1), rules are transparent (A2), and data rights are protected (A3). Extractiveness is systematically constrained: the hybrid VP formula reduces the influence of large holders, Activity Score incentivizes genuine participation, and status degradation excludes passive rent extraction.

### 2.3. Mechanism Design

If Ostrom showed *that* self-governance is possible, Mechanism Design answers the question of *how* to design rules that lead to a desired outcome. This discipline is of fundamental importance for CyberSocium: CSC is not a description of a naturally evolved system, but a *designed* system — its rules, incentives, and constraints are created purposefully.

Leonid Hurwicz, the founder of Mechanism Design, posed a fundamental question: can a social planner (*social planner*) design rules of the game such that rational agents, pursuing their own interests, achieve a socially optimal outcome? [15]. For the answer to this question, Hurwicz, Maskin, and Myerson received the Nobel Prize in Economics in 2007.

Hurwicz formalized the concept of **Incentive Compatibility**: a mechanism is incentive-compatible if it is not in the interest of rational agents to deviate from the "honest" strategy. Formally: for each agent i with private information θᵢ, the optimal strategy in mechanism M is *truthful reporting* — honest disclosure of θᵢ. This fundamental property is the design objective of all GyberExperiment mechanisms:

```
Incentive Compatibility in CSC:

  UnitManager: honest work → rewards ≥ free-riding → penalties
    Formally: utility(contribute) > utility(free-ride)
    when Activity Scoring ≥ θ_min

  Commerce DAO: honest project evaluation → better investments
    Formally: SR(p) = 1 ↔ project is genuinely valuable for SIC

  Code DAO: quality code → reputation ↑ → VP_code ↑
    Formally: utility(quality_PR) > utility(low_effort_PR)

  SES: projects with real value survive
    Formally: fitness(p) correlates with social_value(p)
```

Roger Myerson extended Hurwicz's theory by proving the **revelation principle** (*revelation principle*): for any mechanism implementing a given social choice, there exists a direct incentive-compatible mechanism implementing the same choice [100]. This means that in designing CSC it suffices to consider "honest" mechanisms — if they are incentive-compatible, they are equivalent to any more complex mechanism.

Eric Maskin complemented the picture with the concept of **Maskin monotonicity** — a necessary condition for Nash-implementability of a mechanism [101]. A social choice function is implementable in Nash equilibrium if and only if it satisfies the monotonicity condition: if alternative a is chosen under preferences θ, it must also be chosen under any preferences θ' under which a has not "fallen" in any agent's ranking. This technical condition has an intuitive interpretation for CSC: if project p is approved by the community (SES, Commerce DAO), it must also be approved under any increase in its support — which is ensured by the monotonicity of voting in Governor contracts.

Key results of Mechanism Design for the design of CSC:

**Gibbard–Satterthwaite Theorem:** there is no strategy-proof (*strategy-proof*) voting mechanism, other than dictatorship, for choosing among three or more alternatives [31]. This fundamental constraint means that any DAO voting system is susceptible to strategic manipulation. GyberExperiment addresses this constraint through a combination of mechanisms: quadratic voting (reduces the weight of large holders), reputation multipliers (account for activity, not just capital), and multi-class DAO structure (different mechanisms for different types of decisions). Formally: instead of a single mechanism M susceptible to the GS theorem, CSC uses a portfolio of mechanisms {M_social, M_code, M_commerce, M_economic}, each optimized for its class of decisions.

**VCG Mechanism (Vickrey–Clarke–Groves):** a mechanism ensuring *truthful revelation* — participants have an incentive to honestly disclose their preferences [32]. The VCG mechanism is mathematically elegant but has practical limitations: it is not budget-balanced (may require external subsidies) and is vulnerable to collusion. Vitalik Buterin, Zoë Hitzig, and Glen Weyl in "Liberal Radicalism" (2018) adapted the VCG principle for financing public goods, creating the mechanism of **quadratic funding** (*quadratic funding*, QF) [33]:

```
Quadratic Funding (Buterin, Hitzig, Weyl, 2019):

  For project p funded by N donors with contributions c₁, ..., cₙ:

    F(p) = (Σᵢ √cᵢ)²

  Matching fund = F(p) - Σᵢ cᵢ

  Properties:
    — Samuelson-optimal for public goods
    — Broad support (many small contributions) is worth more
      than deep support (one large contribution)
    — One donor: F = c (no bonus)
    — N donors contributing c/N each: F = c × N (linear amplification!)

  Analogy with PMIP:
    QF amplifies broad support through the matching fund
    PMIP amplifies broad support through |SIC(p)|
    Both mechanisms invert the logic of concentrated
    capital: the more participants, the more efficient
```

GyberExperiment uses a modified variant of the quadratic mechanism in Commerce DAO and Economic DAO: the square root is applied not to financial contributions but to GBR staking for the calculation of voting power, combining the QF principle with a reputation multiplier.

**Principal-Agent Problem.** The classical problem of misaligned interests between owners and managers (Jensen & Meckling, 1976) is resolved in CSC through blockchain transparency and the automatic enforcement of rules. However, a new form of this problem arises — **digital agency risk**: between the community (principal) and AI agents (agent) acting on behalf of the community in the class of tasks designated "AI decides" (Axiom A8). Unlike a human agent, an AI agent has no interests in the classical sense, but may: (a) hallucinate (generate false output), (b) be subject to adversarial attack, (c) systematically amplify biases in the training data. The division principle of A8 addresses precisely this limitation, defining for each class of tasks the permissible level of AI autonomy.

**Arrow's Impossibility Theorem (1951).** Arrow's theorem [102] shows that there exists no social choice function simultaneously satisfying five "reasonable" conditions: unrestricted domain, Pareto efficiency, independence of irrelevant alternatives, non-dictatorship, and transitivity. This fundamental constraint applies to all systems of collective decision-making, including DAO. GyberExperiment does not "circumvent" Arrow's theorem (this is mathematically impossible), but minimizes its practical consequences: the four-class DAO structure narrows the space of alternatives for each class of decisions, reducing the probability of aggregation paradoxes. AI summarization and structured deliberation prior to voting encourage the formation of single-peaked preferences, for which simple majority rule is strategy-proof (Black's theorem).

Glen Weyl and Eric Posner in *Radical Markets* (2018) went further, proposing radical market mechanisms — COST (Common Ownership Self-assessed Tax) and quadratic voting — as tools for solving fundamental problems of property and governance [34]. Quadratic Voting (*Quadratic Voting*, QV) allows participants to express the intensity of preferences, not only their direction: each additional vote on a single issue costs quadratically more (1 vote = 1 credit, 2 votes = 4 credits, 3 votes = 9 credits). This naturally equalizes the influence of those to whom an issue is irrelevant and those for whom it is critical. GyberExperiment does not use COST directly, but integrates the QV principle through the Hybrid Voting Power formula: the square root of GBR staking is functionally analogous to QV — it reduces the marginal "cost" of additional influence for large holders, making the distribution of power more equitable.

**Auction Theory and Resource Allocation.** Paul Milgrom and Robert Wilson (Nobel Prize 2020) developed auction theory, demonstrating how to design market mechanisms for efficient resource allocation under information asymmetry [103]. Their results are applicable to the allocation of computational resources of GyberComputer (auction of GPU time for projects), the allocation of grants from the DUNA treasury (optimal auction for project financing), and price formation in the liquidity pools of project tokens.

### 2.4. Cybernetics and the Theory of Complex Adaptive Systems

CyberSocium as a system cannot be adequately described in the equilibrium terms characteristic of neoclassical economics. It is a **Complex Adaptive System** (CAS) — a term introduced by John Holland [11] and developed at the Santa Fe Institute. This section examines the intellectual traditions that form the analytical apparatus for describing and designing CyberSocium: cybernetics, complexity theory, and system dynamics.

#### 2.4.1. First- and Second-Wave Cybernetics

The foundational ideas for our work trace back to first-wave cybernetics. Norbert Wiener in *Cybernetics* (1948) formulated the feedback principle as a universal mechanism of regulation [35]. Wiener showed that negative feedback — a mechanism by which a system responds to deviation from a goal by correcting its behavior — is a fundamental principle of both biological and engineering systems. In CSC this principle is explicitly realized:

```
Feedback Loops in the CSC Architecture:

  Negative feedback (stabilizing):
    — Activity Score < θ_min → reduction in rewards →
      incentive to increase activity
    — Fund exhaustion → Halving rewards →
      slowing of expenditure → sustainability
    — Governance capture → qualified majority →
      blocking → stability

  Positive feedback (amplifying):
    — More participants → lower individual_cost (PMIP) →
      more projects → more participants
    — Better AI models → more productive ecosystem →
      more data for AI → better models
    — Higher reputation → more influence → more contribution →
      higher reputation

  Balance: stabilizing loops prevent
  uncontrolled growth or collapse;
  amplifying loops ensure exponential
  growth under favorable conditions.
```

W. Ross Ashby in *An Introduction to Cybernetics* (1956) introduced the **Law of Requisite Variety**: for effective control of a system, the controlling mechanism must possess variety no less than that of the system being controlled [36]. Formally: R(controller) ≥ R(system), where R is a measure of variety (the number of distinguishable states). This law has a direct implication for the design of CSC:

```
Ashby's Law and the Architecture of Governance:

  Global economic system:
    R(system) ≈ 10^(10⁹)  (billions of agents ×
    thousands of states each)

  Centralized control (corporation, state):
    R(controller) ≈ 10^(10³)  (thousands of managers/officials)
    R(controller) << R(system) → insufficient!
    Result: systemic failures, bureaucratic
    inefficiency, crises

  Distributed control (CSC):
    R(controller) = R(system)  (each agent is part
    of the controlling mechanism)
    R(controller) = R(system) → Ashby's law satisfied!
    Result: adequate governance at any scale

  Conclusion: only distributed control, in which
  each agent possesses local information and autonomous
  decision-making capacity, satisfies Ashby's law for
  global economic systems.
```

Second-wave cybernetics (Heinz von Foerster, Humberto Maturana, Francisco Varela) extended classical cybernetics by introducing the concepts of **autopoiesis** — the self-reproduction of living systems — and **second-order observation** — the study of how an observer observes. Maturana and Varela (1980) showed that autopoietic systems define their own boundaries and operations — they do not merely react to the environment, but actively create their own reality [104]. CSC possesses autopoietic properties: it defines its own boundaries (NFT membership, DUNA), rules (DAO voting), and criteria of value (social relevance through SES). MacroeconomicDAO is a second-order observer: it not only governs the system, but governs the rules of governance — a meta-mechanism by definition.

Niklas Luhmann, a sociologist who applied autopoiesis to social systems, showed that society consists of *communications*, not of people [105]. Communications generate further communications, forming self-referential systems (law, economy, science, politics). CSC can be regarded as a new subsystem of society — a **cybersocial economic system**, self-referentially closed upon its own communications (DAO proposals, votes, smart contracts), but structurally coupled with other subsystems (the traditional economy through DUNA, the legal system through Wyoming legislation, science through academic publications).

#### 2.4.2. Theory of Complex Adaptive Systems

Stuart Kauffman in his works on complexity theory [12] showed that in complex systems order emerges "for free" (*order for free*) at the **edge of chaos** — in the zone between rigid determinism and complete chaos. Kauffman modeled networks of random Boolean functions (NK models) and found that at certain values of connectivity (K) the system spontaneously self-organizes, forming stable patterns — **attractors**. This result explains why CSC does not require central planning: with properly designed rules of interaction (axioms, protocols, smart contracts), global order emerges emergently from local interactions of agents.

The parameter K in Kauffman's NK model has a direct analogy in CSC. K is the number of connections each element has with other elements:

- At K = 0 (no connections): the system freezes in one state. Analogy: isolated economic agents without coordination — no collective action.
- At K >> 1 (many connections): the system is chaotic, sensitive to small perturbations. Analogy: platform capitalism, where hyperconnectivity (global markets, instantaneous transactions) creates fragility and cascading crises (2008, 2020).
- At K ≈ 2–3 (optimal connectivity): the "edge of chaos" — the system is stable but capable of adaptation. Analogy: CSC, where the four-class DAO taxonomy limits connectivity (each class interacts with a limited number of others), ensuring a balance between stability and adaptability.

John Holland (1995) formalized the concept of CAS as a system consisting of multiple adaptive agents interacting according to local rules, from which global properties emerge that are not derivable from the properties of individual agents [11]. Key characteristics of CAS relevant to CSC: (a) **aggregation** — agents combine into groups with emergent properties (SIC, AG); (b) **nonlinearity** — small changes may have large consequences (a single successful project can attract thousands of new participants); (c) **flow** — resources circulate through the system along network paths (GBR through UnitManager → staking → DAO → projects); (d) **diversity** — the system supports multiple strategies and agent types (6 MemberStatus levels, 4 DAO classes).

Nassim Taleb in *Antifragile* (2012) introduced the concept of **antifragility** — the property of a system that does not merely withstand stress (robustness) but is strengthened by it [35]. Taleb classifies systems according to their response to volatility: fragile (break), robust (endure), antifragile (strengthen). CSC is designed as an antifragile system: economic shocks stimulate an influx of participants seeking an alternative (increase in |NFC|); technical vulnerabilities, once discovered, reinforce security (bug bounty, post-mortem); competition among projects through SES ensures the survival of the most viable. The formal justification for the antifragility of PMIP is provided in Theorem 3 of Section 3.4.

#### 2.4.3. System Dynamics

Donella Meadows in *Thinking in Systems* (2008) systematized the apparatus of system dynamics and introduced the concept of **leverage points** (*leverage points*) — places in a system where a small intervention can produce a large effect [14]. She identified a hierarchy of 12 leverage points — from the least effective (parameter changes: size of flows, buffers) to the most effective (paradigm change: the mental model from which the system arises). CyberSocium acts at the level of the highest leverage points:

```
Meadows' Leverage Point Hierarchy and the Action of CyberSocium:

  12 (weak). Numbers: size of buffers, flows
    CSC: tokenomics parameters (reward rate, halving threshold)

  9-10. Information flows, rules
    CSC: DAO governance, four-class taxonomy

  6-8. Structure: self-organization, rules of rules
    CSC: MacroeconomicDAO (meta-governance),
    FRP (conflict resolution protocol)

  4-5. System goals
    CSC: social relevance instead of profit;
    PMIP: "millions make billions"

  1-3 (strong). Paradigm
    CSC: from hierarchical corporation to CSC;
    from capital → value to participation → value

  CyberSocium acts simultaneously on ALL levels,
  which makes it not a reform of the system, but a phase transition.
```

Jay Forrester, the founder of system dynamics, in a series of works (Industrial Dynamics 1961, Urban Dynamics 1969, World Dynamics 1971) demonstrated the counterintuitive behavior of complex systems: interventions aimed at improving a situation often worsen it due to unforeseen feedback loops and delays [13]. This result explains why CyberSocium uses cadCAD simulations before adopting economic decisions in Economic DAO: intuitive decisions in complex systems are systematically mistaken, and only formal modeling allows the consequences to be assessed. Each proposal in Economic DAO is accompanied by a cadCAD simulation across ≥1000 scenarios — this is the institutional implementation of Forrester's caveat.

Friedrich Hayek, although he did not employ the terminology of complexity theory, anticipated many of its ideas. His concept of "spontaneous order" (*spontaneous order*) and the argument about the impossibility of centralized planning due to the problem of **dispersed knowledge** (*dispersed knowledge*) [37] are formally close to Ashby's law and the theory of emergence. Hayek showed that the market's price mechanism is an information system that transmits the dispersed knowledge of millions of agents in the form of price signals. However, Hayek confined his solution to the market mechanism. CyberSocium extends his intuition: the market is only one of the possible mechanisms of spontaneous order; smart contracts, DAO, and PMIP represent other, potentially more effective, mechanisms for coordinating dispersed knowledge.

The key extension of Hayek consists in the following: the price signal conveys information about *preferences* and *scarcity*, but not about *intentions*, *competencies*, or *ethical assessments*. CSC's smart contracts transmit substantially richer information: Activity Score reflects the participant's real contribution (not only capital, but also activity); DAO voting aggregates assessments of social relevance (not only supply/demand); the SES mechanism integrates the evolutionary history of projects (not only the current price). In Hayek's terminology: CSC solves the *extended* problem of dispersed knowledge — it coordinates not only economic, but also social, technical, and ethical information.

### 2.5. Cryptoeconomics: The Limits of Existing Models and the Unfilled Niche

Cryptoeconomics — a term introduced by Vitalik Buterin to describe the use of cryptography and economic incentives for designing decentralized systems — represents the most direct precursor to the theory of CyberSocium. However, there is a qualitative gap between existing cryptoeconomics and our theory, which we systematize through an analysis of the genealogy of cryptoeconomic thought, a taxonomy of existing DAOs, and the identification of four fundamental problems.

#### 2.5.1. Genealogy of Cryptoeconomic Thought

Cryptoeconomics as an intellectual tradition has passed through four phases, each of which has made a necessary but insufficient contribution to the understanding of decentralized coordination.

**Phase 1: Protocol Phase (Nakamoto, 2008).** Satoshi Nakamoto in "Bitcoin: A Peer-to-Peer Electronic Cash System" (2008) solved the double-spending problem without a trusted intermediary, creating the first viable mechanism of decentralized consensus [22]. Nakamoto's key innovation was not cryptography as such (which had been known for decades), but the *economic mechanism* making an attack unprofitable: the cost of a 51% attack exceeds the potential gain. This is the first explicit application of Mechanism Design to distributed systems, although Nakamoto himself did not use this terminology. Bitcoin proved that trustless coordination is possible, but confined it to a single application — the transfer of value.

**Phase 2: Platform Phase (Buterin, 2014).** Buterin in the Ethereum Yellow Paper (2014) extended Nakamoto's paradigm by creating a programmable blockchain — a platform for arbitrary decentralized applications [23]. This transformed the blockchain from a protocol for transactions into an environment for creating arbitrary institutions. Ethereum opened the possibility for DAO, DeFi, NFT, and other forms of on-chain coordination. However, Buterin himself acknowledged (2014) that "cryptoeconomics is not economics that uses cryptography, but the use of economic incentives to secure cryptographic protocols." This definition focuses on *protocol security*, not on *institutional design*.

**Phase 3: Formal Phase (Zamfir, 2015–2019).** Vlad Zamfir proposed a formalization of cryptoeconomics as "cryptoeconomic theory" — a discipline studying *protocols as mechanisms*, participants as *rational agents*, and security as an *equilibrium property*. Zamfir introduced the concept of "cryptoeconomic security" — a system is secure if no rational agent has an incentive to deviate from the protocol. This formalization was an important step, but it inherited the limitation of game theory: the assumption of complete rationality and fixed preferences of agents.
