In real social systems, preferences are endogenous — they are shaped by the system itself.

**Phase 4: Engineering Phase (Zargham/cadCAD, 2018–present).** Michael Zargham and his BlockScience team proposed the approach of "token engineering" — an engineering discipline for designing token systems, based on control theory and computational models. The key tool is cadCAD (complex adaptive dynamics Computer Aided Design), a framework for modeling and simulating cryptoeconomic systems [45]. Zargham's approach is substantially closer to CyberSocium, as it treats token systems as complex adaptive systems rather than static games. CyberSocium uses cadCAD as a simulation tool (Section 4.8), but extends Zargham's approach in three directions: (1) adds axiomatic grounding instead of a purely engineering approach; (2) integrates a legal shell (DUNA); (3) includes AI as a component of the system, not merely as a tool of analysis.

```
Genealogy of Cryptoeconomic Thought:

  Nakamoto (2008)    →  Trustless consensus  →  Single function (value transfer)
       ↓
  Buterin (2014)     →  Programmable trust   →  Arbitrary applications, but ad hoc design
       ↓
  Zamfir (2015-19)   →  Formal security      →  Rigorous theory, but homo economicus
       ↓
  Zargham (2018-now) →  Token engineering    →  CAS modeling, but no axiomatics
       ↓
  CyberSocium (2025) →  Cybersocial econ.    →  Axioms + CAS + AI + Legal shell

  Each phase expands scope:
    protocol → platform → theory → engineering → SOCIAL SYSTEM
```

#### 2.5.2. DAO Taxonomy: Six Types and Their Limitations

To position CyberSocium, it is necessary to systematize the existing DAO landscape. By 2026, according to DeepDAO data, more than 33,000 DAOs are registered with a combined treasury of approximately $24.5 billion. We propose the following taxonomy by functional purpose:

```
DAO Taxonomy (6 types):

  Type 1: Protocol DAO
    Examples: MakerDAO, Uniswap, Aave, Compound
    Function: management of DeFi protocol parameters
    Governance model: token-weighted voting
    Limitation: plutocracy; whale dominance
    Treasury size: $1M — $10B

  Type 2: Investment DAO
    Examples: The LAO, MetaCartel Ventures, BitDAO
    Function: collective investment
    Governance model: proportional voting by contribution
    Limitation: speculative focus; absence of social mission

  Type 3: Grant DAO
    Examples: Gitcoin, Moloch DAO, Gnosis DAO
    Function: grant distribution
    Governance model: QV/QF + committee review
    Limitation: dependence on external funding; no sustainable model

  Type 4: Service DAO
    Examples: Raid Guild, DXdao, LexDAO
    Function: collective service provision
    Governance model: reputation + multisig
    Limitation: small scale; manual coordination

  Type 5: Social DAO
    Examples: Friends With Benefits, Seed Club, Bankless DAO
    Function: social association and content
    Governance model: token-gated membership
    Limitation: no economic model; club character

  Type 6: Network State DAO
    Examples: Balaji's Network State, Praxis
    Function: construction of parallel social structures
    Governance model: founding team + charter
    Limitation: utopianism; absence of formal theory

  CSC (CyberSocium): DOES NOT FIT WITHIN THE EXISTING TAXONOMY
    Function: integrated economic unit
    Model: 4-class DAO taxonomy + MacroeconomicDAO
    Uniqueness: axiomatics + SES + PMIP + DUNA + AiC
```

None of the six types of DAO simultaneously implements all three functions: production, governance, and value distribution. Protocol DAOs govern but do not produce. Service DAOs produce but do not govern resources. Investment DAOs distribute but do not produce or govern. CyberSocium is the first attempt to create an integrated model in which all three functions are realized within a unified architecture.

#### 2.5.3. Four Fundamental Problems of Cryptoeconomics

Despite the impressive growth of the crypto ecosystem, it suffers from four fundamental problems that are not resolved by existing approaches and that CyberSocium seeks to overcome.

**Problem 1: Plutocratic Governance.** Most existing DAOs use token-weighted voting (1 token = 1 vote), which transforms decentralized governance into a plutocracy — the rule of capital [38]. This is a direct contradiction of the spirit of decentralization. Empirical data confirm the severity of the problem: in MakerDAO, the 5 largest addresses control more than 50% of voting power; in Uniswap, approximately 10 addresses can pass any proposal; in Compound Labs, one address (a16z) controlled approximately 6.5% of all votes at the launch of governance. Vitalik Buterin in a series of articles (2018–2024) proposed several mechanisms to counter plutocracy: quadratic voting (QV), proof-of-personhood, and "soulbound tokens" (SBTs). However, each of these mechanisms has its own limitations: QV is vulnerable to Sybil attacks; proof-of-personhood violates privacy; SBTs create a new form of lock-in.

CyberSocium proposes a comprehensive solution through a **hybrid voting system**:

```
Hybrid Voting Power (CyberSocium):

  VP(i) = √(GBR_staked(i)) × ActivityMultiplier(i) × StatusBonus(i)

  where:
    √(GBR_staked)      — the square root reduces the influence of large holders
                          (a whale with 1M GBR has VP=1000, not 1M)
    ActivityMultiplier  — a multiplier of genuine participation (0.5 — 1.5)
                          measured on-chain through Activity Score
    StatusBonus         — a bonus for proven competence
                          Unit=1.0, Dev=1.2, LeadDev=1.5, ArchDev=2.0, Core=3.0

  Properties:
    1. Anti-plutocracy: VP grows sub-quadratically with tokens
    2. Anti-Sybil: ActivityMultiplier requires real activity
    3. Meritocracy: StatusBonus reflects demonstrated competence
    4. Inclusivity: even a Unit with minimal stake has a voice
```

This formula is not an arbitrary construction but a consequence of Axiom A5 (meritocratic justice) and Axiom A6 (inclusivity): VP must reflect a genuine contribution (A5) and ensure participation by all (A6), while excluding capital dominance (A1, decentralization).

**Problem 2: Speculative Dominance.** The vast majority of tokens are speculative assets rather than instruments of coordination. According to CoinGecko data (2025), of approximately 15,000 active crypto assets fewer than 5% have genuine utilitarian application; 95% of value is determined by speculative expectations. This creates a "liquidity curse": the more liquid the token, the more the speculative component overshadows the utilitarian one. As a result, DAO participants optimize for token value rather than product or service quality — a classic example of goal displacement (Goodhart: "when a measure becomes a target, it ceases to be a good measure").

GyberExperiment counters this with the utilitarian GBR tokenomics featuring triple protection: (1) the LP Burn mechanism ties the value of GBR to real activity rather than speculative expectations; (2) staking linked to MemberStatus creates "useful lock-up": tokens are locked not for APY but for participation in governance; (3) the Activity Score multiplier penalizes passive holders and rewards active participants.

**Problem 3: Fragmentation.** Existing DAOs are isolated organizations, each solving a local task. There is no standard inter-DAO communication protocol, no common governance ontology, and no mechanism for resolving conflicts between DAOs. This reproduces at the blockchain level the very fragmentation that decentralization was supposed to overcome. Attempts at resolution (DAOhaus, Aragon Network, Zodiac from Gnosis Guild) offer *infrastructure* for DAOs, but not a *theory* of their interaction.

CyberSocium proposes a systemic approach: the four-class DAO taxonomy (Social, Code, Commerce, Economic) ensures specialization; MacroeconomicDAO as meta-governance ensures coordination between classes; interaction protocols (IPI-lifecycle) ensure a standard interface; CSC as an integrated economic unit ensures unity of purpose. This is not a toolset, but an *architecture* — a formal system with provable coordination properties.

**Problem 4: Absence of a Theoretical Foundation.** Most crypto projects are built on ad hoc design without formal theoretical justification. White papers describe "what" (tokenomics, mechanisms) but not "why" (theoretical foundations, provable properties). Zamfir (2019) noted: "We have no theory of cryptoeconomic security; we are building systems worth billions of dollars on the basis of intuition and hope." Zargham (2020) added: "Token engineering without formal theory is alchemy, not chemistry."

CyberSocium aspires to be the first theory offering: axiomatic justification (8 axioms with logical structure), formal models with provable properties (PMIP — proven O(1/n) convergence; SES — proven EES stability; FRP — proven termination in a finite number of steps), and an explicit connection between theory and implementation (each axiom → specific mechanisms → specific smart contracts).

#### 2.5.4. From Cryptoeconomics to Cybersocial Economics

Let us systematize the differences between existing cryptoeconomics and the proposed theory of CyberSocium:

```
Cryptoeconomics vs. Cybersocial Economics:

  Parameter             | Cryptoeconomics         | CyberSocium
  ──────────────────────|─────────────────────────|──────────────────────────
  Object                | Protocol                | Social system
  Agent                 | Homo economicus         | Heterogeneous participant
  Goal                  | Protocol security       | Social relevance
  Incentives            | Economic                | Econ. + social + reput.
  Governance            | Token-weighted          | Hybrid VP (quadr. + merit.)
  Financing             | ICO/IDO (speculative)   | PMIP (utilitarian)
  Project selection     | Market / committee      | SES (evolutionary)
  Legal status          | Legal vacuum            | DUNA (Wyoming)
  AI integration        | Absent / ad hoc         | AiC (3 circuits, A8)
  Theoretical basis     | Ad hoc / informal       | Axiomatic system
  Scale                 | Protocol / single DAO   | Ecosystem (CSC = ⟨N,R,P,G,M,E⟩)
  Conflicts             | Hard fork / exit        | FRP (formal protocol)
  Evolution             | Governance proposals    | SES + cadCAD simulations
```

The key distinction lies in the *level of abstraction*: cryptoeconomics designs *protocols*, CyberSocium designs *social systems*. A protocol is a fixed set of rules; a social system is a living organism capable of self-modification, learning, and adaptation. This distinction is analogous to the distinction between programming (writing specific code) and computer science (theory of computation): cryptoeconomics is the programming of decentralized systems, CyberSocium is the science of them.

Thus, the theory of CyberSocium stands at the intersection of five intellectual traditions: the political economy of the digital age (the problem), the theory of the commons (the possibility of self-governance), Mechanism Design (design tools), the theory of complex systems (analytical apparatus), and cryptoeconomics (technical foundation). Each of these traditions provides a necessary but insufficient apparatus. Their synthesis is the subject of the next section.

#### 2.5.5. Failure Analysis: What DAO Failures Teach

Theory without empirics is blind. Before proceeding to our own theoretical constructs, it is necessary to analyze historical DAO failures — cases in which decentralized systems failed to accomplish their stated objectives. Each failure reveals a specific problem for which CyberSocium proposes a specific solution.

**Failure 1: TheDAO (Ethereum, 2016).** The first major DAO project attracted $150M through a token sale and lost $60M due to a reentrancy vulnerability in the smart contract. Root cause: absence of formal verification and code audit. Effect: an emergency hard fork of Ethereum (ETH/ETC split), fundamental undermining of trust in DAO as a concept. Lesson for CyberSocium: *smart contract security is not optional — it is a prerequisite for the existence of the system*. Architectural response: multilevel audit pipeline (Slither + Mythril + Echidna + manual audit), UUPS Proxy (ability to correct vulnerabilities), Timelock (48 hours for detecting anomalies before execution), ReentrancyGuard (OpenZeppelin).

**Failure 2: MakerDAO — "Black Thursday" (March 2020).** A 50% collapse of ETH within 24 hours triggered cascading liquidations in MakerDAO. Oracles could not update prices fast enough; liquidation auctions proceeded at zero bids (0 DAI for ETH); $8.3M in losses for vault users. Root cause: dependence on external oracles and inadequacy of parameters for extreme conditions. Lesson for CyberSocium: *economic parameters must be calibrated for black swan events*. Architectural response: cadCAD modeling of stress scenarios (Section 9.5, KPI); Fund Monitoring with automatic Halving at critical thresholds; diversification of oracles (Chainlink as primary, Band Protocol as fallback); Treasury two-level architecture (on-chain + DUNA fiat reserve).

**Failure 3: Beanstalk — governance attack (April 2022).** An attacker took a flash loan of $1B, obtaining 79% of voting power for approximately 13 seconds, voted for a proposal to transfer all protocol funds ($182M) to their address, and immediately repaid the flash loan. Root cause: token-weighted voting without a lock-up period; immediate execution without a timelock. Lesson for CyberSocium: *flash loan governance attack is a real, not a theoretical, threat*. Architectural response: GBRVotingWrapper requires staking for ≥7 days before obtaining VP (flash loans are impossible); Timelock 48 hours between adoption of a decision and execution; qualified majority (67%) for financial decisions; Emergency Pause (Core + ArchDev multisig).

**Failure 4: ConstitutionDAO (2021).** Raised $47M through crowdfunding to purchase a copy of the United States Constitution at a Sotheby's auction. Lost the auction. The attempt to return funds cost participants $1.5M in gas fees (Ethereum mainnet). The project had no mechanism for continuing activity after failure. Root cause: absence of an IPI cycle (the project was designed for a single outcome); gas inefficiency (Ethereum L1). Lesson for CyberSocium: *a project in CSC must have a lifecycle including failure scenarios*. Architectural response: the IPI cycle (Idea → Discussion → Formation → Implementation → Operation → Legacy) formalizes all stages, including *legacy* — the planned completion or transformation of a project; BSC instead of Ethereum L1 (gas < $0.01); PMIP financing instead of a one-off crowdfunding round.

**Failure 5: Nouns DAO — treasury rage quit (September 2023).** A group of large NounNFT holders initiated a "rage quit" — mass burning of NFTs to receive a proportional share of the treasury ($27M out of $53M). Cause: disillusionment with governance and inefficient use of funds. Root cause: absence of four-class differentiation of decisions (all decisions passed through a single DAO); absence of an accountability mechanism for project initiators; absence of Activity Score (passive holders had equal rights with active ones). Lesson for CyberSocium: *a monolithic DAO does not scale*; participants lose motivation when governance becomes noisy and inefficient. Architectural response: four-class DAO taxonomy (Social, Code, Commerce, Economic) reduces cognitive load by approximately 67.5% (Theorem 5, Section 5.6.2); Activity Score + MemberStatus degradation excludes "passive rent extraction"; LP Burn ensures proof-of-commitment by project initiators.

```
Summary Table of DAO Failures and CyberSocium Architectural Responses:

  Failure             | Root cause                 | CyberSocium response
  ────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────
  TheDAO (2016)       | Contract vulnerability     | Audit pipeline + UUPS +
                      |                            | Timelock + ReentrancyGuard
  ────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────
  MakerDAO Black      | Inadequate parameters      | cadCAD stress-tests +
  Thursday (2020)     | for black swan             | Fund Monitoring + Halving
  ────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────
  Beanstalk (2022)    | Flash loan + instant       | VP lock-up 7d + Timelock
                      | execution                  | 48h + Emergency Pause
  ────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────
  ConstitutionDAO     | No lifecycle + gas         | IPI cycle + BSC (low gas)
  (2021)              |                            | + PMIP financing
  ────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┼──────────────────────────
  Nouns rage quit     | Monolithic DAO + passive   | 4-class DAO + Activity
  (2023)              | rent                       | Score + LP Burn
```

This analysis demonstrates that the architectural decisions of CyberSocium are not arbitrary — each is a response to a specific, empirically observed problem. Therein lies the practical value of the extensive history of DAO failures: it forms a "negative design vocabulary" (*negative design vocabulary*) — a set of anti-patterns that any serious decentralized system must avoid.

### 2.6. Synthesis: The Unfilled Niche and the Positioning of CyberSocium

The review of five intellectual traditions reveals a characteristic pattern: each tradition solves its part of the puzzle, but none offers a complete solution. Let us systematize:

| Tradition | What it explains | What is lacking | Contribution to CyberSocium |
|---|---|---|---|
| Political economy of the digital age | Why existing institutions are inadequate | Architecture of an alternative | Problem formulation (Section 1) |
| Theory of the commons (Ostrom) | That self-governance is possible | Scaling beyond local communities | Design principles (→ 8 axioms) |
| Mechanism Design | How to design rules with desirable properties | Realization in real systems with heterogeneous agents | Formal models (PMIP, SES, FRP) |
| Theory of complex adaptive systems | How emergent order arises from local interactions | Concrete architecture, not only description | Analytical apparatus (CAS model) |
| Cryptoeconomics | Technical infrastructure for trustless coordination | Overcoming plutocracy, speculation, fragmentation | Technological base (GyberNet, contracts) |

**The niche of CyberSocium** is defined by six properties whose combination is not represented in any existing theory or project:

1. **Axiomatic grounding** — unlike the ad hoc design of most DAOs, the architecture is built on a formal system of eight axioms with provable properties
2. **Four-class DAO taxonomy** — unlike monolithic DAOs, different types of decisions are processed by mechanisms optimized for them
3. **PMIP as a financing mechanism** — unlike ICO/IDO (speculative by nature), PMIP provides scalable financing through minimization of individual contribution
4. **SES as a selection mechanism** — unlike centralized curation or a pure market, evolutionary project selection combines market efficiency with collective wisdom
5. **Legal integration (DUNA)** — unlike the legal vacuum of most DAOs, a formal legal shell ensures interaction with the real world
6. **AI integration (A8, AiC)** — unlike the "AI-first" or "no-AI" approach, a principled position: AI augments but does not replace collective judgment (the division principle)

This combination defines CyberSocium as a new interdisciplinary field — **Cybersocial Economics** — standing at the intersection of economic theory, computer science, organization theory, jurisprudence, and artificial intelligence. GyberExperiment is the first concrete realization of this theory.

#### 2.6.1. Formal Positioning: The "Theory × Implementation" Matrix

For precise positioning of CyberSocium within the intellectual landscape, let us introduce a two-dimensional matrix classifying existing approaches along two axes: (1) level of theoretical formalization (from ad hoc to axiomatic) and (2) level of practical implementation (from conceptual to functioning):

```
Positioning Matrix:

                   Practical Implementation
                   Concept.  Prototype  Function.  Scale.
                   ────────  ─────────  ─────────  ────────
  Ad hoc           │ token   │ 95% DAO  │ Uniswap  │ Bitcoin
  design           │ memes   │          │ Compound │
                   │         │          │          │
  Partial          │ Radical │ Aragon   │ MakerDAO │ Ethereum
  formalization    │ Markets │ Gnosis   │ Colony   │
                   │         │          │          │
  Sectoral         │ Zamfir  │ Kleros   │ Optimism │ —
  theory           │ (2019)  │ Aragon   │ RPGF     │
                   │         │ Court    │          │
  Axiomatic        │         │          │          │
  system           │         │ CyberSocium ←───────┤ Goal
                   │         │ (GyberExperiment)    │
```

CyberSocium occupies a unique position: it is the only project claiming axiomatic formalization (bottom row) while possessing a working prototype (column "Prototype"). All existing projects with a high level of practical implementation (Bitcoin, Ethereum, Uniswap) use ad hoc or partially formalized design. All projects with a high level of theoretical formalization (Zamfir, Zargham) remain at the conceptual stage.

This is not accidental but a consequence of a methodological choice: CyberSocium is designed "top-down" (from axioms to implementation), not "bottom-up" (from working code to attempts at theoretical comprehension). Each element of the architecture is not an ad hoc solution to a problem, but a consequence of formal deduction from axioms.

#### 2.6.2. Five Bridges Between Disciplines

CyberSocium constructs five explicit bridges between previously isolated intellectual traditions:

**Bridge 1: Ostrom → blockchain (institutional economics → cryptoeconomics).** Ostrom's design principles, developed for local resource communities with tens of participants, are scaled to global systems with millions of participants through the encoding of principles in smart contracts. The key challenge is preserving the "spirit" of the principles during formalization: how to formalize "graduated sanctions" (principle 5) without losing the flexibility inherent in human judgment? CyberSocium solves this through a two-tier mechanism: automatic sanctions (Activity Score → degradation) for routine cases + FRP (arbitration + voting) for contested situations.

**Bridge 2: Hurwicz → DAO (Mechanism Design → decentralized governance).** Hurwicz's impossibility theorem (it is impossible to simultaneously achieve Incentive Compatibility, individual rationality, and efficiency) defines the space of trade-offs for any mechanism. CyberSocium does not claim to violate this theorem, but uses it to make deliberate choices: in Social DAO the priority is Incentive Compatibility (simple majority, minimal barrier); in Code DAO — efficiency (meritocratic weighting); in Commerce DAO — individual rationality (LP Burn as credible commitment); in Economic DAO — a balance of all three (Hybrid VP + cadCAD modeling).

**Bridge 3: Holland → tokenomics (CAS → Token Engineering).** Holland's theory of complex adaptive systems explains how emergent order arises from local interactions of agents following simple rules. CyberSocium explicitly models CSC as a CAS: participants are adaptive agents; the GBR token and reputation are "building blocks" (building blocks in Holland's sense); SES is the selection mechanism; PMIP is the aggregation mechanism. Each of these elements is formally mathematized, which permits the use of token engineering tools (cadCAD, ABM) to predict the emergent behavior of the system.

**Bridge 4: Maynard Smith → project governance (evolutionary game theory → IPI).** Maynard Smith's concept of the evolutionarily stable strategy (ESS) is applied to project selection: SES is formalized as an evolutionary game in which the strategy of "cooperation" (participation in the SIC of a project) dominates the strategy of "free-riding" provided that Activity Score and MemberStatus make free-riding unprofitable. Proof: a free-rider receives ActivityMultiplier < 1.0, which reduces their VP and rewards; a cooperator receives ActivityMultiplier > 1.0, which increases their VP and rewards. In the limit, the free-rider degrades to status None and loses all privileges.

**Bridge 5: DUNA → DeFi (jurisprudence → smart contracts).** Wyoming DUNA (W.S. § 17-32, 2024) — the first legal instrument designed for DAO. CyberSocium uses DUNA not merely as a "wrapper" for legal compliance, but as an integral element of the architecture: DUNA treasury provides fiat operations that are impossible on-chain; Restricted LP Act Manager ensures compliance with securities regulation; the two-tier treasury (on-chain DAO treasury + DUNA treasury) creates antifragility through diversification of regulatory regimes. This is the first formal architecture in which the legal and cryptoeconomic structures are designed as a unified system, not as a bolted-on compliance afterthought.

#### 2.6.3. CyberSocium Research Programme

On the basis of the conducted literature review, it is possible to formulate the CyberSocium research programme in Lakatosian terms [45]:

**Hard core** (non-falsifiable by agreement premises):
- Decentralized coordination is possible and efficient given proper mechanism design
- Axioms A1–A8 define the necessary and sufficient properties of a cybersocial corporation
- PMIP ensures scalable financing through the minimization of individual contribution

**Protective belt** (falsifiable hypotheses):
- H1: Hybrid Voting Power surpasses token-weighted voting by the metric of Pareto efficiency of decisions
- H2: SES ensures more efficient project selection than centralized curation (for N > 100)
- H3: the four-class DAO taxonomy ensures more efficient decision-making than a monolithic DAO structure
- H4: AI integration (A8, AiC) reduces the cognitive load on participants by ≥40% without reduction in decision quality
- H5: DUNA ensures legal resilience equivalent to traditional corporate forms

**Positive heuristic** (direction of development):
- Formalization and verification of all theoretical models through model checking
- Empirical testing of hypotheses H1–H5 against GyberExperiment data
- Extension of the theory to multi-CSC interaction (meta-SES)
- Integration of AI as a full-fledged element of the theory (not only of the implementation)

**Negative heuristic** (what is not subject to revision):
- Not a single one of the eight axioms can be discarded without a fundamental reconsideration of the theory
- No privileged node or agent violating A1 can be introduced
- No closed-source component violating A2 can be embedded

This formulation in Lakatosian terms allows CyberSocium to be distinguished from "yet another crypto project": it is not a set of technical solutions, but a scientific research programme with a clear hard core, a falsifiable protective belt, and defined development heuristics. It is precisely the presence of falsifiable hypotheses (H1–H5) that makes CyberSocium a scientific theory rather than an ideology: each of these hypotheses can be refuted by empirical data from GyberExperiment, and such refutation would require modification of the protective belt (but not the hard core) of the theory.

### 2.7. Ethics and the Philosophy of Technological Design

The design of decentralized socio-economic systems inevitably raises deep ethical and philosophical questions. CyberSocium is not a value-neutral technical project — it is a *normative architecture*, embodying specific conceptions of justice, autonomy, and the good. The present section makes these ethical foundations explicit, so as to render them subjects of rational discourse rather than implicit premises.

#### 2.7.1. The Problem of "Embedded Values" and the Principle of Values-Sensitive Design

Lawrence Lessig in "Code is Law" (1999) first articulated the thesis that software code is a form of regulation: the architecture of digital systems determines which actions are possible, which are impeded, and which are impossible — often more effectively than legal norms [100]. Winner in the classic article "Do Artifacts Have Politics?" (1980) showed that technical systems are not value-neutral: the bridge over the Long Island highway, designed by Robert Moses too low for buses to pass, effectively encoded racial segregation into infrastructure [101]. These observations are critically important for CyberSocium: smart contracts deployed on the blockchain possess *immutability* — their value choices are fixed in code that is impossible (or extremely difficult) to change after deployment.

Friedman and Kahn (1996) proposed the methodology of **Values-Sensitive Design** (VSD) — design that consciously takes into account the values of all stakeholders [102]. VSD includes three components: conceptual analysis (what values are affected?), empirical investigation (how do participants perceive the system?), and technical design (how are values realized in the architecture?). CyberSocium implements VSD through the following chain:

```
Values-Sensitive Design in the CyberSocium Architecture:

  Value                     Axiom      Technical mechanism
  ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  Autonomy                  A1, A3     Wallet = PK, Federated Learning
  Transparency              A2         Open-source (AGPL-3.0), on-chain audit trail
  Data sovereignty          A3         DDP (IPFS), client-side encryption
  Adaptability              A4         Upgradeable contracts (UUPS proxy)
  Meritocratic              A5         Hybrid VP, Activity Score,
  justice                              MemberStatus
  Inclusivity               A6         PMIP (individual_cost → 0 as N → ∞)
  Self-governance           A7         Four DAO classes, MacroeconomicDAO
  Cognitive freedom         A8         AiC (AI augmentation, not replacement)

  Principle: every value has a formal
  expression (axiom) and a concrete technical realization.
  Values are not declarative — they are algorithmically ensured.
```

Critically, the axioms of CyberSocium do not claim universality: they represent the choices of the system's designers, conscious and explicit. Other CSCs can be constructed on different axioms — for example, replacing A5 (meritocratic justice) with A5' (egalitarian justice: one person = one vote). The formalism CSC = ⟨N, R, P, G, M, E⟩ admits such variations; what is universal is not the specific system of axioms, but the *method* of axiomatic design.

#### 2.7.2. Justice in Distributive Systems: From Rawls to Algorithmic Fairness

The design of an economic system is, ultimately, a response to the question of distributive justice: how should resources, opportunities, and outcomes be distributed among participants? John Rawls in *A Theory of Justice* (1971) proposed two principles: (1) equal basic liberties for all and (2) the permissibility of inequality only to the extent that it improves the position of the least advantaged (*difference principle*) [103]. Rawls's "veil of ignorance" (*veil of ignorance*) — a thought experiment in which agents design rules without knowing what position they will occupy in the system — is a precise description of the ideal design situation for a DAO: a smart contract deployed before the composition of participants is known defines rules for all future agents.

CyberSocium implements a modified Rawlsian approach:

- **Principle 1 (equal basic liberties)** → Axiom A6 (inclusivity): any participant satisfying minimal requirements (wallet + initial stake) receives full access to the basic functions of the ecosystem. There are no geographical, ethnic, gender-based, or property-based entry barriers.

- **Principle 2 (difference principle)** → modified in favor of PMIP: inequality is permissible (MemberStatus, Activity Score, Hybrid VP), but only to the extent that it incentivizes contributions that increase the total volume of resources. Hypothesis: inequality in CSC is *productive* — it creates incentives whose results are distributed through PMIP, reducing individual_cost for all participants.

However, algorithmic fairness raises problems that Rawls did not foresee. Burrell (2016) and O'Neil (2016) in *Weapons of Math Destruction* showed that algorithmic systems can reproduce and amplify existing inequalities, even when formally neutral [106]. In the context of CyberSocium, this means that Activity Score, calculated from on-chain metrics, may systematically undervalue the contribution of participants from regions with unstable internet connectivity; Hybrid VP, accounting for stake size, may de facto discriminate against participants from low-income countries. CyberSocium addresses this problem through three mechanisms: (1) algorithm transparency (A2) allows any participant to verify the calculation formula; (2) parameter controllability through DAO voting allows correction of formulas when discrimination is detected; (3) regular auditing of AI components (AiC Circuit 1) for bias.

#### 2.7.3. Autonomy, Paternalism, and the Problem of the "Wise Contract"

Axiom A1 (decentralization) and A7 (self-governance) postulate the autonomy of participants as a fundamental value. However, full autonomy creates a contradiction: if participants are free to make any decisions through DAO voting, what prevents them from voting to destroy the system? To transfer all funds to one participant? To abolish axioms A1–A8?

This is the classical problem of the "paradox of democracy" (Popper, 1945): can a democratic system democratically vote to abolish democracy? In traditional constitutional systems this problem is resolved through "eternity clauses" (*eternity clauses*) — constitutional norms not subject to amendment (e.g., Art. 79(3) of the Basic Law of Germany). CyberSocium applies an analogous approach: MacroeconomicDAO cannot alter axioms A1–A8 without the Fork Resolution Protocol, which requires a qualified super-majority (≥80%) and grants the dissenting minority the right of exit (fork). This is the technical analogue of eternity clauses — not an absolute prohibition, but an exceptionally high threshold.

The second aspect of the problem is **paternalism**. Thaler and Sunstein in *Nudge* (2008) proposed the concept of "libertarian paternalism" — the design of a choice environment (*choice architecture*) that guides agents toward better decisions without restricting their freedom [107]. However, in the context of smart contracts, a "nudge" acquires a different character: code determines not only the choice environment, but also the *set of permissible choices*. The UnitManager smart contract physically cannot award a reward to a participant with an Activity Score below the threshold — this is not a "gentle nudge" but a rigid constraint. CyberSocium consciously employs both types of regulation:

- **Hard constraints** (in code): protection against attacks (reentrancy guard), prevention of capture (qualified majority for critical decisions), economic invariants (Fund Monitoring, Halving);
- **Soft incentives** (in parameters): Activity Score as a continuous incentive, not a binary barrier; graduated MemberStatus with upward mobility; reward multiplier 50–150% depending on activity.

Principle: hard constraints protect the system from *destruction*; soft incentives guide participants toward *cooperation*. The former are not subject to change without FRP; the latter are adjusted through DAO voting.

#### 2.7.4. Epistemological Responsibility: The Right to Error

CyberSocium, like any new socio-economic system, can err. A smart contract may contain a vulnerability. The Hybrid VP formula may prove unjust. Tokenomics parameters may lead to unforeseen cascading effects. Acknowledging this possibility is not a weakness but an epistemological virtue.

Karl Popper in *The Open Society* (1945) argued that good institutions are not those that *guarantee* correct decisions, but those that allow *errors to be corrected* without catastrophic consequences [108]. Amartya Sen in *Development as Freedom* (2000) developed this idea: institutional quality is determined not by the optimality of outcomes, but by *capability* — the ability of agents to expand their opportunities and correct unfavorable outcomes [109].

CyberSocium embeds the right to error into its architecture:

- **Timelock** (48 hours): every critical decision can be cancelled within 48 hours of adoption — a "cooling-off period" for detecting errors;
- **UUPS Proxy**: smart contracts are upgradeable — discovered vulnerabilities can be corrected without migrating the ecosystem;
- **cadCAD simulations**: each economic proposal is modeled across ≥1000 scenarios *before* adoption, reducing the probability of error;
- **Fork Resolution Protocol**: those who disagree with a decision can create a fork — "exit by voice" (Hirschman, 1970), minimizing the cost of error for the minority;
- **Gradual rollout**: new parameters are introduced incrementally (canary deployment), with metrics monitoring and the possibility of rollback.

This approach contrasts with both traditional hierarchies (where management errors propagate across the entire organization without the possibility of correction from below) and with the "code is law" dogmatism of early DAOs (The DAO hack of 2016 demonstrated that the absence of correction mechanisms leads to catastrophic losses). CyberSocium occupies an intermediate position: code defines the rules, but the rules include *rules for changing rules* — a meta-mechanism ensuring evolutionary adaptability.

In summary: the ethical position of CyberSocium can be characterized as **constructive pluralism** — the system is designed on the basis of explicit values (formulated in axioms A1–A8), but leaves space for their evolution (through FRP), correction (through DAO voting), and competition with alternative systems of values (through inter-system protocols). We do not claim to have discovered the "one correct" economic system — we propose a method for designing systems that are capable of learning from their own errors.

---
## 3. Theory of CyberSocium

### 3.1. Axiomatic Foundations

The theory of CyberSocium is built upon a system of eight axioms — normative principles that define the fundamental properties a CyberSocial Corporation must possess. The axioms serve a triple function: (1) they fix the value priorities of the system; (2) they serve as evaluation criteria for technical decisions; (3) they define the boundaries of permissible behaviour for participants and algorithms.

Each axiom is formulated, justified through references to existing theoretical traditions, and linked to specific implementation mechanisms in the GyberExperiment architecture.

**Axiom 1 (Decentralization).** No single node, agent, group of agents, or algorithm may exercise control over the system as a whole. Governance functions are distributed among all participants and implemented through formalized protocols.

*Justification:* Ashby's law of requisite variety [36]: centralized management is incapable of processing the variety of a global economic system. Empirically: the resilience of Bitcoin and Ethereum, in which the absence of a central point of failure ensures continued operation under any external perturbation.

*Implementation:* P2P architecture of GyberNet, multisig contracts with distributed control, distributed storage (IPFS/DDP), absence of a single point of failure in every component of the ecosystem.

**Axiom 2 (Transparency).** All economic transactions, governance decisions, and system parameters are recorded on the blockchain and are available for verification by any participant in real time.

*Justification:* Ostrom's principle of user-side monitoring [30]. The condition of informational symmetry required for the effective operation of voting mechanisms (Gibbard–Satterthwaite [31]).

*Implementation:* On-chain governance for all DAO decisions, AGPL-3.0 licensing of all code, public blockchain explorer, open audits of smart contracts.

**Axiom 3 (Data Sovereignty).** The user is the sole owner of all rights to the content, metadata, and any other types of data generated by them. No component of the system may use a user's data without their explicit informed consent.

*Justification:* Zuboff's ethical argument [10] on the inadmissibility of "surveillance capitalism." The legal argument of the GDPR [17]. Aaron Swartz's technical argument: the problem lies not in policy but in architecture [18].

*Implementation:* DID (Decentralized Identifiers), IPFS for user-controlled data storage, E2E encryption, zero-knowledge proofs for verification without disclosure of data.

**Axiom 4 (Extensibility).** The system is designed for unlimited expansion without the permission of a central authority. Any participant may create new modules, applications, and integrations that extend the functionality of the system.

*Justification:* The principle of permissionless innovation, realized in Ethereum and demonstrated in practice (thousands of independent applications on a single platform). Modular architecture as a property of complex adaptive systems [11].

*Implementation:* Modular DSP architecture, open APIs, SAPP (extension standard), permissionless participation in the ecosystem.

**Axiom 5 (Meritocratic Fairness).** A participant's reward is determined by their contribution to the system, verifiable through objective mechanisms, rather than by hierarchical position or the volume of capital.

*Justification:* Ostrom's principle of user-side monitoring [30]. Smart contracts and activity-tracking systems (G-Plan) render contribution verification automatable and manipulation-resistant. The VCG mechanism [32] demonstrates the possibility of designing systems in which honest behaviour is a rational strategy.

*Implementation:* UnitManager smart contract (six-level status system: None, Unit, Dev, LeadDev, ArchDev, Core), G-Plan (activity verification), reputation system with on-chain recording, Activity Scoring (score 0–100, reward multiplier 50–150%).

**Axiom 6 (Inclusiveness).** The entry barrier to the system must be minimal, and every participant must have access to the system's resources regardless of their personal involvement in specific projects.

*Justification:* PMIP (Section 3.4) demonstrates mathematically that inclusiveness does not contradict scalability — it is its precondition. Olson [29] showed that a high entry barrier leads to the collective action problem. PMIP resolves this problem: each participant contributes a minimum share, yet the aggregate effect scales without limit.

*Implementation:* GBR liquidity pools with all projects ensure every community member participates in the economic success of any project. Multilingualism, cross-chain compatibility, free basic access to DSP.

**Axiom 7 (Self-Governance).** The system is governed exclusively by its participants through formalized mechanisms of collective decision-making, without external management.

*Justification:* Ostrom's principle of the community's right to self-organization [30]. DAO contracts provide the technical implementation of self-governance. Hayek's argument [37] on the impossibility of effective centralized planning due to the problem of dispersed knowledge.

*Implementation:* Four-class DAO taxonomy (Social, Code, Commerce, Economic), Governor contracts (OpenZeppelin), Timelock, multisig, on-chain voting with a quadratic mechanism and reputational multipliers.

**Axiom 8 (Cognitive Augmentation).** The system uses artificial intelligence as a tool for augmenting the collective cognitive capacities of participants, but not as a substitute for human judgment in matters affecting values, ethics, and strategic direction.

*Justification:* Direct democracy has historically been considered impractical at scale due to two constraints: cognitive (a person cannot deeply evaluate every proposal) and coordinational (millions of people cannot deliberate simultaneously). AI removes both constraints, making large-scale direct democracy practically feasible for the first time. However, without an explicit limitation, AI may become a new form of centralization — an "AI-oligarchy" in which an algorithm, rather than the community, determines direction. Axiom A8 explicitly limits AI to the role of a cognitive augmenter.

*Principle of demarcation:*
- **AI decides autonomously:** technical optimizations (compute distribution, routing, monitoring), aggregation and structuring of information, anomaly and pattern detection, generation of recommendations and forecasts
- **AI informs, the human decides:** strategic decisions about the direction of development, ethical questions, allocation of large resources (Economic DAO), conflicts of values (FRP at the level of principles)
- **AI has no authority to:** independently move treasury funds, modify smart contract parameters without a DAO vote, block or censor participant proposals, make irreversible decisions without a human in the loop

*Implementation:* all AI agents are registered in GyberNet (soulbound tokens), their decisions are recorded on-chain and are auditable, parameters of AI agents are established by the vote of the corresponding DAO, any participant may contest an AI agent's action through the standard proposal mechanism. AiC (three contours: internal, research, commercial) is the ecosystem's infrastructural AI layer.

**Hypothesis on Consistency.** Axioms A1–A8 are consistent and mutually reinforcing. Decentralization (A1) ensures the resilience of transparency (A2); transparency (A2) ensures the verifiability of meritocracy (A5); data sovereignty (A3) and extensibility (A4) create the conditions for inclusiveness (A6); inclusiveness (A6) provides the scale necessary for self-governance (A7); self-governance (A7) protects all other axioms from violation by external forces; cognitive augmentation (A8) ensures the quality of self-governance (A7) at any scale, strengthens meritocracy (A5) through objective analysis of contributions, and respects data sovereignty (A3) through federated learning without transfer of raw data.

```
Axiom mutual-reinforcement graph:

A1 (Decentralization) ──→ A2 (Transparency) ──→ A5 (Meritocracy)
      │                                                │
      └──→ A7 (Self-Governance) ←── A6 (Inclusiveness) ←──┘
                │                        ↑
                └──→ A8 (Cognitive Augmentation)
                                         ↑
      A3 (Data Sovereignty) ──→ A4 (Extensibility)
```

It should be noted that there is a constructive tension between A5 (meritocracy: voting weight proportional to contribution) and A6 (inclusiveness: minimal barrier for all). New participants have access, but little governance weight. This tension is resolved through balancing mechanisms: quadratic voting (reduces the influence of large holders), reputational multipliers (reward activity, not staking alone), and a temporal horizon (a participant's weight grows with activity, not with investment).

### 3.2. CyberSocial Corporation: Definition and Properties

**Definition 3.2.1.** A CyberSocial Corporation (CSC) is an organizational form satisfying the following conditions:

1. **Distributed ownership:** A CSC belongs to all of its participants; no single participant or group of participants holds a controlling share
2. **Algorithmic governance:** key decisions are made through formalized protocols (DAO), executed by smart contracts
3. **Open codebase:** all software code of the CSC is available for audit and modification by any participant
4. **Peer-to-peer structure:** participants interact without hierarchical intermediaries; statuses define the level of responsibility and reward, but not of power
5. **Economic autonomy:** the CSC is capable of generating resources for its own operation and development without dependence on external investors or sponsors
6. **Social orientation:** the criterion for selecting projects for implementation is Social Relevance, not profit maximization

**Comparative analysis of organizational forms:**

```
Characteristic      | Traditional     | DAO             | CSC
                     | Corporation     | (existing)      | (CyberSocium)
─────────────────────┼─────────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────
Ownership            | Shareholders    | Tokenholders    | All participants
                     |                 |                 | (equal shares)
─────────────────────┼─────────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────
Governance           | Board of        | Token-weighted  | Verified
                     | directors       | voting          | activity +
                     |                 |                 | DAO
─────────────────────┼─────────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────
Success criterion    | Shareholder     | TVL / token     | Social
                     | profit          | price           | Relevance
─────────────────────┼─────────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────
Code                 | Proprietary     | Partially       | Fully
                     |                 | open            | open
─────────────────────┼─────────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────
Entry barrier        | Hiring /        | Token           | Participation
                     | share purchase  | purchase        | (minimal)
─────────────────────┼─────────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────
Scalability          | Hierarchical    | Limited         | PMIP-
                     |                 | (plutocracy of  | scaling
                     |                 | tokenholders)   |
─────────────────────┼─────────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────
Resilience to        | Depends on      | Depends on      | Antifragility
crises               | management      | token market    | (Theorem 3)
```

> 📊 **Professional diagram:** [Open SVG](diagrams/conceptual/organizational_forms_comparison.svg)

*Table 3.1: Comparative analysis of three organizational forms across seven key characteristics. The CSC (CyberSocial Corporation) differs qualitatively from traditional corporations and existing DAOs along all dimensions, particularly in ownership (all participants vs shareholders/tokenholders), governance (verified activity vs capital), success criteria (Social Relevance vs profit/TVL), and resilience to crises (Antifragility vs dependence on management/market).*

**Formal Definition 3.2.2.** A CSC is formally defined as a sextuple:

```
CSC = ⟨N, R, P, G, M, E⟩

where:
  N = {n₁, n₂, ..., nₖ} — set of participants (NFC — Network Financial Community)
    Each nᵢ is characterized by a tuple:
    nᵢ = ⟨walletᵢ, statusᵢ, reputationᵢ, activityᵢ, stakingᵢ⟩

  R — aggregate resource of the system:
    R = R_fin ⊕ R_int ⊕ R_rep ⊕ R_inf
    (financial ⊕ intellectual ⊕ reputational ⊕ infrastructural)

  P = {p₁, ..., pₘ} — set of projects (IPI model)
    Each pⱼ passes through the lifecycle:
    Idea → Discussion → Formation → Accumulation → Implementation → Operation

  G = {G_social, G_code, G_commerce, G_economic, G_macro}
    — governance system (four-class DAO taxonomy + MacroeconomicDAO)
    G implements the functions:
      — Collective decision: G × Proposal → {Accept, Reject, Defer}
      — Parameter adjustment: G × Param → Param'
      — Conflict resolution: G × Conflict → FRP(resolution)

  M — set of coordination mechanisms:
    M = {PMIP, SES, FRP, IPI, HybridVP, ActivityScoring}
    Each mechanism mₖ ∈ M possesses provable properties
    (incentive compatibility, Pareto efficiency, convergence)

  E — ecosystem infrastructure:
    E = {DSP, GyberNet, GyberComputer, GBR_token, DUNA, AiC}
    Physical realization of abstract components
```

**Properties of the CSC.** Definition 3.2.1 together with formal structure 3.2.2 yields a set of theoretically significant properties that distinguish the CSC from all existing organizational forms.

**Property 3.2.1 (Self-similarity).** The CSC possesses a fractal structure: each project within the CSC may be regarded as a mini-CSC with its own set of participants (SIC(p)), resources, governance (project-level DAO), and coordination mechanisms. This property ensures scalability: a CSC may contain an arbitrary number of nested CSC-like structures, analogously to Ostrom's "nested enterprises" (design principle 8).

**Property 3.2.2 (Openness of composition).** The set N is not fixed: any agent may join the NFC (A6, inclusiveness) and leave it at any moment. Formally: ∀t, N(t) ⊆ N(t+1) ∪ N'(t+1), where N'(t+1) is the set of agents that have left the system. This openness critically distinguishes the CSC from traditional corporations (closed employee roster) and even from most DAOs (token-purchase barrier).

**Property 3.2.3 (Antifragility, proved in Section 3.4).** The CSC possesses the property of Antifragility in Taleb's sense [39]: external stresses (participant outflows, token price drops, attacks) not only fail to destroy the system, but strengthen it through the activation of adaptation mechanisms. FRP transforms conflicts into diversity; SES selects for resilient projects; Activity Scoring redistributes weight in favour of active participants.

**Property 3.2.4 (Formal verifiability).** Each axiom A1–A8 translates into specific smart contracts with formally verifiable properties. This means that conformity of the system to its axioms can be proved mathematically, and not merely declared. Formal verification pipeline: Axiom → Specification → Solidity code → Slither/Mythril/Certora → Proven invariants.

A substantive distinction between the CSC and existing DAOs requires separate clarification. Most contemporary DAOs — MakerDAO, Compound, Uniswap — employ token-weighted voting, in which voting weight is proportional to the number of tokens held. This creates a plutocracy: large holders determine decisions, while small holders are effectively disenfranchised. Vitalik Buterin has repeatedly pointed to this problem [46]. In the CSC, voting weight is determined not by the number of tokens, but by **verified activity** — participation in projects confirmed by the G-Plan system and by participants of higher status. This implements the principle of meritocratic fairness (A5) and prevents plutocratic degeneration.

To illustrate the fundamental difference between a CSC and a traditional corporation, consider the response to the same external shock — the loss of 30% of active participants:

```
Response to the loss of 30% of participants:

  Traditional corporation:
    — Mass layoffs → loss of knowledge → decline in productivity
    — Share price falls → investors flee → further weakening
    — Management reacts in panic → erroneous strategic decisions
    — Possible outcome: bankruptcy or acquisition
    → Positive feedback (crisis amplification)

  Existing DAO:
    — Tokenholder outflow → token price falls
    — Treasury loses value → funding becomes impossible
    — Remaining participants lose motivation → further outflow
    — Possible outcome: dead protocol
    → Positive feedback (death spiral)

  CSC (CyberSocium):
    — 30% left → Activity Score of the remaining rises
      (less competition for rewards)
    — VP of the remaining increases → stronger governance
    — SES selects the most viable projects
      (resources concentrate on the best)
    — PMIP requires fewer participants to maintain
      financing (cost → 0 as |SIC| → ∞, but it works
      at smaller |SIC| as well)
    — FRP allows the remaining to reorganize quickly
    — Antifragility: the system emerges from the crisis
      more compact, but more efficient
    → Negative feedback (stabilization)
```

This example demonstrates that the CSC is not merely a "better version of a DAO" — it is a qualitatively different organizational form, in which multiple feedback mechanisms (Activity Score, SES, PMIP, FRP) provide resilience that is impossible within traditional corporate or token-weighted models.

### 3.3. Evolution of Forms of Governance over Economic Processes: A Formalization

The historical trajectory described in Section 1.5 can be formalized as a sequence of phase transitions in the space of organizational forms.

**Definition 3.3.1.** Let the space of organizational forms be described by a vector of characteristics:

```
Ω = ⟨D, T, A, S, C⟩

where:
  D ∈ [0, 1] — degree of decentralization of decision-making
  T ∈ [0, 1] — degree of operational transparency
  A ∈ [0, 1] — degree of accessibility of participation (inclusiveness)
  S ∈ [0, 1] — degree of participants' sovereignty over their data and assets
  C ∈ [0, 1] — degree of algorithmic coordination
               (displacement of human hierarchy by formalized protocols)
```

**Definition 3.3.2.** A phase transition in the space Ω is a historically observable qualitative change in the dominant organizational form, in which at least one component of the vector Ω undergoes a jump exceeding a threshold δ > 0.

**Historical sequence of phase transitions:**

```
Phase 0 — Monarchical Centralization (before the 18th century):
  Ω₀ ≈ ⟨0.05, 0.05, 0.02, 0.10, 0.01⟩

  Characteristics: Governance of economic processes
  concentrated in the hands of the sovereign. Trade guilds
  and craft associations operate under royal privilege.
  Transparency is absent. Participation is determined by
  social estate. Coordination — through direct orders
  and tradition.

  Dominant form of economic unit:
  manufactory, trading house, colonial company.

Phase 1 — Parliamentary Capitalism (18th–19th centuries):
  Ω₁ ≈ ⟨0.15, 0.15, 0.10, 0.20, 0.05⟩

  Transition: δ(D) ≈ 0.10 — governance functions passed
  from the monarch to parliament, representing the interests
  of broader economic forces.

  Characteristics: The emergence of the joint-stock company
  as a means of distributing risk and ownership. The birth
  of corporate law. The stock exchange as a mechanism
  for coordinating capital. However, participation is limited
  by property qualifications; the working class is excluded
  from governance.

  Dominant form: joint-stock company, bank.

  Key theorists: Adam Smith [26], David Ricardo.

Phase 2 — Corporate Capitalism (late 19th – mid-20th century):
  Ω₂ ≈ ⟨0.15, 0.20, 0.15, 0.15, 0.10⟩

  Transition: δ(C) ≈ 0.05 — emergence of management as
  a formalized discipline of coordination (Taylor, Ford).
  δ(A) ≈ 0.05 — expansion of participation through mass
  public securities markets.

  Characteristics: Transnational corporations.
  Separation of ownership from management (Berle and Means, 1932).
  The managerial revolution. Keynesian state regulation as
  compensation for market failures.

  Paradox: D does not grow, and in some respects declines —
  concentration of control in the hands of management while
  shareholders are dispersed.

  Dominant form: public corporation, state enterprise.

Phase 3 — Financial Capitalism / Neoliberalism (1970s – 2008):
  Ω₃ ≈ ⟨0.12, 0.18, 0.20, 0.12, 0.15⟩

  Transition: δ(C) ≈ 0.05 — algorithmic trading,
  derivatives, securitization.
  δ(A) ≈ 0.05 — expansion of retail investment.
  However: δ(D) < 0 — effective decline in
  decentralization: financial conglomerates,
  concentration of banking capital, too big to fail.
  δ(S) < 0 — decline in participant sovereignty:
  growing dependence on financial intermediaries.

  Characteristics: Economic hegemony of the financial
  sector. Globalization as intensification of financial
  flows without corresponding globalization of governance.
  Result — the 2008 crisis as a systemic failure of an
  opaque, hyper-concentrated system.

  Dominant form: investment bank, hedge fund,
  financial conglomerate.

  Critics: Stiglitz [9], Piketty [8], Minsky.

Phase 4 — Platform Capitalism (2000s – present):
  Ω₄ ≈ ⟨0.10, 0.15, 0.35, 0.08, 0.30⟩

  Transition: δ(A) ≈ 0.15 — radical reduction of
  participation barriers (any person with a phone is a user
  and a value generator).
  δ(C) ≈ 0.15 — algorithmic coordination through
  platforms (Uber, Airbnb, Amazon Marketplace).
  However: δ(D) < 0 — catastrophic decline in
  decentralization: GAFAM controls ~$10T+ in market
  capitalization, the data of billions of people.
  δ(S) < 0 — minimum of sovereignty: the user owns
  neither the account, nor the data, nor the algorithm.
  δ(T) < 0 — decline in transparency: closed algorithms
  for ranking, recommendations, and pricing.

  Phase 4 Paradox: maximum accessibility of participation
  combined with minimum sovereignty and minimum
  decentralization. Billions of people are involved in
  creating value, but are deprived of control over the
  results of their labour. This structural contradiction
  is the driving force behind the transition to Phase 5.

  Critics: Zuboff [6], Srnicek [7].

  Dominant form: platform (Google, Meta, Amazon).

Phase 5 — Declaration: Cybersocial Economics (emerging):
  Ω₅* ≈ ⟨0.85, 0.90, 0.90, 0.95, 0.85⟩  (target state)

  Transition: Simultaneous jump of all components.
  This is not an incremental improvement of one parameter
  (as in previous phases), but a phase transition in the
  full sense — a qualitative change in the nature of
  the organizational form. AI (A8) is a necessary
  condition for this transition: D=0.85 requires
  decentralized monitoring, T=0.90 — processing
  petabytes of data, C=0.85 — algorithmic coordination
  of millions of participants. Without AI these target
  values are unattainable.

  Characteristics: CSC as the dominant form of
  economic unit. PMIP as a financing mechanism.
  SES as a project selection mechanism.
  MacroeconomicDAO as a coordination tool.

  Marker: GyberExperiment as the first empirical
  realization.
```

**Theorem 3.3.1 (On the directionality of evolution).** The historical sequence Ω₀ → Ω₁ → Ω₂ → Ω₃ → Ω₄ demonstrates a general tendency toward growth in algorithmic coordination (C) and accessibility of participation (A), but a non-monotone dynamic of decentralization (D), transparency (T), and sovereignty (S). Phases 3 and 4 are characterized by *regression* of D, T, and S alongside growth of A and C, which creates a structural contradiction — mass participation in the absence of control. This contradiction is the driving force behind the transition to Phase 5, in which all five components simultaneously attain high values.

*Justification:* The Phase 4 contradiction is not a stable equilibrium. Mass involvement (high A) combined with low sovereignty (low S) and low decentralization (low D) means that enormous human potential is directed by governing algorithms (high C) in the interests of a narrow group of beneficiaries. As participants' awareness grows (the Zuboff effect, the effect of data leaks, the effect of decentralized alternatives), the costs of maintaining this disequilibrium increase, while the attractiveness of alternatives rises. This creates conditions for a phase transition analogous to the way in which the contradiction between the social character of production and the private form of appropriation created conditions for social transformations in the industrial era [26]. A formal proof of this theorem is an open problem and a subject for further research.

**Hypothesis 3.3.2 (On the irreversibility of the transition).** The transition from Phase 4 to Phase 5 possesses the property of irreversibility, conditioned by the following factors:

1. **Technological ratchet:** Cryptographic protocols, once widely disseminated, cannot be "recalled." A user who generates a key pair and encrypts their data acquires a sovereignty that cannot be taken away without physical coercion — and physical coercion does not scale to billions of people.

2. **Knowledge effect:** Understanding of the principles of decentralization, open code, and self-governance, once spread throughout society, cannot be "forgotten." This is analogous to the ratchet of literacy: a society that has learned to read does not return to illiteracy.

3. **Economic ratchet:** Every successful decentralized project creates infrastructure (code, protocols, standards) upon which subsequent projects are built. The cumulative effect makes a return economically irrational.

4. **Network effect:** The value of a decentralized network grows in proportion to the square of the number of participants (Metcalfe's law). After reaching critical mass, the network becomes self-sustaining.

*Counter-argument:* historical examples (the Great Firewall, the crypto winter of 2022, regulatory bans) demonstrate that reversibility is possible under certain conditions — large-scale state coercion, loss of trust in the technology, or a catastrophic economic shock. The hypothesis of irreversibility requires refinement: the transition is irreversible provided the network effect is maintained and no systemic crisis of trust occurs.

**Remark 3.3.3 (On heterogeneity and coexistence of forms).** The assertion of a transition to Phase 5 does not imply the instantaneous disappearance of organizational forms from preceding phases. Just as industrialization did not destroy artisan production but rather created a new dominant form coexisting with its predecessors, so cybersocialization creates a new dominant form — the CSC — which will coexist with traditional forms, gradually transforming the economic landscape without a radical, instantaneous paradigm shift.

### 3.4. The PMIP Concept and the Mechanism of Socio-Economic Selection

The Principle of Minimum Individual Participation (PMIP) is the central economic mechanism of the CSC. It formalizes a fundamental idea: given a sufficient number of participants, **any** project can be realized at **negligibly small** individual costs for each participant.

#### 3.4.1. Definitions

**Definition 3.4.1 (New Financial Community, NFC).** NFC = ⟨A, Gbr, Protocols⟩ — a triple consisting of: a set of agents A = {a₁, a₂, ..., aₙ}; the community token Gbr as a unit of value and coordination; and a set of formalized protocols (DAO, IPI, FRP, SES) defining the rules of interaction.

**Definition 3.4.2 (Social Investment Circle, SIC).** For a project p, SIC(p) ⊆ A is the set of NFC members interested in the realization of p and prepared to participate in its financing through PMIP. |SIC(p)| is unbounded; its size is determined by the Social Relevance of the project.

**Definition 3.4.3 (Active Group, AG).** AG(p) ⊆ SIC(p) is the subset of participants wishing to actively participate in the realization of p (development, testing, management, support). |AG(p)| is determined by the nature and scale of the project. AG is formed voluntarily from among SIC participants.

**Definition 3.4.4 (Coordinating Group, CG).** CG(p) is a professional auxiliary resource for managing the realization process. It may include both members and non-members of the NFC. CG is accountable to SIC(p) through DAO mechanisms.

**Definition 3.4.5 (Collective Capital).** CC(p) = Σᵢ investment_capacity(aᵢ) for all aᵢ ∈ SIC(p) — the aggregate investment capacity of SIC participants.

**Definition 3.4.6 (PMIP).** The cost of project C(p) is distributed across all funding participants:

```
individual_cost(p) = C(p) / |SIC(p)|

Fundamental property: as |SIC(p)| → ∞, individual_cost(p) → 0
```

**Definition 3.4.7 (Social Relevance, SR).** SR(p) = 1, if CC(p) ≥ C(p), i.e., the aggregate capital of the SIC covers the project's cost. SR(p) = 0, otherwise.

**Definition 3.4.8 (Threshold of Social Relevance, TSR).** TSR(p) = C(p) / min_capacity — the minimum number of SIC participants required to realize the project at minimum individual contribution.

#### 3.4.2. The IPI Model: Project Lifecycle

The lifecycle of a project in the CSC is formalized as a finite automaton **Idea → Project → Implementation** (IPI):

```
States: {Idea, Discussion, Formation, Accumulation, Implementation,
         Operation, Completion}

Transitions:
  Idea → Discussion:
    The author publishes the idea in the DSP project space.
    Any NFC participant may propose an idea — entry barrier is zero.

  Discussion → Formation:
    A sufficient number of participants have expressed interest
    (|interested| ≥ θ_formation).
    A preliminary SIC(p) and AG(p) are formed.
    An AI analyst generates a structured feasibility assessment.

  Formation → Accumulation:
    SR(p) = 1 (Social Relevance confirmed).
    Burning of 0.1% GBR of C(p) as a commitment signal.
    Project parameters are fixed: budget, timeline, AG.

  Accumulation → Implementation:
    CC(p) ≥ C(p) (necessary funds have been raised).
    Internal project tokens are issued.
    AG(p) commences realization under oversight of SIC(p).

  Implementation → Operation:
    The project is operational and generating value.
    Internal_token/GBR liquidity pool is created on DEX.

  Operation → Completion:
    Optional, for finite projects.
    Final report, distribution of outcomes.
```

#### 3.4.3. Economic Dynamics of PMIP

PMIP creates a unique economic dynamic, fundamentally different from all existing financing models:

```
┌────────────────────┬───────────────────┬────────────────┬────────────────┬─────────────────┬──────────────────┐
│ Characteristic     │ Venture Capital   │ Crowdfunding   │ Stock Market   │ State Budget    │ PMIP (CSC)       │
├────────────────────┼───────────────────┼────────────────┼────────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────┤
│ Who finances       │ Few (funds)       │ Many (crowd)   │ Shareholders   │ All (taxes)     │ SIC (interested) │
│ Selection criterion│ Profitability     │ Product        │ Market         │ Policy          │ Social           │
│                    │ for investor      │ attractiveness │ valuation      │                 │ Relevance        │
│ Entry barrier      │ $100K+            │ $1+            │ Medium         │ None (taxes)    │ Minimal          │
│ Maximum            │ $1–10B            │ $1–50M         │ $1–100B+       │ National budget │ Unlimited        │
│ project scale      │                   │                │                │                 │ (PMIP theorem)   │
│ Transparency       │ Minimal           │ Medium         │ Regulatory     │ Bureaucratic    │ Full (blockchain)│
│ Resilience to      │ Low               │ Medium         │ Market         │ Depends on      │ High             │
│ manipulation       │ (1 investor)       │                │ volatility     │ system          │ (decentralization)│
└────────────────────┴───────────────────┴────────────────┴────────────────┴─────────────────┴──────────────────┘
```

Key inversion: in traditional models, large-scale projects require concentration of capital; in PMIP, large-scale projects become possible through distribution across many. The more socially significant a project, the larger the SIC → the smaller the individual_cost → the higher the probability of realization.

#### 3.4.4. PMIP Theorems

**Theorem 1 (Universal Realizability).** For any project p with cost C(p) > 0 and any min_capacity > 0, there exists a finite N* = ⌈C(p) / min_capacity⌉ such that for |SIC(p)| ≥ N* the project p is socially relevant (SR(p) = 1).

*Proof:* For N ≥ N*: individual_cost = C(p)/N ≤ C(p)/N* ≤ min_capacity. Therefore CC(p) = N × min_capacity ≥ N* × min_capacity ≥ C(p). ∎

**Theorem 2 (Scale Inversion).** In the PMIP model, with a fixed size of the global NFC (|A| = const), the probability of realizing a project correlates positively with its social significance.

*Justification:* Let the social significance of project p be defined as the fraction of NFC agents interested in its realization: significance(p) = |SIC(p)| / |A|. Then:

```
individual_cost(p) = C(p) / |SIC(p)| = C(p) / (significance(p) × |A|)
```

With fixed C(p) and |A|: the higher significance(p), the lower individual_cost(p), the higher the probability that individual_cost(p) ≤ min_capacity, and hence the higher the probability of SR(p) = 1.

This inverts the traditional logic, in which large-scale projects are accessible only to concentrated capital. In PMIP, large-scale projects become possible precisely through distribution across many — provided the project is sufficiently significant to attract that many. ∎

A formal proof of this theorem is an open problem and a subject for further research.

**Theorem 3 (Antifragility).** A system based on PMIP possesses the property of Antifragility in Taleb's sense [35]: an increase in the number of participants does not merely make the system resilient to shocks, but strengthens its capacity to realize projects of any complexity.

*Proof:* Let δ be an economic shock reducing min_capacity by ε > 0. Then the new threshold:

```
TSR'(p) = C(p) / (min_capacity - ε)  >  TSR(p)
```

However, if simultaneously the number of participants grows: N' = N + Δ, then for sufficient Δ:

```
N' ≥ TSR'(p) ⟺ N + Δ ≥ C(p) / (min_capacity - ε)
⟺ Δ ≥ C(p) / (min_capacity - ε) - N
```

For large N, even significant ε is offset by a small relative increment Δ/N. Moreover, economic shocks, as a rule, stimulate the search for alternative economic models, which increases the inflow of participants into the NFC. Thus, the system not merely withstands shocks, but uses them as a source of growth — which is the defining property of Antifragility. ∎

*Limitation:* the Antifragility of the system depends on the type of shock. The crypto winter of 2022 demonstrated that economic shocks within the crypto industry may cause participant outflow rather than inflow. The theorem holds for shocks in the traditional economy that stimulate a search for alternatives, but requires empirical verification for endogenous crypto-economic crises. A formal proof of this theorem is an open problem and a subject for further research.

**Theorem 4 ("Millions are Billionaires").** With a sufficient number of NFC participants, the collective capital reaches a scale comparable to the budgets of the largest institutions and state programs, at individual costs that are negligibly small for each participant.

*Demonstration:*

```
Scenario A:
  N = 10⁶ (one million participants)
  min_capacity = $10/month
  CC = $10⁷/month = $120M/year

Scenario B:
  N = 10⁷ (ten million participants)
  min_capacity = $5/month
  CC = $5 × 10⁷/month = $600M/year

Scenario C:
  N = 10⁸ (one hundred million participants)
  min_capacity = $3/month
  CC = $3 × 10⁸/month = $3.6B/year

Scenario D:
  N = 10⁹ (one billion participants)
  min_capacity = $1/month
  CC = $10⁹/month = $12B/year

For comparison:
  — Annual CERN budget: ~$1.2B
  — Annual NASA budget: ~$25B
  — Annual National Institutes of Health (NIH, USA) budget: ~$47B
  — Total global venture capital market volume (2023): ~$345B
  — Scenario D at $1/month covers CERN's budget in 1 month
  — Scenario C at $3/month exceeds ESA's budget (European
    Space Agency, ~$7.7B) in 2.1 years
```

Corollary: the statement "Millions are Billionaires" is not a slogan but a mathematical consequence of PMIP. This is arithmetic, not utopia. The question lies not in the mathematics — it lies in creating a coordination infrastructure that ensures: (a) formation of SIC at sufficient scale; (b) transparent and verifiable fund collection; (c) accountable project implementation; (d) fair distribution of results. This is precisely the subject of GyberExperiment. ∎

A formal proof of this theorem is an open problem and a subject for further research.

**Corollary 4.1 (AI Augmentation of PMIP).** Artificial intelligence accelerates the convergence of PMIP toward a zero individual participation threshold simultaneously from two directions, introducing a qualitatively new dynamic:

*First direction — reduction of C(p):*

AI-assisted development (code generation, automated testing, AI security auditing of smart contracts) reduces the cost of project realization. AI coordination within AG(p) reduces management overhead. AI analytics prevent costly design errors at early stages. Therefore:

```
C(p | AI) < C(p)

individual_cost(p | AI) = C(p | AI) / |SIC(p)| < C(p) / |SIC(p)|
```

*Second direction — increase of effective |SIC|:*

Coordination costs at large |SIC| are a fundamental limitation of PMIP (see Loop 5, Section 3.6). Brooks showed that in traditional organizations coordination costs grow as O(n²). In the CSC, formalized protocols reduce this growth to O(n log n). An AI agent as mediator — aggregating the positions of thousands of participants, clustering opinions, formulating decision options for voting — reduces the growth of coordination costs to O(n):

```
Without AI:  effective_capacity(SIC) = |SIC| / coordination_overhead(|SIC|)
             coordination_overhead ∝ log(|SIC|)

With AI:     effective_capacity(SIC | AI) ≈ |SIC|
             coordination_overhead ≈ const (AI scales trivially)
```

*Combined effect:*

```
individual_cost(p | AI) = C(p | AI) / effective_SIC(p | AI)

With C(p | AI) < C(p) and effective_SIC(p | AI) > effective_SIC(p):
  individual_cost(p | AI) << individual_cost(p)

AI accelerates PMIP's convergence to zero EXPONENTIALLY,
not linearly — which in practice means that the threshold
of Social Relevance TSR(p) is crossed for projects of
any cost with significantly fewer participants than
predicted by the model without AI.
```

This corollary has fundamental significance: AI is not an external supplement to PMIP, but a structural factor that eliminates the practical limitations of a theoretically sound mechanism. AI coordination substantially reduces the communication component of Brooks's law (O(n²) → O(n) for routine coordination tasks), although the core of the law — the complexity of judgment coordination — is preserved for strategic decisions (Axiom A8). ∎

#### 3.4.5. Mechanism for Resolving Internal Contradictions: Fork Resolution Protocol

Any system of collective decision-making confronts the problem of internal disagreements. Traditional hierarchical structures resolve it through the mechanism of managerial authority — the decision is made by whoever is higher in the hierarchy. In the CSC no such mechanism exists by definition (Axiom A1). A formalized protocol is required that preserves decentralization.

**The cost estimation problem.**

Precise determination of C(p) is difficult in the early stages of a project. An underestimate leads to underfunding; an overestimate leads to inefficient resource allocation and an artificially inflated TSR.

Solution: iterative cost estimation model — the initial estimate is formulated by the idea's author at the Discussion stage; at the Formation stage the estimate is revised with the participation of AG and CG (if engaged); the final fixation of C(p) occurs upon transition to Accumulation, when the project's internal tokens are issued. In addition, the modular structure of the project allows large projects to be decomposed into independent subprojects, which has demonstrated both potential and risks;
  MolochDAO (2019), Compound Governance (2020),
  Gitcoin (2019), Nouns DAO (2021), ConstitutionDAO (2021).

  Significance: Demonstrated the ability of distributed
  groups to coordinate significant resources without
  centralized management.
  ConstitutionDAO raised $47M in 72 hours from >17,000
  participants — an empirical demonstration of PMIP-
  like dynamics, albeit without a formal theory.

  At the same time, key problems were identified:
  tokenholder plutocracy, low voting turnout,
  vulnerability to governance attacks.

  Theorists: Buterin [46], Weyl and Posner [44],
  Zargent.

Stage 4 — Cybersocial Economics (emerging):
  CSC as the fundamental economic unit.
  PMIP as a financing mechanism.
