  SES as a project selection mechanism.
  MacroeconomicDAO as a tool for
  macroeconomic governance.

  Marker: GyberExperiment.

  Significance: Transition from isolated experiments
  with DAO and DeFi to a coherent system integrating
  financing, governance, implementation, and
  distribution of results into a single
  theoretically grounded framework.
```

### 3.5. Socio-Economic Selection and Project Evolution

The PMIP mechanism resolves the financing problem: how to provide resources for projects without concentrated capital. However, equally important is the question of **selection**: which projects to realize? In the traditional economy, selection is carried out through the market (projects that maximize profit) or through the state (projects determined by political will). The CSC offers a fundamentally different mechanism.

#### 3.5.1. Definition of SES

**Definition 3.5.1 (Socio-Economic Selection, SES).** SES is an evolutionary mechanism in which projects compete not for investor capital, but for **societal interest**. The winner is not the project that is most profitable for the investor, but the one that is most socially relevant — that is, interesting to the greatest number of people.

Formally: let there be a set of projects P = {p₁, p₂, ..., pₘ} competing for NFC resources. For each project pᵢ a SIC(pᵢ) is formed. A project is realized if and only if SR(pᵢ) = 1. Thus, SES is a **decentralized filter**: no central authority makes the decision about realization — the decision is made by the aggregate of individual decisions by agents to join the SIC.

This mechanism is analogous to natural selection in biology, but operates in the space of ideas and projects. As biological evolution selects organisms by their fitness to the environment, SES selects projects by their fitness to the needs of society.

#### 3.5.2. Stages of Cybersocialization of the Economy

**Definition 3.5.2 (Cybersocialization of the Economy).** The *Cybersocialization of the Economy* is a historical process of transformation of global socio-economic relations, in which: information technologies and cryptographic protocols become not tools of the economy, but the **habitat** of economic relations; governance of economic processes passes from hierarchical institutions to distributed peer-to-peer mechanisms; the economic unit transforms from a closed corporation into an open CSC; a collective economic intelligence emerges — the capacity of society to make rational collective decisions about resource allocation through formalized mechanisms.

```
Stages of cybersocialization:

Stage 0 — Prerequisites (1970–2000):
  Development of the internet, free software, e-commerce.
  Markers: ARPANET → WWW, GNU/Linux, Amazon, eBay

Stage 1 — Digitization of exchange (2000–2010):
  Social networks, platforms, Web 2.0.
  Markers: Google, Facebook, YouTube, Wikipedia

Stage 2 — Decentralization of finance (2009–2020):
  Blockchain, cryptocurrencies, DeFi.
  Markers: Bitcoin, Ethereum, Uniswap, MakerDAO

Stage 3 — Decentralization of governance (2016–present):
  DAO, on-chain governance, token-weighted voting.
  Markers: The DAO (2016), MolochDAO, Gitcoin, Nouns DAO

Stage 4 — Cybersocial Economics (emerging):
  CSC as the fundamental economic unit.
  PMIP as a financing mechanism. MacroeconomicDAO.
  Marker: GyberExperiment
```

#### 3.5.3. Historical Analogy

For a deeper understanding of the scale and character of the transformation underway, it is useful to draw an analogy with the Industrial Revolution — the last phase transition of comparable scale.

Carlota Perez, in her foundational work "Technological Revolutions and Financial Capital" (2002), showed that every technological revolution passes through a cycle: installation of a new technological paradigm → financial bubble → crisis → a deployment period in which the institutional structure finally adapts to the new technology [42]. We suggest that the digital revolution is in transition from the bubble/crisis phase (the dot-com crash of 2000, the 2008 crisis, the crypto winter of 2022) to the deployment phase, in which the institutional innovation — the CSC — becomes the dominant form.

```
Phase 0 — Monarchical Centralization (before the 18th century):
  Ω₀ ≈ ⟨0.05, 0.05, 0.02, 0.10, 0.01⟩

  Characteristics: Governance of economic processes
  concentrated in the hands of the sovereign. Trade guilds
  and craft associations operate under royal privilege.
  Transparency is absent. Participation is determined by
  social estate. Coordination — through direct orders
  and tradition.

  Dominant form of economic unit:
  manufactory, trading house, colonial company.

Phase 1 — Parliamentary Capitalism (18th–19th centuries):
  Ω₁ ≈ ⟨0.15, 0.15, 0.10, 0.20, 0.05⟩

  Transition: δ(D) ≈ 0.10 — governance functions passed
  from the monarch to parliament, representing the interests
  of broader economic forces.

  Characteristics: The emergence of the joint-stock company
  as a means of distributing risk and ownership. The birth
  of corporate law. The stock exchange as a mechanism
  for coordinating capital. However, participation is limited
  by property qualifications; the working class is excluded
  from governance.

  Dominant form: joint-stock company, bank.

  Key theorists: Adam Smith [26], David Ricardo.

Phase 2 — Corporate Capitalism (late 19th – mid-20th century):
  Ω₂ ≈ ⟨0.15, 0.20, 0.15, 0.15, 0.10⟩

  Transition: δ(C) ≈ 0.05 — emergence of management as
  a formalized discipline of coordination (Taylor, Ford).
  δ(A) ≈ 0.05 — expansion of participation through mass
  public securities markets.

  Characteristics: Transnational corporations.
  Separation of ownership from management (Berle and Means, 1932).
  The managerial revolution. Keynesian state regulation as
  compensation for market failures.

  Paradox: D does not grow, and in some respects declines —
  concentration of control in the hands of management while
  shareholders are dispersed.

  Dominant form: public corporation, state enterprise.

Phase 3 — Financial Capitalism / Neoliberalism (1970s – 2008):
  Ω₃ ≈ ⟨0.12, 0.18, 0.20, 0.12, 0.15⟩

  Transition: δ(C) ≈ 0.05 — algorithmic trading,
  derivatives, securitization.
  δ(A) ≈ 0.05 — expansion of retail investment.
  However: δ(D) < 0 — effective decline in
  decentralization: financial conglomerates,
  concentration of banking capital, too big to fail.
  δ(S) < 0 — decline in participant sovereignty:
  growing dependence on financial intermediaries.

  Characteristics: Economic hegemony of the financial
  sector. Globalization as intensification of financial
  flows without corresponding globalization of governance.
  Result — the 2008 crisis as a systemic failure of an
  opaque, hyper-concentrated system.

  Dominant form: investment bank, hedge fund,
  financial conglomerate.

  Critics: Stiglitz [9], Piketty [8], Minsky.

Phase 4 — Platform Capitalism (2000s – present):
  Ω₄ ≈ ⟨0.10, 0.15, 0.35, 0.08, 0.30⟩

  Transition: δ(A) ≈ 0.15 — radical reduction of
  participation barriers (any person with a phone is a user
  and a value generator).
  δ(C) ≈ 0.15 — algorithmic coordination through
  platforms (Uber, Airbnb, Amazon Marketplace).
  However: δ(D) < 0 — catastrophic decline in
  decentralization: GAFAM controls ~$10T+ in market
  capitalization, the data of billions of people.
  δ(S) < 0 — minimum of sovereignty: the user owns
  neither the account, nor the data, nor the algorithm.
  δ(T) < 0 — decline in transparency: closed algorithms
  for ranking, recommendations, and pricing.

  Phase 4 Paradox: maximum accessibility of participation
  combined with minimum sovereignty and minimum
  decentralization. Billions of people are involved in
  creating value, but are deprived of control over the
  results of their labour. This structural contradiction
  is the driving force behind the transition to Phase 5.

  Critics: Zuboff [6], Srnicek [7].

  Dominant form: platform (Google, Meta, Amazon).

Phase 5 — Declaration: Cybersocial Economics (emerging):
  Ω₅* ≈ ⟨0.85, 0.90, 0.90, 0.95, 0.85⟩  (target state)

  Transition: Simultaneous jump of all components.
  This is not an incremental improvement of one parameter
  (as in previous phases), but a phase transition in the
  full sense — a qualitative change in the nature of
  the organizational form. AI (A8) is a necessary
  condition for this transition: D=0.85 requires
  decentralized monitoring, T=0.90 — processing
  petabytes of data, C=0.85 — algorithmic coordination
  of millions of participants. Without AI these target
  values are unattainable.

  Characteristics: CSC as the dominant form of
  economic unit. PMIP as a financing mechanism.
  SES as a project selection mechanism.
  MacroeconomicDAO as a coordination tool.

  Marker: GyberExperiment as the first empirical
  realization.
```

> 📊 **Professional diagram:** [Open SVG](diagrams/conceptual/evolution_timeline.svg)

*Figure 3.1: Structural analogy between the Industrial Revolution (18th–19th centuries) and Cybersocialization (21st century). Every technological revolution creates new organizational forms (factory/CSC), economic units (joint-stock company/CyberSocial Corporation), coordination mechanisms (stock exchange/MacroeconomicDAO), social forces (working class/digital intelligentsia), and requires new institutional infrastructure (legislation, theory, philosophy).*

The analogy is not superficial. Each element has a deep structural correspondence. Just as the joint-stock company allowed the risk of large enterprises (transoceanic trade, railway construction) to be distributed across many investors, so the CSC through PMIP allows the costs of large-scale projects to be distributed across an arbitrarily large number of participants. Just as the stock exchange created a mechanism for price discovery and aggregation of dispersed capital, so MacroeconomicDAO creates a mechanism for determining Social Relevance and aggregating dispersed interest. Just as the industrial working class became conscious of itself as a social force and formed institutions to protect its interests (trade unions, workers' parties), so the digital intelligentsia becomes conscious of itself through the Open Source movement, crypto-communities, and DAO experiments, and forms institutions of a new type — decentralized, open, global.

Of course, the analogy has its limits. The Industrial Revolution took place in a world of nation-states and was tied to physical infrastructure (factories, railways). Cybersocialization takes place in a global digital space and is largely abstracted from physical geography. This means that the pace of transformation may be substantially higher, and its scope — global from the very outset, bypassing the stage of national-scale development.

#### 3.5.4. AI-Directed Evolution: from Blind Selection to Designed Selection

The SES model described above represents an analogue of biological evolution (formalized in detail in Section 3.6.2, property E4). However, biological evolution has a fundamental limitation — it is **blind**: mutations are random, selection is undirected, trillions of organisms perish in order to consolidate one successful adaptation.

In the CSC a Lamarckian component is already present — acquired traits (experience, code, infrastructure) are inherited by subsequent projects. Artificial intelligence introduces a qualitatively new element — **foresight** — transforming SES from an undirected evolutionary process into a designed one:

```
Biological evolution:
  Mutation (random) → Selection (blind) → Inheritance

SES without AI:
  Idea (from human) → SES (SR as criterion) → Inheritance of code/experience

SES with AI (directed evolution):
  AI environment analysis → Generation of directed ideas →
  AI forecast of SR → SES (enhanced) →
  Inheritance + AI optimization of the inherited
```

**AI-generated ideas based on ecosystem gap analysis.** An AI agent with access to the full map of projects, their dependencies, and outcomes is capable of identifying: unfilled niches ("the ecosystem has X and Y, but lacks Z to connect them"), bottlenecks ("80% of projects are blocked by the absence of component W"), external trends ("demand for category K is growing; the ecosystem has competencies to realize it"). Ideas prompted or generated by AI have a higher probability of being socially relevant than random ideas from individual agents. At the same time, AI-generated proposals are obligatorily marked as such — the decision to adopt them remains with the community (Axiom A7).

**AI predictor of Social Relevance.** Before the author invests significant effort in a full project specification, AI is capable of: estimating the potential |SIC(p)| based on precedent data, modelling TSR(p) for various budget parameters, and recommending modifications that increase SR. This reduces "evolutionary waste" — efforts expended on projects doomed to SR = 0.

**AI augmentation of FRP.** Fork Resolution Protocol (Section 3.4.5) describes a fork as a last resort; however, a fork is a costly operation: duplication of effort, community split. AI optimizes each stage of FRP:
- *Deliberation stage:* AI clusters participant positions, identifies points of agreement and real (rather than apparent) points of divergence, visualizes the argument space
- *Synthesis stage:* AI proposes compromise solutions v*, maximizing |support(v*)| / |SIC(p)|, based on analysis of each group's preferences
- *Fork stage (if unavoidable):* AI models the viability of each branch — forecasts |SIC| for each option, assesses resource sufficiency, warns of risks

Formally, AI-enhanced SES raises the success rate of the system through three channels:

```
Success_rate_AI = f(directed_diversity, enhanced_selection, accelerated_inheritance)

directed_diversity > random_diversity
  (AI generates ideas in promising directions)

enhanced_selection > blind_selection
  (AI reduces noise in SR evaluation — see property E1 below)

accelerated_inheritance > passive_inheritance
  (AI systematizes and makes instantly accessible
   the experience of all past projects — see property E6 below)
```

Analogy with biology: AI transforms SES from a process of natural selection into a process resembling genetic engineering — directed modifications with predictable outcomes, while maintaining evolutionary diversity through FRP.

**AI transformation of the IPI lifecycle.** At each phase of the Idea-Project-Implementation model, AI introduces qualitatively new capabilities:

- **Idea phase:** three parallel streams — (1) a human proposes an idea, AI validates it; (2) AI identifies ecosystem gaps and generates proposals; (3) AI forms an "opportunity map" based on environmental analysis
- **Formation phase:** AI automatically generates a draft specification based on discussions, identifies unaddressed risks, proposes an optimal composition of AG(p) based on competencies and participant workloads
- **Accumulation phase:** AI forecasts the rate of accumulation, optimizes parameters of internal tokens, models the stability of the future LP
- **Implementation phase:** AI as co-developer — code generation, automated testing, continuous security auditing, automated documentation. AI Project Manager — task decomposition, optimal assignment, early warning of delays
- **Functioning phase:** AI monitoring of metrics, anomaly detection, parameter optimization, revenue and load forecasting

Combined effect: AI reduces the IPI cycle time (from idea to functioning) by a factor of 3–5, which implies a proportional acceleration of Loop 1 (Growth Loop, Section 3.6.3) and, consequently, exponential acceleration of the entire ecosystem's development.

#### 3.5.5. Formal Properties of SES: Game-Theoretic Analysis

Socio-Economic Selection can be formalized in terms of evolutionary game theory (Maynard Smith, 1982 [81]), which allows analysis of its stability and efficiency.

**Definition 3.5.3 (SES as an evolutionary game).** Let the strategy of agent a ∈ A be described by a resource allocation vector:

```
σ(a) = (w₁, w₂, ..., wₘ),  Σwᵢ = 1,  wᵢ ≥ 0

where wᵢ is the share of resources allocated by agent a
to project pᵢ ∈ P.

Payoff function of the agent:
  u(a) = Σᵢ wᵢ × R(pᵢ) × q(a, pᵢ)

where:
  R(pᵢ) — total value of the project (0 if SR=0)
  q(a, pᵢ) — share of the project's value accruing
              to agent a (depends on contribution, status,
              size of SIC)
```

**Theorem 3.5.1 (Pareto Optimality of SES).** In the stationary state, under full information and rational agents, the resource allocation in SES is Pareto-optimal: it is impossible to improve the position of one agent without worsening the position of another.

*Justification:* In the stationary state each agent allocates resources to projects with maximum expected payoff. If a Pareto-improving reallocation existed, rational agents would already have moved to it. The condition SR(p) = 1 ensures that only projects with sufficient societal interest receive resources, which corresponds to the social optimum. Limitation: full information is not satisfied in practice; AI agents partially compensate for this by aggregating and analysing information (A8). A formal proof under conditions of incomplete information is an open problem. ∎

**Theorem 3.5.2 (Evolutionary Stability of Cooperation in SES).** The strategy "participation in SIC" is an Evolutionarily Stable Strategy (ESS) provided |NFC| ≥ N_critical, where N_critical depends on the reward parameters.

*Justification:* Consider two strategies: C (cooperator — participates in SIC and contributes) and D (defector — free-rider, does not participate, but uses the publicly available results). In traditional models D dominates C (the free-rider problem, Olson 1965 [78]). In SES this dynamic is inverted:

```
Payoff of cooperator:
  u(C) = R(p) × q(C, p) − cost(C, p)
  where q(C, p) > 0 (reward through UnitManager,
  reputation, Activity Score, access to services)

Payoff of defector:
  u(D) = external_benefit(p) − 0
  where external_benefit(p) << R(p) × q(C, p)
  (the defector receives only external externalities,
  but not internal rewards)

ESS condition: u(C) > u(D) ⟺
  R(p) × q(C, p) − cost(C, p) > external_benefit(p)
```

UnitManager ensures q(C, p) > 0 through direct GBR rewards. PMIP ensures cost(C, p) → 0 at large |SIC|. Activity Scoring creates an additional gap: cooperators receive a high Activity Score (→ greater voting weight, greater rewards), defectors receive a low Activity Score (→ status degradation, loss of access). Thus, with a sufficiently large |NFC| and a functioning Activity Scoring system, the strategy of cooperation dominates free-riding. ∎

**Theorem 3.5.3 (Diversity as an Evolutionary Advantage).** A system with several competing projects in one niche possesses higher adaptability than a system with a single project for each function.

*Justification:* By analogy with Page's diversity theorem [41], the aggregate prediction accuracy of a group increases with diversity of approaches, not with individual accuracy of each predictor. In the context of SES: several competing implementations of a single idea (made possible by FRP and the low IPI entry barrier) provide evolutionary pressure that raises quality. This formalizes Linus Torvalds's intuition: "given enough eyeballs, all bugs are shallow" (Linus's Law).

FRP is the key mechanism for maintaining diversity: rather than forcing a choice between competing approaches (as in a corporation), the system allows forks — parallel development of alternatives with subsequent evaluation of results through SES. The losing variant is not destroyed, but archived, preserving accumulated experience for future iterations. ∎

#### 3.5.6. Economic Dynamics of SES: A Formal Model

For a quantitative description of SES dynamics, we employ the Replicator Dynamics model, standard in evolutionary game theory.

Let x(pᵢ, t) be the share of NFC resources allocated to project pᵢ at time t. The dynamics are described by a system of differential equations:

```
dx(pᵢ)/dt = x(pᵢ) × [f(pᵢ) − f̄]

where:
  f(pᵢ) = SR(pᵢ) × performance(pᵢ) — "fitness" of the project
  f̄ = Σⱼ x(pⱼ) × f(pⱼ) — average fitness of the system
  performance(pᵢ) = function(revenue, SIC growth,
    product quality, participant satisfaction)
```

**Properties of this dynamic:**

1. *Positive feedback:* projects with f(pᵢ) > f̄ grow, attracting more resources. Projects with f(pᵢ) < f̄ shrink, losing participants.

2. *Stationary states:* the system tends toward a state in which projects with the highest fitness survive. This does not imply a monoculture: different niches (Social DAO, Code DAO, Commerce DAO, Economic DAO) have independent fitness landscapes.

3. *Role of noise:* random fluctuations (new ideas, external shocks, changes in preferences) prevent premature convergence to a suboptimal state (lock-in). FRP is the institutional mechanism for maintaining "noise" — diversity of approaches.

4. *AI modification:* the AI predictor of SR (Section 3.5.4) modifies the dynamic, accelerating reallocation of resources from weak projects to strong ones. Formally: AI increases the coefficient multiplying [f(pᵢ) − f̄], accelerating convergence without loss of diversity (AI recommends, not decides; FRP preserves alternatives).

5. *Meta-evolution:* SES applies not only to projects but to the DAO rules themselves. Proposals to change system parameters (quorums, thresholds, VP formula) compete through the same evolutionary mechanism: successful institutional innovations are copied by other DAOs, unsuccessful ones are rejected. This ensures adaptability of the system at the meta-level.

**Empirical verification.** The formal SES model requires empirical calibration. The necessary data will be collected during GyberExperiment: distribution of resources among projects, SIC dynamics, project success/failure rates, IPI cycle durations. cadCAD simulation (Section 6.3) allows testing of model parameters before their application in the real system.

#### 3.5.7. Mechanism Design of SES: Incentive Compatibility and Strategic Robustness

The key question for any mechanism of resource allocation is whether it is **incentive compatible** — that is, whether honest behaviour is the optimal strategy for each participant. This question is formalized in terms of mechanism theory (Hurwicz, 1960; Myerson, 1981) and allows formal properties of SES as an economic mechanism to be proved.

**Definition (SES mechanism).** The SES mechanism is a tuple M_SES = ⟨A, Θ, S, g, u⟩, where:

```
A = {a₁, ..., aₙ} — set of participants (NFC)
Θ = {θ₁, ..., θₙ} — participant types (private information:
    skills, preferences, time resources)
S = S₁ × ... × Sₙ — strategy space
    (for each participant: which projects to invest in,
     what contribution to make, whom to vote for)
g: S → X — outcome function (distribution of resources
    among projects, accrual of GBR rewards)
u: X × Θ → ℝ — utility function (depending on outcome
    and participant type)
```

**Theorem 5 (Incentive Compatibility of SES in Dominant Strategies).** Under the following conditions, the SES mechanism is incentive compatible in dominant strategies (DSIC):

(C1) Activity Score is computed from verifiable on-chain actions (not manipulable without cost);
(C2) Hybrid VP includes the square root of the stake (√GBR);
(C3) MemberStatus is assigned through verifiable thresholds (Activity Score ≥ θ_status);
(C4) Reward is proportional to f(Activity Score, MemberStatus).

*Proof (sketch).* Consider the strategy of participant aᵢ with type θᵢ. The participant may deviate from honest behaviour in three ways:

**Deviation 1: manipulation of Activity Score.** To increase Activity Score, on-chain activity is required (commits → G-Plan → verification, votes, DSP interactions). Falsification of on-chain data is impossible (guaranteed by blockchain cryptography). Generation of "fictitious" activity (spam transactions, fictitious tasks) is detected by the AI Activity Verifier and Sybil detector (Appendix F.3). The cost of undetected falsification grows with the quality of AI models (GNN precision ≥ 0.95), exceeding the potential gain. Therefore: for a rational agent, the optimal strategy for increasing Activity Score = genuine activity.

**Deviation 2: strategic voting.** In Hybrid VP, voting power VP = √(GBR_staked) × AM × SB. The square root of the stake means that increasing VP through GBR purchase costs quadratically more: doubling VP requires quadrupling the stake. With >100 participants, the cost of manipulating a voting outcome exceeds the expected benefit (proof analogous to the analysis of quadratic voting, see Lalley & Weyl [29]). AM and SB depend on Activity Score and MemberStatus respectively — both based on verifiable metrics (see Deviation 1).

**Deviation 3: coalition attack.** A group of participants may coordinate voting to direct resources to a project controlled by the coalition. Defences: (a) LP Burn — the project initiator irreversibly burns LP tokens, making "shell projects" economically unattractive; (b) the IPI cycle requires passing through stages (Discussion → Formation → Implementation), at each of which unrelated participants may block a suspicious project; (c) SES dynamics ensure that projects without real output lose resources (f(pᵢ) < f̄ → dx(pᵢ)/dt < 0). Formally: the cost of maintaining a coalition grows linearly with the number of participants and time (constant LP Burn, constant activity in IPI), while the benefit is limited to the fund of a single project. ∎

**Corollary.** SES approximates the property of Incentive Compatibility characteristic of Vickrey–Clarke–Groves (VCG) mechanisms, but is realized through *behavioural incentives* (Activity Score, MemberStatus) rather than through monetary transfers. This is an extension of mechanism theory to systems in which a participant's utility depends not only on financial outcome, but also on social status, reputation, and a sense of belonging — factors that Ostrom [30] identified as key to sustainable institutions of collective action.

#### 3.5.8. Multi-population Dynamics: Four DAOs as Ecological Niches

Classical Replicator Dynamics (Section 3.5.6) describes competition among projects *within* a single DAO class. However, the four-class architecture of CyberSocium creates *multi-population* dynamics, in which projects of different classes interact yet compete for partially different resources — analogously to ecological niches in evolutionary biology.

Let us formalize this dynamic. Let x_k(pᵢ, t) be the share of the k-th DAO class's resources (k ∈ {Social, Code, Commerce, Economic}) allocated to project pᵢ. Each DAO class has its own fitness landscape:

```
Multi-population SES:

  Social DAO:
    f_S(p) = engagement(p) × community_growth(p)
    Projects compete for: attention, moderation,
    community building
    Resource: GBR from Social DAO treasury

  Code DAO:
    f_C(p) = code_quality(p) × technical_impact(p)
    Projects compete for: developers, review capacity,
    merge slots
    Resource: GBR from Code DAO treasury + developer hours

  Commerce DAO:
    f_M(p) = revenue(p) × scalability(p) × SR(p)
    Projects compete for: capital (LP Burn),
    users, market niches
    Resource: GBR from Commerce DAO treasury + LP

  Economic DAO:
    f_E(p) = ecosystem_impact(p) × sustainability(p)
    Projects compete for: system parameters,
    strategic resources
    Resource: MacroeconomicDAO treasury

  Cross-class interactions:
    ──────────────────────────────
    Commerce → Code: commercial projects create demand
    for technical infrastructure
    Code → Social: technical tools improve
    user experience
    Social → Commerce: community generates users
    for commercial projects
    Economic → All: meta-rules determine the fitness landscape
    of all classes
```

**Theorem 6 (Stability of Multi-population SES).** In a four-class system with cross-class interactions, a stable dynamic equilibrium exists if the following conditions are satisfied:

(i) fitness functions f_k are sublinear (diminishing returns to scale within each class);
(ii) cross-class interactions are positive (mutual benefit between classes);
(iii) MacroeconomicDAO adjusts parameters upon deviation from equilibrium.

*Sketch proof.* Under condition (i), no project can capture the entire resource of a class (diminishing returns). Condition (ii) ensures that the growth of one class stimulates the growth of others (positive feedback between classes). Condition (iii) creates negative feedback at the meta-level (MacroeconomicDAO as stabilizer). The combination of positive feedback within classes and negative feedback between levels is a classical recipe for stable complex order (Kauffman, "edge of chaos" [12]). A formal proof via dynamical systems theory (existence of an Attractor) requires numerical verification through cadCAD and remains an open problem (RQ5, Section 10.4). ∎

This multi-population model explains why the four-class architecture outperforms a monolithic DAO: instead of one fitness landscape in which projects of different types compete by incommensurable criteria, the system creates four specialized landscapes, each optimized for its type of decisions. This is analogous to biological specialization of ecological niches, which increases the overall productivity of the ecosystem.

---

### 3.6. Formal Model of CyberSocium as a Complex Adaptive System

The theoretical constructs introduced in the preceding sections — CSC, PMIP, SES, FRP, IPI — describe individual components and mechanisms. However, CyberSocium as a coherent system possesses properties that cannot be reduced to the sum of its components' properties. To describe these **Emergent Properties**, the apparatus of the theory of Complex Adaptive Systems (CAS) is required [36, 37].

#### 3.6.1. System Components

```
CyberSocium CS = ⟨A, P, R, T, G, Φ⟩

where:
  A = {a₁, ..., aₙ} — set of agents (NFC participants)
    Each agent aᵢ is characterized by a vector:
        aᵢ = ⟨walletᵢ, reputationᵢ, statusᵢ, skillsᵢ, capacityᵢ⟩

        where:
          walletᵢ — cryptographic address identifying the agent in the system
          reputationᵢ ∈ ℝ≥₀ — accumulated reputation, determined by the history
                               of verified activity
          statusᵢ ∈ {Unit, Dev, LeadDev, ArchDev, Core} — status in the hierarchy
                               of responsibility (not authority), determining
                               the level of reward and confirmation powers
          skillsᵢ ⊆ S — subset of the set of competencies S,
                         relevant to the ecosystem's projects
          capacityᵢ ∈ ℝ>₀ — investment capacity of the agent
                             (the resource they are prepared to direct to projects)

      P = {p₁, ..., pₘ} — set of projects (including potential ones)
        Each project pⱼ is characterized by:
        pⱼ = ⟨descriptionⱼ, C(pⱼ), stateⱼ, SIC(pⱼ), AG(pⱼ), tokensⱼ, revenueⱼ⟩

        where:
          descriptionⱼ — formalized description of the project
          C(pⱼ) ∈ ℝ>₀ — estimated cost of realization
          stateⱼ ∈ {Idea, Discussion, Formation, Accumulation,
                    Implementation, Operation, Completion} — current
                    state in the IPI model
          SIC(pⱼ) ⊆ A — Social Investment Circle
          AG(pⱼ) ⊆ SIC(pⱼ) — Active Group
          tokensⱼ — internal project tokens (issued upon
                    transition to Accumulation)
          revenueⱼ(t) — project revenue function of time
                         (defined for state = Operation)

      R = ⟨R_fin, R_int, R_rep, R_inf⟩ — aggregate resource of the system

        where:
          R_fin — financial resource: aggregate Gbr liquidity,
                  funds in pools, accumulated project revenues
          R_int — intellectual resource: aggregate competencies
                  of participants, Σᵢ |skillsᵢ|, accumulated codebase,
                  documentation, research
          R_rep — reputational resource: aggregate reputation
                  of the ecosystem as a whole (distinct from the sum
                  of participants' reputations — an Emergent Property)
          R_inf — infrastructural resource: GyberNet, GyberComputer,
                  DSP, IPFS nodes, deployed smart contracts,
                  liquidity pools

      T = {Gbr} ∪ {token(pⱼ) | pⱼ ∈ P, stateⱼ ∈ {Accumulation,
           Implementation, Operation}} — set of tokens

        Gbr — primary utility token of the ecosystem
        token(pⱼ) — internal token of project pⱼ

        Relation between tokens: each token(pⱼ) is linked to Gbr
        through a liquidity pool created before opening
        trading on DEX. This ensures Axiom 6 (inclusiveness):
        active participants receive tokens of any project
        through Gbr, regardless of personal participation in that project.

      G = ⟨G_social, G_code, G_commerce, G_economic⟩ — governance structure

        G_social — Social DAO: decision-making within the community,
                   organization of social actions
        G_code — Code DAO: management of the platform's codebase
                 through voting on changes
                 to the main repository branch
        G_commerce — Commerce DAO: crowd-investing in business ideas,
                     proposing and realizing commercial
                     projects at the community's expense
        G_economic — Economic DAO: accumulation of social, financial,
                     and economic resources for the realization
                     of the most large-scale projects

        Each G_x is implemented as a smart contract based on Solidity
        and the OpenZeppelin library, with a proposal
        and voting mechanism.

      Φ = {PMIP, FRP, IPI, RepProtocol, StakingProtocol,
           RewardProtocol} — set of interaction protocols

        PMIP — Principle of Minimum Individual Participation
               (Section 3.4.1)
        FRP — Fork Resolution Protocol (Section 3.4.5)
        IPI — "Idea → Project → Implementation" model (Section 3.4.2)
        RepProtocol — Reputation protocol: set of rules determining
                      how an agent's activity is converted into reputation
        StakingProtocol — Staking protocol:
          Gbr staking: staking percentage determined by vote of
                       Economic DAO and adapted to the economic
                       parameters of the ecosystem
          Project token staking: 10 / [total number of issued
                       tokens] % of the specific project's revenue
        RewardProtocol — Reward protocol through UnitManager:
          Frequency: once per month
          Conditions: status verification + activity confirmation
                   through G-Plan
          Amounts by status:
            Unit:    10,000,000 Gbr
            Dev:     100,000,000 Gbr
            LeadDev: 1,000,000,000 Gbr
            ArchDev: 10,000,000,000 Gbr
            Core:    determined by the community
    ```

#### 3.6.2. Emergent Properties

Define the properties that arise from the interaction of CS components but are not inherent to any of them individually. It is precisely these properties that make CyberSocium a qualitatively new system, rather than a simple aggregate of existing technologies.

**E1 — Collective Economic Intelligence.**

The capacity of the system to identify and realize socially significant projects through the SES mechanism. No individual agent possesses sufficient information or resources to determine which projects are most significant for society as a whole. However, the aggregate behaviour of agents — SIC formation, participation voting, FRP — produces an information signal (Social Relevance) that is a collective assessment of the significance of a project. This is a direct analogue of "the wisdom of crowds" in Surowiecki's formulation [40], but realized not through surveys or prediction markets, but through the mechanism of economic participation.

Formally: let utility(p) be the true social utility of project p (unobservable). Then SR(p), determined through |SIC(p)|, is an unbiased estimator of utility(p) provided that: (a) agents hold independent utility assessments; (b) the cost of joining SIC(p) is sufficiently small (ensured by PMIP) so as not to create a barrier to expressing preferences; (c) agents act in their own interests, which on average correlate with social utility. Condition (c) is the strongest assumption; however, Page [41] showed that diversity of assessments in large groups compensates for individual errors and biases, ensuring the quality of a collective decision that exceeds the quality of any individual expert's decision.

**E2 — Antifragility.**

The system is strengthened by stresses. This property is formally proved in Theorem 3 for PMIP, but at the level of the entire CS system it manifests more broadly:

- Economic crises → growing disillusionment with traditional institutions → inflow of participants into NFC → growth of |A| → reduction of TSR → expansion of the range of realizable projects.
- Failure of individual project pᵢ → accumulation of experience (what does not work) → improvement of quality of subsequent projects. Code written for pᵢ remains open and can be reused.
- Attack on an individual node → redistribution of load to remaining nodes + incentive to improve security → more resilient network.

An individual agent is fragile — they may lose motivation, resources, or competencies. An individual project is fragile — it may fail. But the aggregate of agents and projects, connected by CS mechanisms, possesses Antifragility: stresses at the component level strengthen the system at the level of the whole.

**E3 — Self-Scaling.**

Successful projects attract new participants → R increases → TSR decreases for future projects → more large-scale projects are realized → even more participants are attracted. This is a positive feedback loop without central management — a property characteristic of complex adaptive systems [36].

Formally: let N(t) be the number of participants at time t, and let a successful project pⱼ with revenue rⱼ attract ΔN(rⱼ) new participants (where ΔN is an increasing function of r). Then:

```
N(t+1) = N(t) + Σⱼ ΔN(rⱼ(t)) — attrition(t)

where attrition(t) — participant outflow.

When Σⱼ ΔN(rⱼ(t)) > attrition(t), the system grows.

With growth of N(t):
  — the number of proposed ideas grows (more agents → more ideas)
  — TSR decreases for each project (Theorem 1)
  — CC grows for each project
  — the number of realizable projects grows
  — total revenue Σⱼ rⱼ grows
  — ΔN grows

This is a self-sustaining growth cycle, limited only by:
  (a) the finite number of people on the planet;
  (b) the finite capacity of coordination mechanisms
  (this constraint is substantially mitigated by the AI coordinator,
   reducing costs from O(n log n) to O(n) — see Loop 5);
  (c) competition with alternative systems.
```

**E4 — Economic Evolution.**

Through the SES mechanism the system continuously generates, tests, and selects projects, adapting to changes in the external environment without centralized planning. Analogue of biological evolution:

```
Biological evolution          CyberSocium
────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Mutations              →    New ideas proposed by agents
Genetic diversity      →    Diversity of projects and their versions (FRP)
Natural selection      →    Socio-Economic Selection (SES):
                    SR as survival criterion
Fitness                →    Social Relevance
Heredity               →    Reuse of code, knowledge,
                    infrastructure, experience
Habitat                →    Aggregate of interests, needs,
                    and resources of NFC participants
Speciation             →    Branching of projects through FRP
Co-evolution           →    Mutual influence of projects: the infrastructure
                    of one creates opportunities for another
Extinction             →    Completion of projects with SR = 0
                    (without loss of accumulated experience and code)
```

Unlike biological evolution, economic evolution in CS has a Lamarckian component: acquired "traits" (experience, code, infrastructure) are inherited by subsequent projects. This makes evolution in CS substantially faster than biological evolution, and more directed — accumulation of knowledge ensures not random, but cumulative progress.

**E5 — Distributed Resilience.**

Failure of any individual project, node, agent, or even group of agents does not lead to failure of the system as a whole. This property follows from Axiom 1 (decentralization) applied to the scale of the entire system.

Formally: for any subset S' ⊂ A, |S'| < |A|/2, removal of S' does not halt the functioning of CS. This is true because: (a) the infrastructure (GyberNet, DSP) is distributed across nodes controlled by different agents; (b) the code is open — any agent can bring up a new node; (c) data is replicated in the blockchain and IPFS; (d) governance is distributed through DAO — there is no single decision-making point whose removal would paralyze the system.

This is qualitatively different from the resilience of a traditional corporation (dependent on management) or of existing DAOs (dependent on large tokenholders). In CS, resilience does not depend on specific agents — it is a structural property of the architecture.

**E6 — Knowledge Compounding.**

Each realized project pᵢ produces:

(a) Open source code, available to future projects. The code of project pᵢ may be a library, framework, or module that reduces the cost of realizing pᵢ₊₁, ..., pₖ.

(b) Experience and competencies of AG(pᵢ) participants. A developer who participated in realizing pᵢ possesses skills applicable in pᵢ₊₁.

(c) Infrastructure reusable in subsequent projects: deployed nodes, liquidity pools, integrations with external services.

(d) Economic resource through Gbr liquidity: each project that enters the market creates a liquidity pool with Gbr, increasing its aggregate liquidity and utility.

Formally:

```
R(t+1) = R(t) + ΔR(pᵢ)

where ΔR(pᵢ) = ⟨ΔR_fin(pᵢ), ΔR_int(pᵢ), ΔR_rep(pᵢ), ΔR_inf(pᵢ)⟩

Upon successful realization: ||ΔR(pᵢ)|| > cost(pᵢ)

That is: the system creates more aggregate resource
than it consumes for project realization — owing to
the intangible cumulative effect of knowledge.

Moreover, the cumulative effect is superlinear:
  ΔR(pᵢ | p₁, ..., pᵢ₋₁ realized) > ΔR(pᵢ | only pᵢ realized)

That is: each project creates more value in the context
of previously realized projects than in isolation. This is
a direct consequence of the reuse of code, infrastructure, and experience.
```

This effect is one of the most powerful advantages of the CSC over traditional organizational forms. In a traditional corporation, knowledge is proprietary and does not spread beyond its boundaries. In the CSC, all knowledge is open by definition (Axiom 4), allowing the entire ecosystem to benefit from every project. Raymond [20] described this effect for individual software projects; in the CSC it scales to the entire economic system.

#### 3.6.3. System Dynamics — Formalization of Feedback Loops

The behaviour of CyberSocium as a CAS is determined by the interaction of positive (reinforcing) and negative (stabilizing) feedback loops. Donella Meadows in "Thinking in Systems" [39] showed that it is the structure of feedback loops, not the components in isolation, that determines the long-term behaviour of a system.

**Positive feedback loops (reinforcing):**

```
Loop 1 — Growth Loop:

  More participants (↑A)
→ more ideas proposed to the project space
→ more projects proceed through IPI
→ more successful realizations
→ more value created in the ecosystem
→ higher attractiveness of NFC
→ more participants (↑A)

  Speed of loop: determined by the IPI cycle time
  (from idea to first results). Optimizing this
  time is one of the key infrastructure tasks.

Loop 2 — Liquidity Loop:

  More projects enter the market
→ more liquidity pools created with Gbr
→ higher aggregate Gbr liquidity
→ higher value of Gbr as a medium of exchange
→ greater reward for active participants (in real terms)
→ higher motivation for active participation
→ more and better quality projects
→ more projects enter the market

  Key role: the mechanism described in the original documentation —
  "all projects create a liquidity pool with Gbr before opening
  trading on DEX, allowing active community members to
  receive tokens of any project, regardless of personal
  participation in that project" — is the implementation
  of Axiom 6 (inclusiveness) and the key mechanism of this loop.

Loop 3 — Competence Loop:

  More realized projects
→ more experience accumulated in the community (↑R_int)
→ higher quality of new projects
→ higher percentage of successful realizations
→ higher ecosystem reputation (↑R_rep)
→ attraction of more qualified participants
→ even more experience

  This loop explains why the CSC becomes more
  efficient over time, not less — unlike
  bureaucratic organizations, where growth in size is usually
  accompanied by a decline in efficiency (Parkinson's law).
```

**Negative feedback loops (stabilizing):**

```
Loop 4 — Quality Filter:

  Growth in the number of proposed projects
→ decline in average quality of ideas
  (more participants → more inexperienced → more weak ideas)
→ SES mechanism filters out irrelevant projects
  (low SIC → SR = 0 → project does not proceed to Accumulation)
→ only projects with high Social Relevance are realized
→ increase in average quality of realized projects

  This is a stabilizing loop: it prevents ecosystem
  degradation during rapid growth of participant numbers. SES
  acts as an immune system, filtering out non-viable
  projects without a centralized "selection committee."

Loop 5 — Coordination Cost Loop:

  Growth in participant numbers
→ growth in coordination costs
  (more people → harder to reach agreement →
   longer deliberations → more conflicts)
→ incentive to improve coordination tools
  (G-Plan, DAO, automation, AI-assisted governance)
→ reduction of coordination costs per participant
→ possibility of further growth

  This loop describes the key challenge of scaling.
  Brooks in "The Mythical Man-Month" [50] showed that
  in traditional projects, coordination costs grow
  as O(n²), where n is the number of participants. In the CSC
  modular architecture and formalized protocols (PMIP, FRP)
  allow keeping cost growth closer to O(n log n),
  but this estimate requires empirical verification.

Loop 6 — Accountability Loop:

  Growth of aggregate ecosystem resource (↑R)
→ growth of attractiveness for unscrupulous agents
  (fraudsters, free-riders, manipulators)
→ activation of the reputational mechanism:
  unscrupulous behaviour is detected through G-Plan
  and blockchain transparency
→ decline in reputation of the unscrupulous agent
→ exclusion from active roles (AG, implementor)
→ growth of trust in the community
→ growth of willingness to participate in projects
→ growth of resource (↑R)

  Critical: the reputational mechanism works
  only with transparency (A2) and verifiability
  of activity (G-Plan). Without these conditions the loop
  breaks, and the system becomes vulnerable.
```

#### 3.6.4. System Attractor

**Hypothesis 3.6.1 (On the Attractor of CyberSocium).** With sufficient initial conditions (N₀ > N_critical, R₀ > R_critical), the CS system evolves toward a state characterized by:

```
(a) |A| → A_max — coverage of a significant portion of the global population
possessing access to digital technologies
(as of 2025: ~5.4 billion people)

(b) TSR(p) → accessibility for planetary-scale projects
at individual_cost negligibly small for each participant

(c) Decentralization index → max
(no agent or coalition < |A|/3 controls the system)

(d) Gini(R_fin within CS) → min
(a more equal distribution of resources than in any
 existing economic system of comparable scale)

(e) Rate of project realization → optimal
(not maximum, but determined by quality:
 loop 4 ensures balance of speed and quality)

(f) Diversity of projects → maximum
(FRP ensures coexistence of different approaches,
 not convergence to a single one)
```

This state is an Attractor because the positive feedback loops (Loops 1–3) dominate the negative ones (Loops 4–6) provided the axioms A1–A8 are maintained. Dominance does not mean that the negative loops are suppressed — they are active and perform a critically important stabilizing function. But the net effect of the feedback loops is directed toward the described state.

**Theorem 3.6.1 (On the Axiomatic Stability of the Attractor).** Loss of any of the axioms A1–A8 transfers the system to a trajectory toward a different Attractor that does not possess properties (a)–(f). In particular, loss of A8 (cognitive augmentation) leads to degradation of E1 (collective intelligence) during scaling and restoration of the coordination bottleneck (Loop 5). Analysis of degradation is provided in Section 3.6.2.

**Corollary 3.6.1.** Axioms A1–A8 are not idealistic desiderata, but **structural conditions for system stability**. Their violation is not an ethical but a systemic problem, leading to degradation of Emergent Properties E1–E6 and transition to degenerate attractors that reproduce the pathologies of existing organizational forms. Protection of the axioms is not a question of ideology, but a question of engineering: the axioms must be embedded in the system's architecture in such a way that their violation is technically difficult or economically disadvantageous.

**Assessment of critical initial conditions.** Hypothesis 3.6.1 is conditional on the sufficiency of initial conditions. Let us estimate N_critical and R_critical:

```
N_critical — minimum number of participants at which
Loop 1 (growth) begins to dominate outflow:

  Condition: Σⱼ ΔN(rⱼ(t)) > attrition(t)

  Empirical estimate: based on data from existing
  DAO ecosystems (MakerDAO: ~70K active addresses;
  Gitcoin: ~300K unique participants; Ethereum governance:
  ~50K active voters), N_critical is estimated
  in the range 10³–10⁴ active participants.

  At N < N_critical the system is in "manual launch"
  mode — growth requires targeted efforts
  to attract and retain participants.

  At N > N_critical the system transitions to
  self-sustaining growth mode.

R_critical — minimum aggregate resource at which
realization of the first projects is possible, creating
positive feedback:

  R_critical = R_inf_min + R_fin_min

  R_inf_min — minimum infrastructure:
deployed smart contracts,
functioning DSP prototype,
GyberComputer nodes,
Gbr liquidity pools

  R_fin_min — minimum financial resource for
realization of the first cascade projects
(Section 5, Roadmap):
estimated at $50K–$500K
(depending on the complexity of the first projects)
```

---

### 3.7. Cybersocial Industry: From Digital Governance to Real Production

#### 3.7.1. Problem Statement: The Individual Barrier to Industrial Production

Sections 3.1–3.6 described CyberSocium as a system of digital governance, coordination, and financing. However, the theory of CyberSocium is not limited to the digital sphere. This section formulates the next evolutionary step — the **Cybersocial Industry** (CSI): the integration of cybersocial mechanisms into processes of real industrial production.

The central problem addressed by CSI may be called the **individual barrier to industrial production** — the structural impossibility for an individual or small group to realize an idea at industrial scale. This barrier has several dimensions:

```
Individual barrier to industrial production:

  1. Capital barrier:
     Industrial production requires significant
     investment: factory, equipment, raw materials, logistics.
     Entry threshold: $500K — $50M+ (depending on sector).
     Accessible to: large corporations, venture funds.
     Inaccessible to: individuals, small groups, communities.

  2. Competence barrier:
     An industrial product requires competencies that
     no single person can cover: engineering, design,
     materials science, electronics, certification, logistics,
     marketing, legal support, scaling.
     Result: specialization → dependence on the corporation.

  3. Coordination barrier:
     Even if capital and competencies are available,
     coordinating hundreds of participants, suppliers, and contractors
     requires organizational infrastructure, which is
     traditionally provided by corporate hierarchy.

  4. Distribution barrier:
     Producing a product is insufficient: distribution channels,
     logistics networks, and after-sales service are needed.
     These are controlled by retail giants.

  5. Certification barrier:
     Regulatory requirements (CE, FCC, ISO, GOST)
     presuppose an institutional subject: a legal entity,
     financial statements, liability insurance.
```

The aggregate of these barriers creates a fundamental asymmetry: **ideas are born with individuals, but realized only by corporations**. This means that a vast layer of potentially valuable products is never realized — not because they are technically impossible or economically unviable, but because their authors lack access to the institutional infrastructure of production. According to WIPO (2024), fewer than 5% of patents granted to individual inventors reach the stage of industrial production, versus ~35% of corporate patents.

CyberSocium proposes a systemic solution to this problem — and this is precisely what constitutes its most ambitious long-term implication.

#### 3.7.2. The "Prompt → Product" Model: The Cybersocial Production Pipeline

The central idea of CSI is formalized as the **"prompt → product" model** — a pipeline in which the initial impulse (an idea described by a participant) passes through a series of cybersocial mechanisms and is transformed into a real industrial product. Each stage of the pipeline removes one of the barriers described in 3.7.1.

```
Cybersocial production pipeline:

  Prompt                   CSC Mechanism             Result
  (initial impulse)        (barrier removal)         (intermediate product)
  ─────────────────────    ──────────────────────    ─────────────────────────

  "Product idea" ─────────→ Social DAO ──────────→ Validated concept
  (text description,         (discussion,              (community feedback,
   sketch, AI prompt)         voting,                   demand assessment,
                              SIC formation)            AG identification)

  Validated ──────────────→ AiC + Code DAO ──────→ Engineering specification
  concept                    (AI-generated            (3D models, BOM,
                              CAD models,               circuit diagrams,
                              simulation,               material specification,
                              engineer review)          cost estimation)

  Engineering ────────────→ Commerce DAO ────────→ Prototype
  specification              (LP Burn = commitment,    (3D printing, CNC,
                              financing,                lab samples,
                              milestone voting,         functional testing)
                              contracting)

  Prototype ──────────────→ Economic DAO ────────→ Industrial product
                              (large-scale              (serial production,
                              financing                  certification,
                              through PMIP,              distribution,
                              partnership with           after-sales
                              manufacturers,             service)
                              DUNA = legal entity)

  Industrial ─────────────→ SES ─────────────────→ Evolution
  product                    (feedback,                (iterations,
                              Social                    new versions,
                              Relevance,                derivative products,
                              adaptation)               product ecosystem)
```

Key observation: **none of the stages requires the idea's author to independently overcome all five barriers**. Each barrier is removed by the corresponding cybersocial mechanism:

- **Capital barrier** is removed by PMIP: individual_cost(product) = C(product) / |SIC(product)| → 0. If an industrial product costing $5M attracts a SIC of 50,000 participants, individual_cost = $100 — accessible to everyone.
- **Competence barrier** is removed by AiC + NFC: AI models generate engineering specifications from text prompts; the global NFC provides access to expertise in any domain (engineers, designers, materials scientists — all are CSC participants).
- **Coordination barrier** is removed by the four-class DAO taxonomy: Social DAO validates the idea, Code DAO manages technical development, Commerce DAO coordinates production, Economic DAO provides financing.
- **Distribution barrier** is removed by the scale of NFC: 50,000 SIC participants are simultaneously the first 50,000 buyers. Decentralized distribution through the participant network (each DSP node = a potential distribution point).
- **Certification barrier** is removed by DUNA: a Wyoming DUNA legal entity may obtain certificates, conclude contracts with factories, and bear responsibility for the product.

#### 3.7.3. Formalization: from PMIP to PMIP-Industrial

Let us extend the formal PMIP model for industrial production. We introduce **PMIP-Industrial** — an extension of the Principle of Minimum Individual Participation, accounting for the specifics of physical production.

In classical PMIP, the project cost C(p) is predominantly the cost of labour (developer person-hours). In PMIP-Industrial, the project cost is decomposed into components:

```
C_industrial(p) = C_design(p) + C_prototype(p) + C_tooling(p)
                  + C_production(p) + C_certification(p)
                  + C_distribution(p) + C_support(p)

where:
  C_design      — cost of engineering design
                   (reduced by AI: CAD generation, simulation)
  C_prototype   — cost of prototyping
                   (reduced: 3D printing, open-source hardware)
  C_tooling     — cost of tooling for serial production
                   (injection moulds, dies, PCB production)
  C_production  — cost of serial production
                   (raw materials, energy, labour, depreciation)
  C_certification — cost of certification
                   (CE, FCC, ISO; DUNA bears legal costs)
  C_distribution — cost of distribution
                   (logistics, marketing, retail)
  C_support     — cost of after-sales service
                   (warranty, repair, updates)

Application of PMIP:

  individual_cost_industrial(p) = C_industrial(p) / |SIC(p)|

Example:
  Product: open-source laptop
  C_design      = $200K   (AI-generated schematics + engineer review)
  C_prototype   = $50K    (10 prototypes, testing)
  C_tooling     = $500K   (injection moulds for case, PCB)
  C_production  = $2M     (first batch: 10,000 units)
  C_certification = $100K (FCC, CE, RoHS)
  C_distribution = $300K  (logistics, warehouses)
  C_support     = $100K   (warranty fund, documentation)
  ──────────────────────────────
  C_industrial  = $3.25M

  If |SIC| = 50,000 participants:
    individual_cost = $3.25M / 50,000 = $65

  If |SIC| = 200,000 participants:
    individual_cost = $3.25M / 200,000 = $16.25

  Conclusion: an industrial product costing $3.25M
  becomes accessible to each participant for $16–$65
  — without bank loans, venture capital,
  or corporate hierarchy.
```

**Theorem 3.7.1 (Universal Industrial Realizability).** For any industrial product p with finite cost C_industrial(p) < ∞, there exists a threshold SIC* such that for |SIC(p)| ≥ SIC*, individual_cost_industrial(p) ≤ ε for any given ε > 0.

*Proof* is analogous to the Theorem of Universal Realizability of PMIP (Section 3.4.3): SIC* = ⌈C_industrial(p) / ε⌉. For |SIC| ≥ SIC* we have individual_cost = C_industrial / |SIC| ≤ C_industrial / SIC* = ε. ∎

Practical corollary: **"Millions are Factories."** If PMIP in the digital sphere yields "millions are billionaires" (any digital project is realizable with a sufficient NFC), then PMIP-Industrial yields: any industrial product is realizable with a sufficient NFC. The difference lies only in the absolute value of SIC*: for a digital product SIC* ~ 100–10,000; for an industrial product SIC* ~ 10,000–1,000,000. But with a global NFC ≥ 10M, both ranges are achievable.

#### 3.7.4. The Role of AI in the Cybersocial Production Pipeline

Axiom A8 (Cognitive Augmentation) acquires particular significance in the context of industrial production. If in the digital sphere AI accelerates development and reduces cognitive load, then in the industrial sphere AI **fundamentally changes the architecture of the production process**:

**AI as engineering translator (prompt → specification).** Modern AI models (GPT-4, Claude, Gemini) are capable of generating structured engineering descriptions from text prompts. Specialized models (AutoCAD AI, Fusion 360 Generative Design, SolidWorks xDesign) generate 3D models from text and graphic specifications. In the context of CSI this means: a participant without engineering education can describe a product idea as a text prompt, and the AI system AiC (Contour 2) translates this prompt into an engineering specification, ready for review by professional engineers (the project's AG).

```
Example of "prompt → specification" translation:

  Participant's prompt:
    "A portable device for purifying water
     from contaminants, powered by solar energy,
     the size of a thermos, costing up to $50 in serial
     production. Target audience: regions without
     access to clean water."

  AI generation (AiC Contour 2):
    → Requirements analysis: portability, autonomy,
      cost, target purification characteristics
    → Review of existing solutions: UV purification, filtration,
      reverse osmosis, electrochemical treatment
    → Technology recommendation: UV-C LED + activated carbon filter
      (optimal efficiency/cost ratio)
    → Generation of 3D housing model (parametric)
    → BOM (Bill of Materials): ~15 components, $22 cost price
    → Electrical schematic: solar panel → Li-ion → UV-C LED
    → Performance calculation: 2L/hour at irradiance >500 W/m²
    → Preliminary certification: NSF/ANSI 55 (UV), IP67
    → Serial cost estimate: $38 at a run of 10,000

  Result: complete engineering specification,
  ready for review by AG (engineers, materials scientists,
  certification specialists)
```

**AI as production process optimizer.** At the scaling stage (Commerce DAO → Economic DAO), AI models optimize: selection of production partners (matching factories by competencies, capacities, geography); logistics routes; production parameters (tolerances, yield rates, energy efficiency); pricing accounting for production, distribution, and margin costs.

**AI as generator of derivative products.** After successful production of the first product, AI models analyse feedback (SES) and generate proposals for: modifications (size, colour, material); accessories; compatible products; products for adjacent markets. This launches a SES cycle at the level of a product line, not an individual product.

#### 3.7.5. Cybersocial Industry and the Global Economy

CSI represents not merely a technical possibility, but a potential structural transformation of the global economy. If CyberSocium in the digital sphere competes with technology corporations (Google, Meta, Apple), then CSI competes with industrial giants (Foxconn, Samsung, Siemens) — not by creating an alternative industrial infrastructure, but by **democratizing access to the existing one**.

```
Traditional model                 Cybersocial model
of industrial production          of industrial production (CSI)
─────────────────────────         ─────────────────────────────────

Idea → Corporation:               Idea → Prompt:
  Inventor sells                    Participant describes idea
  patent to corporation             in DSP; AI translates
  (or founds startup                into specification
  with venture financing)

Development → R&D department:     Development → AiC + AG:
  Company engineers                 AI generates models;
  design product                    engineers from NFC review
  (closed process)                  (open process)

Production → Factory:             Production → Contract Manufacturing:
  Corporation builds/rents          Commerce DAO contracts
  production facilities             existing factories
  (capex = $10M+)                   (DUNA = legal entity; payment from
                                     PMIP fund)

Distribution → Retail:            Distribution → NFC:
  Corporation concludes             SIC(p) = first buyers;
  agreements with retailers         decentralized logistics
  (Walmart, Amazon, etc.)           through DUNA partners

Profit → Shareholders:            Revenue → CSC:
  Profit distributed                Revenue distributed:
  among shareholders                10% → CSC (MacroeconomicDAO)
  proportional to capital           AG Reward → AG participants
                                     LP Burn → permanent liquidity
                                     Buyback → GBR ecosystem

Control → Board of directors:     Control → DAO:
  Decisions made by                 Decisions made by
  10–15 people                      all SIC participants through
  on behalf of shareholders         four-class DAO system

Scale → Limited by                Scale → Limited only by
  corporation capital:               NFC size:
  $10M → 1 factory                  PMIP: $10M / 100K = $100/person
```

Three fundamental advantages of CSI over the traditional model:

**1. Democratization of initiative.** In the traditional model, the right to initiate an industrial product belongs to those who control capital (corporations, VC). In CSI, the right of initiative belongs to every NFC participant — it suffices to describe an idea in a prompt and attract a SIC. This removes the filter "the idea must be approved by an investor" and replaces it with the filter "the idea must be socially relevant" (SR(p) = 1).

**2. Alignment of incentives.** In the traditional model, there is a conflict between: shareholders (maximizing profit), workers (maximizing wages), consumers (minimizing price), society (minimizing externalities). In CSI, these roles are combined: a SIC participant is simultaneously an investor (contributes individual_cost), a developer/worker (participates in AG), a consumer (receives the product), and a manager (votes in DAO). The conflict of interest is structurally eliminated.

**3. Adaptability through SES.** A traditional corporation is committed to its products (sunk cost fallacy, organizational inertia). CSI through the SES mechanism ensures evolutionary selection: products with high SR(p) receive more resources; products with low SR(p) receive less. There is no corporate policy protecting unprofitable product lines. There are no "sacred cows." Only Social Relevance determines a product's survival.

#### 3.7.6. Precedents and Analogues

CSI does not arise in a vacuum. Precedents exist that demonstrate individual elements of the model:

**Crowdfunding (Kickstarter, Indiegogo).** Removes the capital barrier, but does not remove the competence, coordination, or certification barriers. The project author must independently organize production. According to Kickstarter data (2024), only 35% of successfully funded hardware projects deliver the product on time; 9% never deliver. CSI differs: the DAO structure provides coordination; AiC provides competencies; DUNA provides certification.

**Open-Source Hardware (Arduino, RISC-V, Open Source Ecology).** Removes the competence barrier through open specifications, but does not remove the capital or coordination barriers. Production remains the responsibility of the individual or a commercial company. CSI differs: PMIP provides financing; Commerce DAO coordinates production.

**Platform Cooperativism (Mondragon, Fairphone).** Removes the incentive alignment problem through cooperative ownership, but is limited geographically and in scale. Fairphone — perhaps the closest analogue: an ethical smartphone produced with regard for the interests of all stakeholders. But Fairphone remains a traditional company (BV in the Netherlands), managed by managers rather than by a community. CSI differs: global scale; DAO governance; AI augmentation.

**Contract Manufacturing (Foxconn, Jabil).** The contract manufacturing industry demonstrates that physical ownership of a factory is not a necessary condition for production: Apple does not own a single factory. CSI uses the same model: DUNA contracts production through Commerce DAO, without owning production facilities. The difference: in the traditional model, contract manufacturing is accessible only to large corporations (MOQ = 100K+ units); CSI through PMIP aggregates demand and reaches minimum order volumes through the scale of NFC.

#### 3.7.7. Formal Conditions of CSI Realizability

Cybersocial Industry is realizable upon satisfaction of four conditions:

**Condition 1: Sufficient NFC.** |NFC| ≥ 10⁵ (one hundred thousand active participants) ensures: sufficient SIC size for any product with C_industrial ≤ $10M; sufficient diversity of competencies (engineers, designers, lawyers, marketers); sufficient market for the first batch. At |NFC| ≥ 10⁶ (one million), products with C_industrial ≤ $100M — the scale of the automotive industry — become realizable.

**Condition 2: Maturity of AiC.** AI models of Contour 2 must ensure: generation of engineering specifications from text prompts with accuracy sufficient for review (rather than complete design from scratch); optimization of production parameters; market analysis and pricing. Current state of AI (2026): partially achievable for simple products; full realization — a horizon of 3–5 years.

**Condition 3: Functioning DAO infrastructure.** The four-class DAO system must ensure: coordination of dozens of parallel production projects; milestone voting with integration of production metrics; management of financial flows (PMIP → contract manufacturing → distribution → revenue → CSC). Current state: prototype (Phase I); full realization — Phase III.

**Condition 4: Legal infrastructure.** DUNA must be capable of: concluding contracts with manufacturing factories; obtaining industrial certificates; bearing responsibility for the product before consumers; providing warranty and after-sales service. Current state: DUNA registered; expansion of functions — Phase II–III.

#### 3.7.8. Vision: from the Digital Economy to the Cybersocial Industry

If CyberSocium in the digital sphere answers the question "how to organize collective governance?", then CSI answers the question "how to organize collective production?" Together they form a complete model of the cybersocial economy — from idea to physical product, from prompt to industrial batch, from individual to global ecosystem.

A prompt is not merely a text input. A prompt is a **formalized intention** which, passing through cybersocial mechanisms (SIC formation, DAO voting, AI translation, PMIP financing, contract manufacturing), acquires material form. In this lies the fundamental difference of CSI from all preceding models: not capital produces a product, but Social Relevance. Capital (PMIP) is merely a consequence of relevance (SR(p) = 1), not its cause.

This is an inversion of the traditional economic logic: not "there is capital → we produce a product → we seek a buyer," but "there is relevance → SIC forms → capital = consequence → product = result of collective action." Industrial production ceases to be a privilege of those who control capital, and becomes a possibility for anyone whose idea is socially relevant.

---
## 4. Architecture of GyberExperiment: Technical Specification

### 4.1. From Theory to Implementation: A Correspondence Cartography

Sections 1–3 established the theoretical foundation: they defined the problem, formulated the axioms, introduced the formal models CSC, PMIP, SES, FRP, described CyberSocium as a complex adaptive system, and articulated the concept of cyber-social industry. The present section translates theory into architecture — describing how each theoretical construct is realised technically within the GyberExperiment framework.

![Correspondence of theory and implementation](diagrams/architecture/theory_implementation_map.svg)

*Figure 4.2: Correspondence cartography between the theoretical concepts of CyberSocium (left column) and their technical realisation in GyberExperiment (right column). Each axiom (A1–A7), mechanism (PMIP, SES, IPI), and system component (GBR, Agents, Resources) has an explicit technical embodiment in the architecture.*

**High-level diagram:**

```
        ┌──────────────────────────────┐
        │      MacroeconomicDAO         │
        │  ┌────────┐  ┌────────┐      │
        │  │Social  │  │ Code   │      │
        │  │  DAO   │  │  DAO   │      │
        │  └────────┘  └────────┘      │
        │  ┌────────┐  ┌────────┐      │
        │  │Commerce│  │Economic│      │
        │  │  DAO   │  │  DAO   │      │
        │  └────────┘  └────────┘      │
        └──────────────┬───────────────┘
                      │ governance
        ┌─────────────▼────────────────┐
        │     GyberNet Blockchain       │
        │  (Security & Transparency)    │
        └─────────────┬────────────────┘
                      │ trust layer
  ┌───────────────────────┼───────────────────────┐
  │                       │                       │
┌─────────▼───────────┐ ┌────────▼─────────┐ ┌───────────▼──────────┐
│  Decentralised       │ │  GyberComputer   │ │  Gbr Token Economy   │
│  Social Platform     │ │  (Distributed    │ │                      │
│                      │ │   Computing)     │ │ ┌──────────────────┐ │
│ ┌──────────────────┐ │ │                  │ │ │ UnitManager      │ │
│ │ Project Spaces   │ │ │ ┌──────────────┐ │ │ │ (BSC, Solidity)  │ │
│ │ Chat / Voice     │ │ │ │ AI Models    │ │ │ ├──────────────────┤ │
│ │ Video Hosting    │ │ │ │ (AiC Project)│ │ │ │ Project Liquidity│ │
│ │ File Sharing     │ │ │ │ Compute      │ │ │ │ Pools            │ │
│ │ G-Plan (Tasks)   │ │ │ │ Services     │ │ │ │ Staking Contracts│ │
│ │ Reputation       │ │ │ └──────────────┘ │ │ │ Reputation Oracle│ │
│ │ Shared Spaces    │ │ │                  │ │ └──────────────────┘ │
│ └──────────────────┘ │ │                  │ │                      │
└──────────────────────┘ └──────────────────┘ └──────────────────────┘
  │                       │                       │
  └───────────────────────┼───────────────────────┘
                      │ data layer
        ┌─────────────▼────────────────┐
        │           IPFS               │
        │  (Distributed File Storage)   │
        │  + Community Pinning Nodes     │
        └──────────────────────────────┘
```

> 📊 **Professional version:** [Open SVG](diagrams/architecture/system_architecture.svg)

*Figure 4.1: Complete architecture of the GyberExperiment ecosystem, showing four layers: (1) Governance Layer (MacroeconomicDAO with four DAO classes), (2) Trust Layer (GyberNet Blockchain), (3) Application Layer (DSP, GyberComputer, GBR tokenomics), and (4) Data Layer (IPFS with community pinning nodes).*

#### 4.1.1. Formal Correspondence Matrix: Theory → Implementation

Every theoretical construct introduced in Sections 1–3 must have an explicit, verifiable technical embodiment. The complete correspondence matrix below links abstract concepts to concrete contracts, protocols, and system components.

**Axioms → technical mechanisms:**

| Axiom | Formulation | Technical realisation | Contract / Module | Verification |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| A1 (Decentralisation) | Absence of a single point of control | UUPS proxy + Gnosis Safe (M-of-N) + Timelock | `UnitManager.sol`, `Governor.sol` | Gnosis Safe: ≥3-of-5 signers |
| A2 (Transparency) | All economic decisions on-chain | BSC → GyberNet: all votes, transactions, statuses recorded on blockchain | `Governor.sol`, BSC explorer | Verification via Etherscan/BscScan |
| A3 (Data sovereignty) | User = sole data owner | 3-layer encryption in DSP; DID standard; Verifiable Credentials | DSP encryption layer, DDP (IPFS) | Proof: server has no decryption keys |
| A4 (Extensibility) | Unlimited modularity | SAPP standard (Socio-App Protocol Package); Plugin API in DSP | DSP Module Registry | Any participant may publish a module |
| A5 (Meritocratic fairness) | Weight proportional to contribution | Hybrid Voting Power: VP = √(GBR_staked) × ActivityMultiplier × StatusBonus | `GBRVotingWrapper.sol` | Activity Score 0–100, transparent formula |
| A6 (Inclusivity) | Minimal entry threshold | UnitManager: free registration; PMIP: individual_cost → 0 | `UnitManager.sol` | MemberStatus.Unit = base level |
| A7 (Self-governance) | All rules modifiable through DAO | OpenZeppelin Governor + TimelockController | `Governor.sol`, `TimelockController.sol` | 4 DAO classes with parameters |
| A8 (Cognitive augmentation) | AI augments but does not replace | AiC: three circuits (internal, research, commercial); soulbound audit tokens | AiC Agent Framework | Division principle: AI ⊂ {recommends, decides} |

**Mechanisms → technical components:**

| Mechanism | Formal model | Technical realisation | Parameters |
|---|---|---|---|
| PMIP | individual_cost(p) = C(p) / \|SIC(p)\| → 0 | LP Burn: participant burns LP tokens to initiate a project; UnitManager distributes rewards | burn_threshold = $1,000 in GBR/USDT LP |
| SES | Fitness f(p) = w₁·SR(p) + w₂·EV(p) - w₃·C(p) | Commerce DAO + Economic DAO voting; cadCAD parameter simulation | w₁ = 0.4, w₂ = 0.4, w₃ = 0.2 (calibrable) |
| IPI (Idea→Project→Implementation) | Finite automaton: Idea → Project → Implementation | Social DAO voting (Idea→Project); Commerce DAO management (Project→Impl) | Idea: quorum 5%, 72h; Project: quorum 15% |
| FRP (Fork Resolution) | Protocol for resolving conflicting bifurcations | On-chain Dispute Resolution: escrow, arbitration, fork-voting | Cooling period: 7 days; arbitration quorum: 30% |
| CAS (Complex Adaptive System) | Agents + environment + rules → emergence | All components together: DSP (agents), GyberNet (environment), MacroDAO (rules) | Monitoring via cadCAD + Dune Analytics |

**System properties → observable metrics:**

| Property (Section 3.6) | Formal definition | Metric | Measurement instrument |
|---|---|---|---|
| Self-organisation | Spontaneous formation of SIC without external coordination | Number of SIC formed organically (without promotion) | G-Plan analytics |
| Emergent governance | Quality of DAO decisions > average individual participant decision | Condorcet indicator: P(DAO_correct) > P(individual_correct) | Retrospective decision analysis |
| Adaptability | Response time to external shocks | Time from problem detection to adoption of a corrective DAO proposal | Timestamped on-chain events |
| Antifragility | System improves under stress | Growth in active participants and TVL following crisis events | Dune Analytics dashboards |
| Scalable coordination | O(n) → O(1) via AI | Cognitive load per participant does not grow with increasing N | Decision time × quality |

#### 4.1.2. Architectural Principles of Implementation

The translation of theory into code is governed by several fundamental architectural principles that ensure systemic integrity.
