SAPP as a Commerce DAO project: budget ~50,000 GBR, SIC ~500 participants, AG = 12 developers + 3 designers, implementation period 12 months. Revenue model: application marketplace with 15% commission on sales.

#### 6.5.3. PowerSwapMeta

PowerSwapMeta is a DEX aggregator with meta-transaction support that optimises token exchange within the GyberExperiment ecosystem. The core innovation: users do not pay for gas directly — the fee is paid from the GBR liquidity pool, which lowers the barrier to entry (A6, inclusivity).

```
PowerSwapMeta Architecture:

  Components:
    — Route Optimizer: algorithm for finding the optimal
      exchange route across multiple pools
      (split routes, multi-hop)
    — Meta-Transaction Relayer: EIP-2771-compatible
      relayer paying gas on behalf of the user
    — Cross-Chain Bridge: secure exchange between
      BSC ↔ Ethereum ↔ Polygon
      (lock-mint with M-of-N threshold signatures)
    — Gas Station Network: GBR pool for gas payment
      (replenished from fees)

  Meta-transactions:
    1. User signs exchange intent (off-chain)
    2. Relayer pays gas from Gas Station Pool
    3. Exchange is executed on-chain
    4. Fee (0.05% of volume) covers gas + profit

  AI Route Optimizer (AiC Circuit 3):
    — ML model predicts the optimal route
      based on current liquidity, volatility,
      gas price, and historical patterns
    — Slippage prediction: AI warns
      of potential slippage before execution
    — MEV protection: detection of sandwich attacks
      and front-running, automatic selection
      of a protected route (private mempool)

  Supported pools:
    — PancakeSwap V2/V3 (BSC)
    — Uniswap V2/V3 (Ethereum, Polygon)
    — SushiSwap (multichain)
    — Ecosystem project pools (via LQD)
```

PowerSwapMeta as a Commerce DAO project: budget ~30,000 GBR, SIC ~300 participants, AG = 8 developers, period 9 months. Revenue model: 0.05% of exchange volume; projected annual revenue at $100M volume = $50,000 in GBR equivalent.

#### 6.5.4. Contact

Contact is a decentralised identity and contact management system for ecosystem participants. Contact implements Axiom A3 (data sovereignty) for the domain of digital identity: the user exercises full control over their identity and chooses what data to disclose.

```
Contact Architecture:

  DID (Decentralized Identifier):
    — Format: did:gybernaty:<wallet_address>
    — Storage: on-chain (GyberNet registry)
    — Control: only the wallet owner may
      update/revoke the DID

  Verifiable Credentials (VC):
    — MemberStatus VC: confirmation of current status
      (issued by UnitManager, signed on-chain)
    — Skill VC: confirmation of competencies
      (issued by Code DAO after peer review)
    — Project VC: confirmation of project participation
      (issued by G-Plan upon milestone completion)
    — AI Audit VC: confirmation of AI audit passage
      (issued by AiC, soulbound)

  Zero-Knowledge Proofs:
    — ZK-proof of membership: proof that
      "I am an NFC participant with status ≥ Dev"
      without disclosing wallet or name
    — ZK-proof of activity: proof that
      "my Activity Score ≥ 60"
      without disclosing the exact value
    — ZK-proof of age: proof that
      "I have been a participant for more than 6 months"
      without disclosing the registration date
    — Technology: Semaphore (ZK-SNARK group membership)

  Portable identity:
    — One DID for all ecosystem projects
    — Cross-platform: DSP, SAPP modules, GyberNet, external dApps
    — Selective disclosure: the user chooses
      which VC to present for each interaction
    — Revocation: the user may revoke
      any issued VCs at any time

  AI integration:
    — Sybil detection: GNN analyses the identity graph,
      identifying clusters of fake accounts
    — Anomaly detection: detection of suspicious
      patterns (mass registration, coordinated
      activity, wash trading)
    — Reputation scoring: AI aggregates VC, Activity Score,
      voting history to form
      a comprehensive reputation score
```

Contact as a Commerce DAO project: budget ~20,000 GBR, SIC ~250 participants, AG = 6 developers + 2 cryptographers, period 8 months. Revenue model: basic DID free, advanced VC services (enterprise verification, cross-chain bridging) — paid.

#### 6.5.5. Interrelationship of Auxiliary Projects

The four auxiliary projects form an integrated infrastructure stack in which each component reinforces the others:

```
Integration matrix of auxiliary projects:

  LQD ←→ PowerSwapMeta:
    — LQD creates pools, PowerSwapMeta aggregates them
    — PowerSwapMeta routes traffic to LQD pools
    — Shared AI Route Optimizer optimises both

  SAPP ←→ Contact:
    — SAPP modules use Contact DID for authorisation
    — Contact provides VC for SAPP permissions
    — ZK-proofs ensure privacy in SAPP

  LQD ←→ Contact:
    — Contact DID identifies LP providers
    — Activity VC from LP → Activity Score increase

  SAPP ←→ PowerSwapMeta:
    — SAPP modules may embed token exchange
    — Meta-transactions lower the barrier for SAPP

  All four ←→ DSP:
    — DSP is the operating environment for all projects
    — Unified UX: users interact via DSP
    — G-Plan tracks tasks of all four projects
```

The aggregate value of these four projects exceeds the sum of their individual values — a classic manifestation of the emergent property of a complex adaptive system (section 3.6, property E2). LQD without PowerSwapMeta creates pools with limited traffic; PowerSwapMeta without LQD has no project pools to aggregate; SAPP without Contact cannot provide private authorisation; Contact without SAPP has limited application. Together they form a complete "financial-social stack" for the CyberSocium ecosystem.

### 6.6 Portfolio Validation and Ecosystem Integration

All components of the applied ecosystem are connected by a unified economic circuit:

```
Ecosystem economic circuit:

  Participant
    │
    ├─► G-Plan: completes tasks → activity verified
    │     │
    │     └─► UnitManager: receives GBR reward
    │           │
    │           └─► GBR token: used for
    │                 │
    │                 ├─► DAO voting
    │                 ├─► Staking (passive income)
    │                 ├─► Investment in projects (Commerce DAO)
    │                 └─► Access to services (GyberComputer, AiC)
    │
    ├─► DSP: participates in social life
    │     │
    │     └─► Reputation: grows with activity → increases
    │           voting weight in DAO
    │
    └─► IPI projects: invests / works
          │
          └─► Project tokens → LP with GBR → DEX
                │
                └─► Revenue → distribution via
                      smart contracts
```

This integration ensures the realisation of all eight axioms:

| Axiom | Implementation in the ecosystem |
|---|---|
| A1 (Decentralisation) | P2P architecture of DSP, distributed GyberComputer, multisig governance |
| A2 (Transparency) | On-chain transactions in GyberNet, open code (AGPL-3.0) |
| A3 (Data sovereignty) | DID identification, IPFS storage, E2E encryption |
| A4 (Extensibility) | Modular architecture, SAPP protocol, open APIs |
| A5 (Meritocratic fairness) | UnitManager, G-Plan verification, reputation system |
| A6 (Inclusivity) | PMIP (minimum participation threshold), multilingualism, cross-chain |
| A7 (Self-governance) | Four-class DAO taxonomy, Governor contracts |
| A8 (Cognitive augmentation) | AiC (three circuits), AI audit in Code DAO, AI Treasury Advisor in Economic DAO, AI proposal summarisation, federated learning on GyberComputer |

### 6.7 Deployment Architecture and Integration Testing

The GyberExperiment ecosystem consists of five software modules, each performing a specialised function. Their integration ensures the realisation of the full cycle of the cybersocial corporation.

#### 6.7.1 Five Ecosystem Modules

```
GyberExperiment Modular Architecture:

  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │              DSP Client (Frontend)                │
  │  Next.js 15, React, TypeScript                    │
  │  Role: DSP user interface                         │
  │  Interacts with: GPROD (API), CONTRACTS (Web3)    │
  └────────────────────┬─────────────────────────────┘
                       │ REST/GraphQL
  ┌────────────────────▼─────────────────────────────┐
  │                GPROD (Backend)                     │
  │  NestJS 11, Prisma 6, PostgreSQL                  │
  │  Role: blockchain middleware, API gateway,         │
  │        business logic coordination                 │
  │  Interacts with: DSP Client (API), CONTRACTS (ethers),│
  │        Supabase (data), AiC (AI services)         │
  └────────────────────┬─────────────────────────────┘
                       │ ethers.js / viem
  ┌────────────────────▼─────────────────────────────┐
  │              CONTRACTS (Blockchain)                │
  │  Solidity ^0.8.20, OpenZeppelin, BSC              │
  │  Role: on-chain logic — UnitManager, Governor,    │
  │        Timelock, GBRVotingWrapper, LP Burn         │
  │  Interacts with: GPROD (events/tx), DSP Client (Web3)│
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │              Supabase (Database)                   │
  │  Self-hosted PostgreSQL + Auth + Realtime          │
  │  Role: off-chain data storage — profiles,          │
  │        G-Plan tasks, project metadata,             │
  │        blockchain event cache                      │
  │  Interacts with: GPROD (Prisma ORM)               │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  ┌──────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │              DDP (Phase 2)                         │
  │  IPFS + ContentRegistry + ACL + P2P messaging     │
  │  Role: decentralised content storage,             │
  │        P2P communication, media                   │
  │  Interacts with: DSP Client, GPROD (index),       │
  │        CONTRACTS (ACL on-chain)                   │
  └──────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

#### 6.7.2 The Principle of "Wallet = Universal Key"

A single principle connects all modules: **the wallet address is the primary key** for identifying a participant across all system components.

```
Wallet Address as Primary Key:

  DSP Client: user.walletAddress → profile, UI state
  GPROD:     User.walletAddress → API authorisation,
             blockchain queries
  CONTRACTS: msg.sender → MemberStatus, Activity Score,
             reward claims, DAO voting
  Supabase:  users.wallet_address → off-chain data,
             G-Plan tasks, project metadata
  DDP:       wallet → DID → IPFS storage,
             ACL access control

  Synchronisation:
    CONTRACTS (source of truth for on-chain data)
      → GPROD (indexes events, caches)
        → DSP Client (displays to user)
    Supabase (source of truth for off-chain data)
      → GPROD (ORM access)
        → DSP Client (displays to user)
```

#### 6.7.3 Containerisation and DevOps Pipeline

Each ecosystem module is containerised using Docker and orchestrated via Docker Compose (for development and staging) and Kubernetes (for production). This ensures environment reproducibility and simplifies deployment.

```
Docker architecture (docker-compose.yml):

  services:
    dsp-client:
      build: ./DSP
      ports: ["3000:3000"]
      environment:
        - NEXT_PUBLIC_GPROD_URL=http://gprod:4000
        - NEXT_PUBLIC_CHAIN_ID=97  # BSC Testnet
        - NEXT_PUBLIC_WALLETCONNECT_PROJECT_ID=${WC_ID}
      depends_on: [gprod]
      healthcheck:
        test: ["CMD", "curl", "-f", "http://localhost:3000/api/health"]
        interval: 30s
        timeout: 10s
        retries: 3

    gprod:
      build: ./GPROD
      ports: ["4000:4000"]
      environment:
        - DATABASE_URL=postgresql://gyber:${DB_PASS}@supabase-db:5432/gybernaty
        - BSC_RPC_URL=${BSC_RPC}
        - UNIT_MANAGER_ADDRESS=${UM_ADDR}
        - GOVERNOR_ADDRESS=${GOV_ADDR}
        - JWT_SECRET=${JWT_SECRET}
      depends_on: [supabase-db, redis]

    supabase-db:
      image: supabase/postgres:15.1.1.78
      volumes: ["db-data:/var/lib/postgresql/data"]
      environment:
        - POSTGRES_PASSWORD=${DB_PASS}
        - POSTGRES_DB=gybernaty

    redis:
      image: redis:7-alpine
      volumes: ["redis-data:/data"]
      healthcheck:
        test: ["CMD", "redis-cli", "ping"]

    hardhat-node:
      build: ./CONTRACTS
      command: npx hardhat node --fork ${BSC_RPC}
      ports: ["8545:8545"]
      profiles: ["dev"]  # Development only

  volumes:
    db-data:
    redis-data:
```

**CI/CD pipeline (GitHub Actions):**

Each module has its own CI/CD pipeline, activated on push and pull request. Pipelines are unified via a monorepo strategy with path-based filters.

```
CONTRACTS pipeline (on push to contracts/**):

  1. checkout → setup-node → install dependencies
  2. compile: npx hardhat compile
  3. lint: npx solhint 'contracts/**/*.sol'
  4. test: npx hardhat test --network hardhat
  5. coverage: npx hardhat coverage → ≥90%
  6. static analysis: Slither → vulnerability report
  7. gas report: npx hardhat test --gas
  8. [on merge to main] deploy to BSC Testnet
  9. [on release tag] deploy to BSC Mainnet (manual approval)

GPROD pipeline (on push to GPROD/**):

  1. checkout → setup-node → install dependencies
  2. lint: eslint + prettier check
  3. type check: tsc --noEmit
  4. test: jest --coverage → ≥80%
  5. build: nest build
  6. docker build → push to registry
  7. [on merge to main] deploy to staging
  8. integration test: E2E tests against staging

DSP Client pipeline (on push to DSP/**):

  1. checkout → setup-node → install dependencies
  2. lint: eslint + prettier check
  3. type check: tsc --noEmit
  4. test: vitest --coverage → ≥80%
  5. build: next build
  6. lighthouse CI: performance ≥90, accessibility ≥95
  7. docker build → push to registry
  8. [on merge to main] deploy to Vercel staging
  9. E2E: Playwright against staging
```

All secrets (private keys, API keys, database passwords) are stored in GitHub Secrets and HashiCorp Vault. Private keys for smart contract deployment are held in hardware keys (Ledger), connected via Gnosis Safe for production deploys.

#### 6.7.4 Integration Testing Strategy

Given the critical role of smart contracts (transaction irreversibility, financial management), integration testing follows a multi-level strategy:

```
Testing levels:

  Level 1: Unit Tests (each module separately)
    — CONTRACTS: Hardhat/Foundry tests for each
      contract (UnitManager, Governor, Timelock)
    — GPROD: Jest tests for each NestJS module
    — DSP Client: Vitest + React Testing Library
    — Coverage: ≥90% for CONTRACTS, ≥80% for GPROD/DSP Client

  Level 2: Integration Tests (pairwise integration)
    — GPROD ↔ CONTRACTS: testing on Hardhat fork
      of BSC mainnet (realistic state)
    — DSP Client ↔ GPROD: E2E tests (Playwright/Cypress)
    — GPROD ↔ Supabase: migrations, transactional integrity

  Level 3: End-to-End Scenarios
    — Full cycle: registration → task completion
      → verification → claimReward() → voting
    — Test environment: BSC Testnet + Supabase staging
    — Automation: GitHub Actions CI/CD

  Level 4: Security Testing
    — AI audit: Slither + Mythril + Octane Security
    — Formal verification: UnitManager invariants
      (balance ≥ Σ pending_rewards)
    — Fuzzing: Echidna/Medusa for edge cases
    — Penetration testing: external auditors

  Level 5: Staging / Canary Deployment
    — BSC Testnet: full replica of production environment
    — Canary: 5% of traffic on new version
    — Rollback: automatic upon detection
      of anomalies (AI monitoring)
```

#### 6.7.5 Monitoring and Operational Resilience

```
Ecosystem operational monitoring:

  Infrastructure:
    — Prometheus + Grafana: metrics for all modules
    — Uptime: SLA ≥99.9% for GPROD and DSP
    — Alerts: PagerDuty/Opsgenie for Core participants

  Blockchain:
    — Dune Analytics dashboard:
      TVL, volume, active wallets, reward distributions
    — AI anomaly detection:
      anomalous transaction patterns → alert
    — Fund Monitoring: remaining UnitManager balance,
      projected depletion at current payout rate

  Application:
    — Sentry: error tracking for DSP Client and GPROD
    — Custom metrics: DAU, MAU, G-Plan tasks,
      DAO proposals, votes
    — AI agent: weekly ecosystem status report
      for Economic DAO

  Security:
    — Chainalysis monitoring: suspicious
      addresses, sanctions lists
    — Rate limiting: DDoS protection
    — WAF: Web Application Firewall for DSP Client/GPROD
    — Gnosis Safe: signature monitoring, alerts
      on unexpected transactions
```

### 6.8 Open Development Model and Community Governance

The GyberExperiment ecosystem is developed as an open-source project under the AGPL-3.0 licence (code) and CC-BY-4.0 (documentation and research). This model is not merely a technical decision but a fundamental architectural choice flowing from Axioms A1 (decentralisation) and A2 (transparency). This section describes the organisational model of open-source development, contribution policies, and quality mechanisms.

#### 6.8.1. Principles of Open Development

Open-source code in the context of CyberSocium performs four functions that go beyond the traditional understanding of open source:

**1. Governance transparency.** Every participant may verify that the smart contracts deployed on BSC correspond to the published source code. This eliminates the need for trust: a participant does not need to believe that Governor works correctly — they may read and verify the code. Formally: for each contract C_deployed there exists C_source such that bytecode(compile(C_source)) = bytecode(C_deployed), and C_source is publicly accessible.

**2. Auditability.** Open-source code may be audited by any expert without an NDA or special permission. This enables: (a) AI audit via public tools (Slither, Mythril); (b) community peer review through Code DAO; (c) professional audit by external auditors with public reports. The model of "security through transparency" is the opposite of "security through obscurity" and is consistent with Kerckhoffs's cryptographic principle.

**3. Extensibility.** Any developer may fork the repository, modify it for their own needs, and propose changes back to upstream. This implements Axiom A4 (extensibility): the ecosystem grows not only through the core developers but also through contributors from the global community.

**4. Reproducibility.** Open code ensures the reproducibility of the scientific experiment: any researcher may deploy a complete copy of the GyberExperiment ecosystem (on a testnet) and verify the theoretical predictions of CyberSocium on their own data.

#### 6.8.2. Contribution Process

Contribution to the GyberExperiment codebase is governed by Code DAO and follows a formalised process:

```
Contribution process:

  1. Task identification:
     — Participant selects a task from G-Plan (bound to DAO)
     — Or creates a GitHub issue describing a bug/feature
     — AI assistant helps classify the issue:
       type (bug, feature, refactor, docs),
       priority (critical, high, medium, low),
       complexity estimate (XS, S, M, L, XL)

  2. PR (Pull Request) formation:
     — Participant forks the repository
     — Creates a branch: feature/<name> or fix/<name>
     — Develops the solution with tests
     — Opens PR with description: what, why, how to test

  3. Automated checks (CI/CD):
     — Lint: ESLint + Prettier (TypeScript) / Solhint (Solidity)
     — Type check: tsc --noEmit / slither (Solidity)
     — Tests: unit + integration, coverage ≥ threshold
     — Build: compilation check
     — AI Code Review: automatic vulnerability and code quality
       analysis (AiC Circuit 1)

  4. Peer review (Code DAO):
     — Minimum 2 approvals from participants
       with status ≥ Dev
     — For critical modules (CONTRACTS):
       minimum 1 approval from LeadDev+
     — Review includes: correctness, security,
       style, test coverage, documentation

  5. Merge and reward:
     — Merge after approval + CI pass
     — G-Plan records the contribution
     — Activity Score updated (EIP-712 proof)
     — Contributor receives reward
       via UnitManager (by status and complexity)

  6. Deployment:
     — Auto-deploy to staging (merge to main)
     — Manual approval for production (CONTRACTS)
     — Canary deployment for DSP Client and GPROD
```

**Contribution reward model:**

Each G-Plan task has a complexity rating that determines the base reward. The Activity Multiplier modulates the reward amount based on the quality of past contributions.

| Task complexity | Base reward (GBR) | Examples |
|---|---|---|
| XS (Extra Small) | 100,000 | Typo fix, dependency update |
| S (Small) | 500,000 | Bug fix, adding a test |
| M (Medium) | 2,000,000 | New feature, module refactoring |
| L (Large) | 10,000,000 | New contract, major feature with tests |
| XL (Extra Large) | 50,000,000 | Architectural change, new module |

Final reward = base_reward × AM(contributor) × review_quality_bonus, where review_quality_bonus ∈ [1.0, 1.3] is awarded by Code DAO reviewers for exceptional code quality.

#### 6.8.3. Versioning and Releases

All modules follow Semantic Versioning (SemVer 2.0.0): MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH. For smart contracts, given their irreversibility, additional constraints apply:

```
Smart contract versioning policy:

  PATCH (0.0.x):
    — Bug fixes via UUPS upgrade
    — Does not change the external interface
    — Requires: Code DAO approval (2 reviews)
    — Timelock: 24 hours

  MINOR (0.x.0):
    — Addition of new functions (backward-compatible)
    — Possible change to internal state
    — Requires: Code DAO approval + AI audit
    — Timelock: 48 hours

  MAJOR (x.0.0):
    — Breaking changes: interface change,
      data migration, new contracts
    — Requires: Code DAO + Economic DAO approval
      + external audit + AI audit
    — Timelock: 72 hours
    — Mandatory canary period: 7 days on testnet

  Each release:
    — Tagged in git: v1.2.3
    — Accompanied by CHANGELOG.md with detailed description
    — Verified on Etherscan/BscScan
    — Announced in Social DAO and DSP
```

#### 6.8.4. Codebase Governance

Management of the codebase is a function of Code DAO with escalation to Economic DAO for strategic decisions. Formalisation:

```
Code DAO decision types:

  Tactical (routine):
    — Merge PR
    — Dependency updates
    — CI/CD configuration
    Process: peer review (2 approvals)
    DAO vote: not required

  Operational (significant):
    — Change of architecture decision (ADR)
    — Addition of new module
    — Technology stack change
    Process: Code DAO vote (simple majority)
    Quorum: 10%

  Strategic (critical):
    — Smart contract upgrades (MAJOR)
    — Licence change
    — Integration of a new blockchain network
    — Decisions with economic consequences >10,000 GBR
    Process: Code DAO + Economic DAO vote
    Quorum: Code DAO 10%, Economic DAO 25%
```

This three-level model ensures balance: routine operations are not slowed down by the bureaucracy of DAO voting, while strategic decisions undergo thorough community scrutiny.

---
## 7. Comparative Analysis and Related Work

CyberSocial economics and GyberExperiment do not exist in a vacuum. A number of projects and theoretical frameworks address similar problems, albeit from different positions. This section situates the contribution of CyberSocium within the context of existing literature, active DAO projects, and alternative approaches to constructing decentralised socio-economic systems. The comparison is conducted along five dimensions: (1) theoretical foundations, (2) organisational architecture, (3) economic model, (4) legal integration, (5) AI integration.

### 7.1 Theoretical Frameworks: Review and Positioning

#### 7.1.1. Commons-Based Peer Production (Benkler, 2006)

Yochai Benkler, in his foundational work *The Wealth of Networks*, described the model of commons-based peer production (CBPP), whereby large groups of people coordinate their efforts to create public goods without market pricing or hierarchical management. Classic examples include Wikipedia, Linux, and OpenStreetMap.

**Connection to CyberSocium.** The PMIP model (Section 3.4) is a direct development of Benkler's ideas: the principle of minimal individual participation formalises the intuition that, given a sufficient number of participants, the individual contribution tends toward a vanishingly small quantity. The formula individual_cost(p) = C(p)/|SIC(p)| → 0 constitutes a mathematical axiomatisation of Benkler's observations concerning the "modularity" and "granularity" of tasks in CBPP systems.

**Fundamental distinction.** Benkler describes CBPP as a *supplement* to the market economy: Linux exists within the capitalist system and depends upon it (developers receive salaries from corporations that use Linux). CyberSocium lays claim to the creation of an *autonomous economic system* in which participants have no need of external financing. The PMIP + SES + IPI model creates a closed cycle: idea → project → realisation → value → distribution → new ideas. Benkler also does not address the problem of formal verification of contribution: in Wikipedia, contribution is measured by the number of edits, but not by their quality. UnitManager resolves this problem through a meritocratic system of statuses (None → Unit → Dev → LeadDev → ArchDev → Core) with programmatic verification of activity.

#### 7.1.2. Institutional Economics of the Commons (Ostrom, 1990)

Elinor Ostrom, Nobel Laureate of 2009, empirically demonstrated that communities are capable of effectively managing common-pool resources without privatisation or state regulation — provided that eight institutional design principles are observed [Ostrom, 1990]. These principles were examined in detail in Section 2.2, with a mapping onto the architecture of GyberExperiment.

**Connection to CyberSocium.** All eight of Ostrom's principles are implemented in the CSC architecture: clearly defined boundaries (NFT membership), congruence of rules (four-class DAO taxonomy), collective choice arrangements (Governor contracts), monitoring (on-chain transparency), graduated sanctions (Activity Scoring, status degradation), conflict-resolution mechanisms (FRP), recognition of rights to organise (DUNA), nested enterprises (modular DSP architecture).

**Fundamental distinction.** Ostrom studied *local* communities: irrigation systems in Nepal, fishing cooperatives in Turkey, forest commons in Japan. The maximum scale of her case studies was several thousand participants with geographic attachment. CyberSocium is designed for *global* scale with participants having no physical attachment. This requires fundamentally different mechanisms of identification (DID in place of personal acquaintance), monitoring (on-chain analytics in place of social control), and sanctions (automatic status degradation in place of social pressure). Furthermore, Ostrom did not examine *digital* common resources, which, unlike fish stocks or pastures, are non-rivalrous: one participant's use of code does not diminish its availability to others. This radically alters the economics of resource governance and eliminates the classical "tragedy of the commons" for digital assets, but creates new problems: free-riding, Sybil attacks, governance spam.

#### 7.1.3. Platform Cooperativism (Scholz, 2016)

Trebor Scholz and Nathan Schneider, in the manifesto *Ours to Hack and to Own* (2016), proposed the model of "platform cooperativism" — the reform of digital platforms by transferring ownership to workers and users. Examples include Stocksy (a photographers' cooperative), Up & Go (a cleaners' cooperative), and Resonate (a musicians' cooperative).

**Connection to CyberSocium.** Platform cooperativism shares with CyberSocium a critique of the extractive model of the platform economy and a commitment to democratic ownership. The DUNA structure of GyberExperiment is functionally analogous to a cooperative charter, ensuring collective ownership of infrastructure.

**Fundamental distinction.** Platform cooperativism is a *reform* of the existing model: Stocksy remains a photo agency, simply with a different ownership structure. CyberSocium is a *new paradigm*: not a platform reform, but the creation of an economic system in which the "platform" is merely one of the technological layers. More significantly: Scholz's cooperatives operate within the framework of traditional law (LLC, Cooperative Corp) and do not employ programmable economic incentives. CyberSocium implements incentives at the level of smart contracts (UnitManager, GBRVotingWrapper), rendering them transparent, verifiable, and resistant to manipulation.

#### 7.1.4. Cryptoeconomics (Buterin, 2014; Zamfir, 2015)

Vitalik Buterin introduced the term "cryptoeconomics" to describe the use of economic incentives and cryptographic proofs to ensure the properties of distributed systems. Vlad Zamfir extended this approach by proposing a formal methodology for the design of cryptoeconomic protocols [Zamfir, 2015].

**Connection to CyberSocium.** Buterin's cryptoeconomics is one of the theoretical foundations of CyberSocium (Section 2.5). The mechanisms of staking, slashing, and economic guarantees are implemented in UnitManager and the GBR tokenomics.

**Fundamental distinction.** Classical cryptoeconomics focuses on the *protocol* level: how to ensure consensus, how to prevent double-spend, how to incentivise validators. CyberSocium extends cryptoeconomics to the *socio-economic* level: how to organise collective decision-making, how to finance public goods, how to ensure fair value distribution. The CyberSocium axiomatic system (A1–A8, Section 3.1) goes far beyond cryptoeconomic protocols, incorporating axioms of meritocratic fairness (A5), inclusivity (A6), and cognitive augmentation (A8) that have no analogues in the work of Buterin or Zamfir.

#### 7.1.5. Token Engineering (Voshmgir, 2020; Zargham, 2020)

Shermin Voshmgir (*Token Economy*, 2020) and Michael Zargham (BlockScience, cadCAD) developed the discipline of "token engineering" — the systematic design of token ecosystems using methods from control theory, dynamical systems, and agent-based modelling.

**Connection to CyberSocium.** The cadCAD modelling methodology proposed by Zargham is applied in the simulation of GBR tokenomics (Section 6.3). Many of Voshmgir's principles — separation of governance tokens and utility tokens, velocity analysis, emission modelling — are implemented in the economic architecture of GyberExperiment.

**Fundamental distinction.** Token engineering is an *engineering methodology* applicable to any token project. CyberSocium is a *theoretical framework* defining for what purpose and why token systems are designed. Token engineering answers the question "how to design a sustainable tokenomics?"; CyberSocium answers the question "what economic system are we building and why is it superior to existing ones?". Furthermore, CyberSocium integrates the legal dimension (DUNA) and the AI dimension (AiC), which lie beyond the scope of the purely technical discipline of token engineering.

#### 7.1.6. Mechanism Design Theory (Hurwicz, 1960; Myerson, 1981)

Leonid Hurwicz laid the foundations of mechanism design theory, while Roger Myerson and Eric Maskin developed it into a formal discipline, awarded the Nobel Prize in 2007. Mechanism design theory studies the design of rules of interaction (mechanisms) under which rational agents with private information behave optimally for society (incentive compatibility).

**Connection to CyberSocium.** The formal mechanisms of CyberSocium — SES, PMIP, IPI, FRP — are designed with attention to the principles of incentive compatibility. Activity Scoring implements a "preference revelation" mechanism: participants demonstrate their engagement through verifiable on-chain actions, rather than through declarations.

**Fundamental distinction.** Classical mechanism design theory presupposes rational agents with complete information about their own preferences. CyberSocium operates under conditions of bounded rationality (Simon, 1955) and evolving preferences: DAO participants do not always know what they want, and their preferences change as they learn. Axiom A8 (cognitive augmentation) is a direct response to this problem: AI agents compensate for participants' bounded rationality by providing analytics, summarisation, and recommendations.

### 7.2 Practical Projects: Detailed Comparative Analysis

In order to position GyberExperiment among existing DAO projects, we conduct a detailed comparison along six key parameters. Projects representing different archetypes of decentralised organisations have been selected.

#### 7.2.1. MakerDAO / Sky (Ethereum, 2017)

**Description.** MakerDAO is one of the earliest and most successful DeFi protocols, managing the stablecoin DAI through a system of collateralised debt positions (CDPs). In 2024, it rebranded as Sky with the SKY/USDS tokens.

**Governance.** Token-weighted governance via the MKR/SKY token. Voting on key parameters: stability fee, collateral types, risk parameters. Executive votes are implemented through smart contract spells.

**Economics.** Dual-token model: MKR (governance + recapitalisation) and DAI (stablecoin). Revenue from stability fees is distributed through MKR buyback-and-burn.

**Comparison with GyberExperiment.** MakerDAO demonstrates that complex financial systems can be managed in a decentralised manner. However, MakerDAO is a *single-product* protocol (stablecoin), whereas GyberExperiment is a *multi-project ecosystem*. MakerDAO has no mechanisms for meritocratic governance: voting power is determined exclusively by the quantity of MKR held, which has led to the dominance of Rune Christensen (the founder) and several large holders. In GyberExperiment, Hybrid Voting Power combines token weight with a reputational coefficient (Activity Score), preventing plutocracy. Furthermore, MakerDAO does not integrate AI components into governance, whereas AiC is an institutional part of the GyberExperiment architecture.

#### 7.2.2. Aragon (Ethereum, 2017)

**Description.** Aragon is a platform for creating and managing DAOs, providing modular tools: Aragon Court (decentralised arbitration), Aragon Govern (optimistic governance), Aragon Voice (voting).

**Governance.** ANT token for voting on protocol upgrades. Aragon Court uses ANT staking to form juries.

**Economics.** ANT as governance token. The value model of ANT is tied to the number of DAOs created on the platform.

**Comparison with GyberExperiment.** Aragon is *infrastructure*, not a *system*. Aragon provides building blocks for DAOs but does not define how DAOs should function economically. GyberExperiment defines not only technical primitives, but also the economic model (PMIP, SES), the governance architecture (four-class taxonomy), and the theoretical foundation (8 axioms). By analogy: Aragon is AutoCAD (a draughting tool); GyberExperiment is an architectural project (a building with drawings, calculations, and specifications). Aragon Court implements *legal* arbitration within a DAO; the FRP of CyberSocium implements *economic* arbitration at the level of project forks.

#### 7.2.3. Gitcoin (Ethereum, 2017)

**Description.** Gitcoin is a platform for financing public goods in Web3 through a grant system with Quadratic Funding (QF), proposed by Buterin, Hitzig, and Weyl [Buterin et al., 2019].

**Governance.** GTC token for governing Gitcoin DAO. The Gitcoin Passport programme for Sybil resistance.

**Economics.** Matching pool + quadratic funding. Donors contribute funds; the matching pool amplifies contributions proportionally to the square root of the number of unique donors.

**Comparison with GyberExperiment.** Gitcoin implements an elegant financing mechanism, but is constrained by the *grant* model: a project receives one-off funding and subsequently exists autonomously. GyberExperiment, through the IPI model, implements a *complete lifecycle*: from idea (Idea) through project (Project) to realisation (Implementation), with continuous funding, monitoring, and iteration. Furthermore, Gitcoin Passport addresses the Sybil attack problem through off-chain verification (binding to Twitter, Google, ENS), whereas GyberExperiment employs on-chain meritocratic mechanisms: a Sybil attacker may create multiple accounts but cannot falsify Activity Score, which requires genuine verified activity.

#### 7.2.4. Colony (Ethereum, 2018)

**Description.** Colony is a protocol for the decentralised management of organisations, employing "lazy consensus" and a domain system for structuring work.

**Governance.** Reputation-weighted governance: voting weight is determined by reputation within a specific domain, not by token holdings. Reputation is earned by completing tasks and distributing rewards.

**Economics.** CLNY token + native reputation system. Reputation is non-transferable and decays over time.

**Comparison with GyberExperiment.** Colony is most similar to GyberExperiment in its conception of reputation-weighted governance. Colony's domain system is analogous to GyberExperiment's four-class DAO taxonomy — both divide governance by functional areas. However, Colony operates at the level of *a single organisation*: one Colony = one company/project. GyberExperiment designs an *ecosystem of organisations* (MacroeconomicDAO), in which multiple projects (IPI) exist and compete within a single economic space with a shared token (GBR), shared selection mechanisms (SES), and a shared legal wrapper (DUNA). Colony also does not address legal integration or AI components.

#### 7.2.5. Nouns DAO (Ethereum, 2021)

**Description.** Nouns DAO is an experimental project in which one NFT is minted daily at auction and all proceeds are directed to the DAO treasury. Each Noun NFT = one vote.

**Governance.** One-NFT-one-vote. Minimal quorum for proposals. Vote delegation.

**Economics.** Perpetual auction → DAO treasury. At its peak, the treasury exceeded $50M.

**Comparison with GyberExperiment.** Nouns DAO is of interest as an experiment in *perpetual funding*: the daily auction creates a continuous inflow of resources to the treasury. GyberExperiment implements an analogous idea through different mechanisms: the Governance Pool with 80% of initial supply, staking rewards, and SES contributions. The fundamental distinction: Nouns DAO is a *financial club* without a defined mission (the treasury is spent on everything from graffiti to sports sponsorship), whereas GyberExperiment is a *purposeful economic system* with formalised project selection criteria (SES) and measurable performance metrics. The Nouns problem: the "fork wars" of 2023, in which a group of holders exited the DAO, taking their proportional share of the treasury. The FRP of CyberSocium formalises the fork procedure, preventing chaotic splits.

#### 7.2.6. DAOstack (Ethereum, 2018)

**Description.** DAOstack is a framework for scalable governance, based on the "holographic consensus" algorithm, in which prediction markets determine which proposals deserve the attention of the majority.

**Governance.** GEN token + holographic consensus: stakers predict whether a proposal will be approved and receive rewards for correct predictions. This filters proposals and reduces the burden on voters.

**Economics.** GEN as utility token for staking in prediction markets.

**Comparison with GyberExperiment.** DAOstack's holographic consensus solves the same problem as AI summarisation in AiC GyberExperiment: scaling governance as the number of proposals grows. DAOstack uses a *market* mechanism (prediction markets); GyberExperiment uses an *AI mechanism* (AI analyst). DAOstack's advantage: markets are decentralised and independent of AI providers. GyberExperiment's advantage: AI summarisation is available to every participant, not only stakers, and provides deeper analysis (impact assessment, risk analysis, cross-DAO coordination).

### 7.3 Multi-Dimensional Comparative Matrix

For a systematic comparison of GyberExperiment with the projects examined above, the following dimensions are employed:

| Dimension | MakerDAO | Aragon | Gitcoin | Colony | Nouns | DAOstack | **GyberExperiment** |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| **Type** | DeFi protocol | DAO infrastructure | Grant platform | Organisational protocol | Financial club | Governance framework | **CSC ecosystem** |
| **Scale** | Single-product | Meta-platform | Single-function | Single organisation | Single treasury | Meta-platform | **Multi-project ecosystem** |
| **Voting** | Token-weighted | Token-weighted | QF for grants, token for governance | Reputation-weighted | NFT (1=1) | Holographic consensus | **Hybrid (token + reputation + AI)** |
| **Meritocracy** | No | No | Partial (QF) | Yes (reputation) | No | Partial (staking) | **Yes (UnitManager, Activity Score)** |
| **Legal wrapper** | Cayman Foundation | Swiss Verein | Cayman Foundation | None | Unincorporated | None | **DUNA (Wyoming)** |
| **AI integration** | No | No | Sybil scoring | No | No | No | **AiC (3 circuits)** |
| **Formal theory** | No | No | QF [Buterin et al., 2019] | No | No | Holographic consensus | **8 axioms, PMIP, SES, IPI, FRP** |
| **Project lifecycle** | N/A | No | Grant → autonomy | Task-based | Ad hoc | Proposal → vote | **IPI (Idea → Project → Implementation)** |
| **Fork protocol** | Emergency Shutdown | No | No | No | Ragequit (Nouns Fork) | No | **FRP (formalised)** |
| **Treasury** | Stability fee → MKR burn | ANT sales | Matching pools (external) | Internal | NFT auction | GEN sales | **Governance Pool + LP Burn + SES** |

### 7.4 Thematic Comparison by Key Problems

#### 7.4.1. The Problem of Plutocracy

Token-weighted voting is the de facto standard in the DAO ecosystem, but it engenders *plutocracy*: power concentrates among large holders. Different projects offer different solutions:

| Project | Anti-plutocracy mechanism | Limitations |
|---|---|---|
| Gitcoin | Quadratic Funding (square root of number of donors) | Vulnerable to Sybil attacks without Passport |
| Colony | Reputation-weighted (non-transferable) | Reputation tied to domain; manipulation via "easy" tasks |
| Optimism | Bicameral system (Token House + Citizens' House) | Centralised issuance of soulbound tokens for Citizens' House |
| GyberExperiment | Hybrid Voting Power = f(GBR_staked, activity_score, member_status) | Requires reliable Activity Scoring; AI dependency |

CyberSocium offers the most comprehensive approach: not the abandonment of token weighting (which reduces economic incentives), but a *correction* of weight through a reputational multiplier. The formula: voting_power = sqrt(GBR_staked) × activity_multiplier × status_bonus ensures sub-quadratic scaling of token weight and a premium for activity.

#### 7.4.2. The Problem of Governance Scalability

As a DAO grows, the number of proposals exceeds participants' cognitive capacity (voter fatigue), leading to decreased participation and degraded decision quality.

| Project | Scaling mechanism | Limitations |
|---|---|---|
| DAOstack | Holographic consensus (prediction markets) | Requires liquid market; manipulation by stakers |
| Compound | Vote delegation (liquid democracy) | Professional delegates form a new elite |
| Nouns | Low quorum (10%) + delegation | Low quorum reduces decision legitimacy |
| GyberExperiment | Four-class taxonomy + AI summarisation + delegation | AI dependency; complexity of four-class system for new participants |

GyberExperiment's four-class DAO taxonomy is a unique solution without parallel: the division of proposals by type (SocialDAO, CodeDAO, CommerceDAO, EconomicDAO) with different voting rules, quorums, and timelines for each type. This *reduces* cognitive load: a participant may focus on the type of decisions in which they are competent, delegating the remainder.

#### 7.4.3. The Problem of Legal Integration

The legal status of DAOs remains an unresolved problem. Most DAOs either ignore legal status (which creates the risk of unlimited liability for participants) or use traditional corporate forms (which contradicts the principles of decentralisation).

| Project | Legal form | Limitations |
|---|---|---|
| MakerDAO | Cayman Foundation | Offshore jurisdiction; limited recognition in other countries |
| Aragon | Swiss Verein (Association) | Swiss Verein has operational limitations |
| Lido | Cayman Foundation → DAO | Transitional period; indeterminate status |
| GyberExperiment | DUNA (Wyoming, USA) | Single-state jurisdiction; requires compliance for international participants |

DUNA (Decentralised Unincorporated Nonprofit Association) is the most advanced legal form, developed specifically for DAOs. Wyoming W.S. § 17-32 (2024) provides: limited liability for participants, recognition of on-chain voting as legally binding, and compatibility with IRS 501(c)(6). GyberExperiment is one of the first major projects to use DUNA as its legal wrapper (see Section 4.6 and Appendix E).

#### 7.4.4. The Problem of AI Integration

AI integration in DAO systems is at an early stage. Most projects do not consider AI as a systemic architectural component.

| Project | AI usage | Level of integration |
|---|---|---|
| MakerDAO | Risk analytics (external providers) | Ad hoc, not institutionalised |
| Gitcoin | Sybil scoring (Gitcoin Passport ML) | Single-function (anti-Sybil) |
| Compound | None | — |
| GyberExperiment | AiC (3 circuits: internal, research, commercial) | **Institutional** (Axiom A8, formalised AI governance) |

AiC (AI Community) of GyberExperiment is a unique institutional solution: not merely the use of AI tools, but the *democratic governance of AI development* through DAO mechanisms. The three AiC circuits (internal — AI for CSC operations; research — community AI research; commercial — AI products of the ecosystem) provide the full spectrum of AI integration, from the automation of routine operations to fundamental research.

### 7.5 The Unique Contribution of GyberExperiment: A Synthesis

On the basis of the analysis conducted, it is possible to identify five key dimensions along which GyberExperiment represents a unique contribution not replicable by existing projects:

1. **Theoretical completeness.** GyberExperiment is the only project that rests upon a formal axiomatic system (8 axioms), a set of formalised mechanisms (PMIP, SES, IPI, FRP), and mathematically proven properties (the Pareto-optimality theorem for SES, the PMIP convergence theorem). Neither MakerDAO, nor Aragon, nor Colony possesses a comparable theoretical foundation.

2. **Architectural complexity.** The four-class DAO taxonomy implementing differentiated governance for different types of decisions (social, technical, commercial, economic) has no analogues in the existing DAO ecosystem. Colony divides governance by domains (functional areas), but not by *types of decisions* with different voting rules.

3. **Economic model.** The integration of PMIP (principle of minimal participation), SES (evolutionary project selection), IPI (idea lifecycle), and LP Burn (liquidity burning mechanism) into a single economic system has no precedent. Existing projects implement individual components (QF in Gitcoin, reputation in Colony, auction in Nouns) but do not integrate them into a coherent economic theory.

4. **Legal integration.** The use of DUNA in combination with a two-tier treasury (DUNA Treasury for fiat, On-Chain Treasury for crypto) represents the most advanced model of DAO legal integration. The separation of concerns between the legal and technical levels ensures compliance without sacrificing decentralisation.

5. **AI integration as a systemic principle.** Axiom A8 (cognitive augmentation) introduces AI integration at the level of the *axiomatic system*, rather than as an ad hoc tool. AiC institutionalises the governance of AI development through DAO mechanisms. None of the projects examined includes AI at the level of architectural principles.

**Synthetic claim.** GyberExperiment represents the first attempt to construct a *complete* socio-economic system on the basis of blockchain technologies, encompassing all levels — from formal theory (axioms, theorems) through technical architecture (smart contracts, protocols) to legal integration (DUNA) and the institutional governance of AI (AiC). This distinguishes the project from existing DAOs, which, as a rule, operate on one or two of the above-mentioned levels.

### 7.6 Why the Synthesis Had Not Been Achieved Earlier: An Analysis of Barriers

If the need for a complete decentralised socio-economic system is so evident, why did none of the projects examined attain the completeness of CyberSocium? This question is not rhetorical — its answer sheds light on the necessary conditions that coincided only by 2024–2025, and explains why the synthesis became possible precisely now.

#### 7.6.1. Technological Barrier: Infrastructure Immaturity

The first DAOs (TheDAO, 2016) appeared on Ethereum, which at the time processed approximately 15 transactions per second, with gas costs on the order of $10–50 for complex operations. CyberSocium's four-class DAO system generates an order of magnitude more transactions than a monolithic DAO: each class requires its own voting processes, cross-class proposals generate additional on-chain operations, and Activity Score is updated periodically for each participant. With approximately 1,000 participants and four DAO classes, the system would generate approximately 4,000 votes/month × approximately 3 transactions/vote = approximately 12,000 transactions/month for governance alone — which was economically infeasible on Ethereum prior to 2021 (the emergence of L2) and practically inadvisable prior to BSC, Polygon, and analogous high-performance networks.

Key technological preconditions that became available by 2024:

- **EVM-compatible L1/L2 with low gas** (BSC, Polygon, Arbitrum): gas < $0.01 per transaction, making the four-class DAO architecture economically viable;
- **OpenZeppelin Governor** (2021): standardised contract for on-chain governance, reducing development cost from months to days;
- **UUPS Proxy pattern** (2021): the ability to upgrade contracts without state migration, critically important for iterative calibration of parameters;
- **Account Abstraction** (ERC-4337, 2023): reduction of the cognitive entry barrier for non-crypto users;
- **IPFS + Filecoin** (mature by 2022): decentralised storage, necessary for DDP;
- **Federated Learning frameworks** (PySyft, Flower, 2020+): infrastructure for implementing A3 (data sovereignty) without loss of AI model quality;
- **cadCAD** (BlockScience, 2018+): a modelling tool enabling simulation of cryptoeconomic systems prior to deployment.

Prior to the convergence of all these components, designing a system of the CyberSocium level was technically feasible, but economically inadvisable and practically risky.

#### 7.6.2. Theoretical Barrier: Disciplinary Silos

The problem addressed by CyberSocium lies at the intersection of at least six disciplines: economic theory (mechanism design, tokenomics), computer science (distributed systems, cryptography), law (corporate law, DAO regulation), sociology (organisation theory, collective action), philosophy (ethics, epistemology), and AI/ML (LLM, GNN, Federated Learning). Traditional academic and industrial structures are organised along disciplinary lines: economists design tokenomics without understanding gas costs; engineers write smart contracts without understanding mechanism design; lawyers analyse compliance without understanding cryptography.

This disciplinary silo explains the characteristic limitations of each project examined. MakerDAO was created by financial engineers — a brilliant DeFi architecture, but without a theory of collective action. Aragon was created by developers — a modular DAO platform, but without an economic model for project financing. Colony was created by managers — a task and role system, but without formal axiomatics. Gitcoin was created by crypto activists — innovative financing mechanisms (QF), but without legal integration.

CyberSocium became possible thanks to the conscious decision to design the system as a *transdisciplinary synthesis* from the outset, rather than as an extension of one of the existing disciplines. The formalisation as CSC = ⟨N, R, P, G, M, E⟩ is not merely a notation, but a statement that all six components must be designed simultaneously, taking into account their mutual dependencies. The axiomatic system A1–A8 serves as the interface between disciplines: each axiom has an interpretation in each of the six disciplines, ensuring the coherence of the entire design.

#### 7.6.3. Regulatory Barrier: Absence of a Legal Form

Prior to 2024, no legal form existed that adequately described a DAO as a legal entity. Projects used surrogates: BVI Foundation Company (MakerDAO), Cayman Foundation (Uniswap), Swiss Verein (Aragon), or simply remained without legal entity status (the majority of DAOs). Each surrogate created a fundamental contradiction: the legal form presupposed centralised governance (board of directors, registered agent), whereas actual governance was conducted through DAO voting. This gap generated legal uncertainty, made banking relationships impossible, and created tax compliance problems for participants.

Wyoming DUNA (Decentralised Unincorporated Nonprofit Association, W.S. § 17-32, entered into force 1 July 2024) for the first time provided a legal form in which:
- DAO participants are legally recognised as members of the association;
- Governance is conducted through smart contracts and on-chain voting (Article 17-32-107);
- There is no requirement for a centralised board of directors;
- Limited liability for participants (Article 17-32-104);
- Tax status 501(c)(6) is available (nonprofit).

Without DUNA or an analogous form, building a complete socio-economic system (and not merely a DeFi protocol) was legally impossible: a system claiming to coordinate real economic activity (labour relations, goods, services) must possess legal capacity. The emergence of DUNA in 2024 removed the last of the three fundamental barriers.

#### 7.6.4. Cultural Barrier: From "Code Is Law" to Institutional Design

The early DAOs (2016–2020) proceeded from the philosophy of "code is law" — the conviction that a smart contract fully replaces legal norms and social institutions. TheDAO hack (2016), the loss of funds due to the vulnerability in Parity Multisig (2017), governance attacks on various DeFi protocols — a series of catastrophic failures gradually brought the community to the understanding that code *cannot* be the sole regulator. What are required are: (a) formal theory to justify architectural decisions; (b) a legal wrapper for interaction with the external world; (c) social norms and processes for resolving situations not anticipated by the code.

This cultural evolution — from crypto-anarchist maximalism to mature institutional design — took nearly a decade. CyberSocium is a product of this evolution: the project consciously combines cryptographic trustlessness (smart contracts ensure security without trust in specific agents) with institutional design (DUNA, FRP, and MacroeconomicDAO ensure adaptability and conflict resolution).

**Section conclusion.** A synthesis of the CyberSocium level became possible at the intersection of four conditions: (1) technological maturity of EVM-compatible blockchains and AI/ML tools; (2) a transdisciplinary approach consciously overcoming disciplinary silos; (3) legal innovation (DUNA, 2024); (4) cultural maturation of the blockchain community from "code is law" to institutional design. Not one of these conditions was fulfilled prior to 2024, which explains why existing projects — for all their merits — remained partial solutions.

---

## 8. Discussion: Implications, Limitations, and Open Questions

### 8.1 Theoretical Implications

The model of CyberSocial economics presented in this document carries several important theoretical implications, touching on fundamental questions in economic theory, organisation theory, political philosophy, and computer science.

#### 8.1.1. Implications for Economic Theory

CyberSocium proposes a model of economic organisation in which value is not concentrated but distributed through the PMIP mechanism. The formula individual_cost(p) = C(p) / |SIC(p)| → 0 as |SIC(p)| grows demonstrates that collective action is capable of overcoming the constraints of individual capital without recourse to centralised institutions — banks, state funds, or venture capital. This extends the models of Ostrom (commons governance) and Benkler (commons-based peer production) into the domain of full-fledged economic governance.

A deeper implication of PMIP is that it blurs the traditional distinction between microeconomics and macroeconomics. In classical economic theory there is a clear division: microeconomics describes the behaviour of individual agents and firms; macroeconomics describes the aggregate indicators of the economy as a whole. In the CSC model this division loses its meaning: every individual act of participation (a micro-decision to join a SIC) is immediately translated into a macro-effect (reduction of individual_cost for all participants). The PMIP mechanism creates a direct, formalised link between individual action and systemic outcome — a link that in the traditional economy is mediated by a multitude of institutions (banks, exchanges, state regulators) and is accompanied by significant information losses.

For the theory of the firm, CyberSocium proposes a rethinking of the boundaries of the firm. Ronald Coase (1937) explained the existence of the firm through the minimisation of transaction costs: the firm arises where internal coordination is cheaper than market coordination [8]. Oliver Williamson (1985) developed this theory by demonstrating the role of opportunism and asset specificity [9]. CSC proposes a third model that is reducible neither to the firm (hierarchical coordination) nor to the market (price coordination): protocolised coordination through smart contracts, in which transaction costs are minimised not by centralisation but by automation. The boundaries of CSC are defined not by organisational structure but by the perimeter of the protocol — all those interacting through shared contracts are "inside" CSC.

For the theory of public goods, the implications are no less substantial. The classical problem of public goods (Samuelson, 1954) holds that rational agents are not inclined to voluntarily finance goods they can use for free (the free-rider problem). Olson (1965) showed that collective action is impossible without coercion or selective incentives [29]. PMIP in combination with SES offers a third path: not coercion and not external incentives, but structural design in which participation becomes a rational strategy. When individual_cost → 0, and access to the results of a project is conditioned on participation (through the project token), the free-rider problem is solved architecturally rather than institutionally.

#### 8.1.2. Implications for Organisation Theory

The four-class DAO taxonomy proposes a new approach to structuring organisational governance, in which different types of decisions are processed by different, purpose-optimised processes. This contrasts both with the monolithic corporate hierarchy and with undifferentiated DAOs, where all decisions pass through a single mechanism.

Classical organisation theory (Mintzberg, 1979; Burns and Stalker, 1961) distinguishes several basic organisational forms: machine bureaucracy, professional bureaucracy, simple structure, divisional form, adhocracy. CSC fits none of these categories. The closest analogy is Mintzberg's adhocracy — a flexible, project-oriented structure — but CSC surpasses it in several dimensions: (a) formalised coordination through smart contracts in place of informal mechanisms; (b) democratic governance of parameters in place of managerial control; (c) transparent and verifiable history of all decisions; (d) built-in economic incentives in place of employment contracts.

The implication for corporate governance theory (Berle & Means, 1932 [91]) lies in the elimination of the fundamental problem of the separation of ownership and control. In the traditional corporation, shareholders (owners) delegate management to managers, which creates the agency problem — a misalignment of interests. In CSC this problem is resolved structurally: each participant is simultaneously a "shareholder" (GBR holder), a "manager" (voting in the DAO), and a "worker" (contributor to a G-Plan). The Hybrid Voting Power formula VP = √(GBR_staked) × ActivityMultiplier × StatusBonus ensures that voting power depends not only on capital but also on real contribution, eliminating both the plutocracy of pure token-weighted voting and the inefficiency of pure democracy (one person = one vote).

#### 8.1.3. Implications for Game Theory and Mechanism Design

The SES (socio-economic selection) model proposes an evolutionary mechanism for project selection that requires no centralised arbiter, yet avoids the tragedy of the commons problem through formalised incentives (GBR burn, reputation, staking).

From the perspective of mechanism design theory, GyberExperiment constitutes a large-scale institutional experiment (*mechanism design experiment*) — an attempt to realise in practice what mechanism design theory describes abstractly: the design of rules of the game under which rational agents, acting in their own interests, achieve a socially optimal outcome. The key distinction from classical mechanism design is that the rules of GyberExperiment are not fixed — they are themselves subject to voting and evolution. This is a "meta-mechanism" — a mechanism for designing mechanisms.

Formally, if a mechanism M = (S, f) is defined by a strategy space S and an outcome function f, then MacroeconomicDAO constitutes a meta-mechanism M* = (S*, f*), where S* is the set of possible mechanisms M, and f* is the function determining which mechanism M is selected on the basis of DAO voting. This creates a recursive structure: participants vote not only on outcomes, but on the rules by which they vote — up to and including changes to the voting rules themselves (changes to quorum, voting period, Hybrid Voting Power formula). The stability of such a system is an open theoretical question (Section 8.5.4).

#### 8.1.4. Implications for Philosophy and Social Theory

CyberSocium implicitly proposes a new ontology of economic relations. In classical political economy, the subjects of the economy are individuals, firms, and states. In the CSC model a new type of subject appears — the CyberSocial corporation, which is neither a firm (no hierarchy and no employment contracts), nor a state (no territory and no monopoly on violence), nor a market (there is explicit governance and collective decisions), nor a commune (there are formalised economic incentives and tokenomics).

This resonates with Michel Foucault's concept of "heterotopia" — a space that functions according to different rules from the surrounding society, yet actually exists (as distinct from utopia). CSC is a heterotopia in the economic space: it functions within the existing economic system (it uses fiat money through DUNA, tokens on BSC, servers in traditional data centres) but is subject to different rules of coordination and distribution.

### 8.2 Practical Implications

#### 8.2.1. Implications for the Blockchain Industry

GyberExperiment demonstrates that DAOs can move beyond the governance of a single protocol or financial product and function as full-fledged economic systems with diversified projects, differentiated governance, and legal legitimacy.

The current state of the DAO ecosystem is characterised by several patterns that GyberExperiment seeks to overcome:

**Mono-functionality.** Most successful DAOs manage a single product or protocol: MakerDAO manages the stablecoin DAI, Uniswap DAO manages the DEX protocol, Aave DAO manages the lending protocol. GyberExperiment proposes a model of a multi-product DAO, in which a single ecosystem coordinates multiple projects of varying nature — from social (DSP) to technical (GyberNet) and commercial (Commerce DAO projects).

**Plutocratic governance.** According to DeepDAO data (2024), in the majority of large DAOs more than 80% of voting power is concentrated among fewer than 1% of participants [92]. GyberExperiment addresses this problem through Hybrid Voting Power, in which the square root of stake reduces the weight of large holders, and Activity Multiplier and Status Bonus ensure that real contribution carries weight.

**Regulatory grey zone.** The overwhelming majority of DAOs operate without a legal entity, which creates risks for participants (unlimited personal liability) and barriers to interaction with the traditional economy (inability to enter into contracts, open bank accounts, hire employees). DUNA resolves this problem by providing a legal wrapper that preserves the principles of decentralisation.

**Voter apathy.** Average turnout in DAO votes amounts to 3–5% of the total number of token holders (Chainalysis, 2024 [93]). GyberExperiment proposes a comprehensive solution: AI summarisation of proposals, tiered governance (minor decisions are automated), reputation incentives for participation, and liquid democracy for delegation.

#### 8.2.2. Implications for Policy and Regulation

The use of DUNA as a legal wrapper offers a model for the regulatory recognition of decentralised organisations that preserves the principles of decentralisation while ensuring legal accountability.

Specific implications for regulators:

- **The DUNA model as precedent.** The Wyoming Decentralised Unincorporated Nonprofit Association Act (W.S. § 17-32, 2024) is the world's first specialised law for DAOs. Successful operation of GyberExperiment under DUNA will create a precedent for other jurisdictions and may stimulate the adoption of analogous legislation.
- **Tax model for DAO participants.** The formalisation of relations between DUNA and participants (compensation for services, 1099 in the United States) creates a transparent tax model applicable to other DAOs.
- **Howey test for utility tokens.** The architecture of GBR (distribution for work rather than sale to investors; utility functions of governance and staking; absence of "promoters" — value depends on collective efforts) offers a utility token model that minimises the risk of classification as a security.
- **Cross-jurisdictional compatibility.** The interaction of DUNA with European regulation (MiCA — Markets in Crypto-Assets Regulation [95]), Singaporean regulation (Payment Services Act [96]), and Swiss regulation (DLT Act [97]) forms a map of regulatory compatibility for global DAOs.

#### 8.2.3. Implications for the AI Industry

AiC offers an alternative model of AI development organisation, based on a community rather than a corporate structure, with democratic governance of research priorities and ethical constraints.

The current AI industry is characterised by extreme concentration: OpenAI, Anthropic, Google DeepMind, and Meta AI control the development of the most powerful models. This creates several problems that AiC seeks to address:

- **Concentration of control over AI.** Decisions about the capabilities, constraints, and ethical boundaries of AI models are made by a narrow circle of corporate executives. AiC proposes a model in which AI development priorities are determined by the community through Code DAO.
- **Opacity of data and training process.** Commercial AI models are trained on proprietary data; the training process is opaque. AiC uses open-source models (AGPL) with a transparent training process on GyberComputer.
- **Dependence on API providers.** Most organisations use AI through cloud provider APIs, which creates dependency and privacy risks. GyberComputer + Federated Learning provides computational autonomy.

#### 8.2.4. Implications for Education and Universities

The GyberExperiment model proposes a radically new model for organising academic research and educational processes. The traditional academic system is characterised by several problems that CyberSocium addresses directly.

**The problem of grant funding.** Modern science depends on grant agencies (NSF, ERC, RFBR), which act as centralised arbiters determining which research receives funding. The grant review process is opaque, subjective, and prone to conservatism: revolutionary ideas that do not fit the mainstream are systematically underfunded (Nicholson & Ioannidis, 2012). The IPI model of CyberSocium offers an alternative: a research project receives funding proportional to the number of interested participants (|SIC|), not to a subjective expert committee assessment. This does not eliminate expert evaluation (Code DAO conducts peer review), but supplements it with a democratic mechanism for priority determination.

**The problem of publication pressure.** The "publish or perish" principle stimulates quantity of publications at the expense of quality, leading to a replication crisis and journal inflation. In the CyberSocium model, where contribution is measured through Activity Score and G-Plan, a researcher is rewarded for real contribution (code, data, reproducible results), not for number of publications. Verifiable Credentials issued by Code DAO provide verifiable confirmation of contribution without dependence on h-index metrics.

**The model of the decentralised university.** CyberSocium enables one to envision a "decentralised university" — an educational and research organisation functioning as a CSC: students and faculty are participants in NFC; courses and research are organised as IPI projects; quality is assessed through peer review (Code DAO) and social relevance (SES); degrees and certificates are Verifiable Credentials on the blockchain. The SAPP module GyberLearn (Section 6.5.2) is the first step towards realising this model.

#### 8.2.5. Implications for Developing Countries

The PMIP model has particular significance for countries with limited access to traditional financing. In developing countries, entrepreneurs and inventors face a double barrier: the absence of local capital and the impossibility of attracting international financing due to institutional constraints (corruption, instability, absence of legal infrastructure).

CyberSocium offers a bypass of these barriers: a participant from Ghana or Bangladesh may: (a) join NFC by confirming activity through G-Plan, without requiring a bank account or credit history; (b) obtain Unit → Dev status through verified work, without requiring formal education or references; (c) initiate a project through Commerce DAO, attracting participants from the global NFC; (d) receive rewards in GBR, convertible through a DEX, without dependence on local banking infrastructure.

Formally: PMIP eliminates the geographic bias of traditional venture capital (85% of VC investments are concentrated in 3 countries: USA, China, United Kingdom). In CSC the sole criterion for project financing is social relevance SR(p) ≥ 1, determined by the global |SIC(p)|, not by the geographic location of the initiator.

**Case study (hypothetical): Project "CleanWater".** An engineer from Kenya develops a water purification system applicable in rural areas. In the traditional economy: requirement of $50,000 for prototyping → approach to local banks (refusal due to absence of collateral) → approach to international grant agencies (competition with thousands of applications, wait of 6–12 months) → possible rejection. In the CSC model: publication of the idea on DSP → formation of SIC (15,000 participants from NFC are interested in accessible water) → individual_cost = $50,000 / 15,000 = $3.33 per participant → project is socially relevant (SR = 1) → Commerce DAO approves → AG is formed (engineer + 8 developers + 3 water treatment experts from NFC) → AiC optimises design → prototype → testing → scaling. Full cycle: 3–6 months instead of 12–24 in the traditional model.

#### 8.2.6. Implications for the Cooperative Movement

CyberSocium may be regarded as the digital evolution of the cooperative movement — a tradition tracing back to the Rochdale Pioneers (1844). Trebor Scholz (2016) introduced the term "platform cooperativism" to describe digital platforms owned and managed by their users [74]. Nathan Schneider (2018) developed this idea by proposing the concept of "exit to community" — the process of transferring control of a platform from founders to users [75].

GyberExperiment realises both of these principles: the platform (DSP) belongs to participants through DAO governance, and progressive decentralisation formalises the "exit to community" process. The distinction from traditional cooperatives: (a) global scale without geographic constraints; (b) formalised governance through smart contracts rather than meetings; (c) crypto-economic incentives in place of share contributions; (d) AI augmentation of participants' cognitive capacities.

### 8.3 Limitations and Challenges

We acknowledge the following limitations and challenges. For each limitation, the degree of mitigation provided by AI integration (Axiom A8, Section 5.6) is indicated:

1. **The governance scalability problem:** as the community grows, the number of proposals and votes may exceed participants' cognitive capacity, leading to voter fatigue and degraded decision quality. Vote delegation (liquid democracy) and AI summarisation of proposals are partial solutions.
   > **AI mitigation (substantial).** The AI analyst (5.6) generates personalised summaries of proposals, impact analysis, and recommendations for each participant, reducing cognitive load from O(n) to O(1). The AI Cross-DAO Coordinator automatically routes proposals, eliminating information noise. Formally: cognitive_load(participant | AI) ≈ const for any number of proposals. The governance scalability problem is transformed from a fundamental constraint into an engineering challenge.

2. **The bootstrapping problem:** the PMIP model is most effective when |SIC| is large, but the initial stages of a project inevitably proceed with a small number of participants. Mechanisms for attracting early participants (elevated statuses in UnitManager, early access to tokens) are a compromise.
   > **AI mitigation (moderate).** AI agents compensate for small |SIC| in the early stages by performing coordination functions that in a mature system are distributed among participants: the AI assistant manages projects, the AI code reviewer ensures code quality, the AI analyst conducts due diligence. Effectively: effective_capacity(SIC | AI) > |SIC| even when |SIC| is small. However, the problem of attracting initial human participants remains.

3. **Regulatory uncertainty:** despite DUNA as a legal wrapper, the regulatory landscape for DAOs continues to evolve, and legislative changes may require adaptation of the organisational structure.

4. **Technical risks:** dependence on smart contracts introduces the risks of software errors with potentially irreversible financial consequences. Auditing, formal verification, and multi-level Timelock mechanisms reduce but do not eliminate these risks.
   > **AI mitigation (substantial).** The AI Code Reviewer (5.6.2) provides multi-level automated checking: static analysis, vulnerability detection (integration with Octane Security, Sherlock, AuditAgent from Nethermind), formal verification of invariants. The AI auditor blocks merges upon detecting critical vulnerabilities (class "AI decides" per A8). At the same time, AI introduces its own class of risks — AI model errors, hallucinations, adversarial attacks on AI systems — which require additional protective mechanisms (see point 6 below).

5. **The plutocracy problem:** despite mechanisms for activity verification and reputational weighting, token voting remains substantially weighted by capital, creating a risk of domination by large holders. Quadratic voting and conviction voting are being studied as possible mitigation mechanisms.

6. **AI dependency risks (new limitation).** Deep AI integration (Axiom A8) introduces its own class of risks: dependence on AI providers, adversarial attacks on AI models, risk of "bias amplification" (automation of bias), potential atrophy of collective judgement under excessive AI delegation. The division principle (A8) and soulbound tokens for AI auditing are the primary protective mechanisms, but the long-term effects of AI dependency in decentralised systems are an open research question.

7. **The problem of verifying AI decisions (new limitation).** When an AI agent acts in the class "AI decides" (automatic blocking of vulnerable code, emergency stop upon anomaly detection), the community must have the ability to verify the correctness of these decisions. For complex AI models (LLM, deep learning), full explainability of decisions remains an unsolved problem (the black box problem). On-chain logging and DAO voting on challenging AI decisions are partial solutions.

8. **Key-person risk (bootstrap-phase limitation).** The current phase of the experiment is characterised by elevated key-person risk — dependence on a narrow group of initiators controlling the Governance Pool, GitHub repositories, and DUNA registration. This risk is a deliberate property of the bootstrap phase and is reduced as the transition to MacroeconomicDAO proceeds: transfer of funds to a stabilisation fund (smart contract), expansion of the circle of participants with ArchDev/Core rights, diversification of Gnosis Safe signers.

### 8.4 Threat Model and Security Analysis

A systematic analysis of attack vectors and their mitigation mechanisms is a necessary condition for transitioning from the experimental to the production phase. This section formalises the threat model for the GyberExperiment architecture, following the STRIDE methodology (Spoofing, Tampering, Repudiation, Information Disclosure, Denial of Service, Elevation of Privilege) and supplementing it with threats specific to decentralised systems.

#### 8.4.1. Threat Classification

Threats are classified at three levels: protocol (attacks on smart contracts and blockchain), governance (attacks on DAO mechanisms and voting), and economic (attacks on tokenomics and financial flows).

**Protocol threats:**

| Attack vector | Description | Mitigation | Status |
|---|---|---|---|
| Flash loan attack on governance | Attacker takes a flash loan, acquires GBR, votes for a malicious proposal, and repays the loan within a single transaction | Snapshot-at-proposal-time in Governor contract: voting weight is fixed at the time of proposal creation, not at voting time. Voting delay between creation and start of voting excludes the use of borrowed tokens | Implemented in Governor contract |
| Smart contract vulnerabilities | Exploitation of vulnerabilities (reentrancy, overflow, logic errors) | Multi-level audit: (1) static analysis (Slither, Mythril); (2) formal verification of invariants (Certora Prover); (3) AI audit pipeline (Octane Security, Sherlock AI); (4) Timelock + Gnosis Safe as additional barriers; (5) bug bounty programme | AI audit — target architecture; Timelock and Safe — implemented |
