| Oracle manipulation | Manipulation of price oracles to exploit contracts dependent on external data | TWAP (Time-Weighted Average Price) instead of spot price. Multiple data sources (Chainlink, Band Protocol). AI detection of anomalous price deviations | Target architecture |
| MEV (Maximal Extractable Value) | Sandwich attacks, front-running on user transactions | Use of MEV-protected RPC (Flashbots Protect), commit-reveal schemes for critical transactions, batch processing of votes | Target architecture |
| Social engineering on signers | Compromise of Gnosis Safe signer keys | Emergency multisig: pause only, not withdraw. M-of-N threshold scheme. Scheduled signer rotation. Hardware keys (Ledger/Trezor) mandatory | Implemented |

**Governance threats:**

| Attack vector | Description | Mitigation | Status |
|---|---|---|---|
| Governance capture | Accumulation of a significant volume of GBR to control voting | Supermajority for critical decisions. Timelock delay (48h minimum). Quadratic voting reduces weight of large holders. Reputational multipliers. AI monitoring of anomalous token accumulation | Partially implemented |
| Sybil attacks on G-Plan | Creation of fictitious accounts to receive rewards | GNN Sybil detection: interaction graph analysis. Proof of Personhood. Activity confirmation by higher-status participants. Activity Scoring (0–100) with minimum threshold | Manual verification — implemented; AI detection — target |
| Voter apathy | Low turnout (<5%) gives a small group disproportionate influence | Vote delegation (liquid democracy). AI summarisation of proposals reduces cognitive load. Reputational incentives for voting participation. Tiered governance: minor decisions automated; voting only on significant matters | Target architecture |
| Collusion | Secret collusion between participants to extract rent | Transparency of on-chain data. AI detection of suspicious voting patterns. Mandatory cooling period for repeat proposals by the same author. Public discussion prior to voting | Target architecture |

**Economic threats:**

| Attack vector | Description | Mitigation | Status |
|---|---|---|---|
| Death spiral (GBR) | Price decline → participant exodus → project contraction → further decline | Fixed supply. Buyback & burn from project revenues. x5 multiplier incentivises project completion. Two-tier DUNA treasury limits fiat losses. Stabilisation fund (Phase II) | Partial — requires empirical verification |
| Fund exhaustion (UnitManager) | Depletion of the 60% supply allocated for rewards | Adaptive rewards: Fund Monitoring contract tracks remaining balance and automatically adjusts payout size. Activity Scoring (multiplier 50–150%) prevents free-riding. Halving mechanism upon reaching thresholds (50%, 25%, 10% of remaining fund) | Target architecture |
| Liquidity fragmentation | Multiple token/GBR pools create illiquid markets | Liquidity consolidation through LP aggregator. Minimum pool size requirements for listing. Pools created only for projects that have passed the Implementation stage | Target architecture |
| Regulatory risk | Qualification of GBR as security (Howey test) | DUNA as nonprofit form: revenue is reinvested, not distributed. Buyback & burn — liquidity management mechanism, not dividend. Absence of ICO/IEO: tokens distributed through UnitManager for activity. Legal opinion from specialist firm | Target architecture; legal opinion — in progress |

#### 8.4.2. Howey Analysis: GBR Token as Utility

To determine whether GBR constitutes an "investment contract" (security), the four-part Howey test (SEC v. W.J. Howey Co., 1946) is applied:

1. **Investment of money** — GBR is not sold to investors; tokens are distributed through UnitManager as a reward for verified activity. A participant does not "invest" money but receives tokens for labour.

2. **In a common enterprise** — GyberExperiment is a common enterprise (common enterprise); this criterion is fulfilled.

3. **With an expectation of profit** — Participants receive GBR as compensation for work, not as an investment with an expectation of profit. An increase in the value of GBR is not guaranteed and is not the primary motivation for participation.

4. **Solely from the efforts of others** — The value of GBR depends on the collective efforts of all participants. Each participant makes their own contribution; there are no "promoters" upon whose efforts profit depends.

**Conclusion:** GBR conforms to the utility token model (analogous to ETH), rather than to a security. However, this qualification requires confirmation by a specialised legal opinion and may depend on jurisdiction.

#### 8.4.3. Tax Analysis for Participants

Participants in GyberExperiment who receive rewards in GBR through UnitManager may be subject to different tax regimes depending on jurisdiction:

- **United States:** GBR received for work qualifies as ordinary income at the time of receipt. The tax base is the fair market value of GBR at the time of receipt. Upon subsequent sale — capital gains/losses.
- **EU (general approach):** Analogously: income at the time of receipt + capital gains upon sale. Specific rules vary by country.
- **Through DUNA:** Participants receiving compensation as contractors of DUNA receive standard tax documents (1099 in the United States).

DUNA is not an employer of participants. All rewards are paid as compensation for services, not as employment income.

#### 8.4.4. Mitigation Plan: Prioritisation

```
Priority 1 (Critical — prior to launch):
  — Smart contract audit (UnitManager, Governor, Timelock)
  — Gnosis Safe deployment with M-of-N signers
  — Howey analysis by a law firm
  — Snapshot-at-proposal-time in Governor

Priority 2 (High — first 6 months):
  — AI audit pipeline (Slither + Mythril + Certora)
  — GNN Sybil detection for G-Plan
  — Fund Monitoring contract for UnitManager
  — Bug bounty programme

Priority 3 (Medium — first year):
  — Quadratic voting
  — AI monitoring of governance anomalies
  — Stabilisation fund
  — cadCAD simulation of tokenomics

Priority 4 (Low — Phase II+):
  — Formal verification of contracts
  — MEV protection
  — Cross-chain security
  — Federated learning for Sybil detection
```

### 8.5 Open Research Questions

The theory of CyberSocium generates a series of open questions, each of which represents an independent direction for academic research.

**8.5.1. Formal verification of macroeconomic properties.** Is it possible to formally prove that the GyberExperiment system possesses certain desirable macroeconomic properties (stability, equitable distribution, antifragility)? Existing approaches (cadCAD, TokenSPICE, Gauntlet) allow the modelling of specific scenarios but do not provide formal guarantees. The application of model checking and theorem proving to economic systems is a promising but underdeveloped direction.

**8.5.2. Optimal DAO parameters.** What should the optimal parameters of quorum, voting periods, and approval thresholds be for each DAO class? Empirical data from existing DAOs (MakerDAO: quorum approximately 10%, Compound: approximately 4%, Uniswap: approximately 4%) shows significant variation. Required: (a) collection of empirical data from GyberExperiment; (b) agent-based modelling (ABM) for parameter assessment; (c) adaptive mechanisms for automatic parameter self-correction.

**8.5.3. Inter-system interaction of CSCs.** How can multiple CyberSocial corporations interact with one another? What protocols are required for cross-CSC coordination? Does a "meta-SES" emerge — evolutionary selection between CSCs? And how does a CSC interact with traditional economic institutions (banks, exchanges, state regulators)?

**8.5.4. Long-term evolution.** How will the system evolve on the scale of decades? What emergent properties may arise from the interaction of hundreds of DAOs? Is there an attractor for the system — a stationary state towards which it tends? What are the conditions for its existence and stability?

**8.5.5. AI and the quality of democratic decisions.** How does the integration of AI systems into governance processes (AI summarisation, AI analysis of proposals, AI monitoring) affect the quality and democratic character of decisions? Is there a risk of "AI laundering" — a situation in which decisions are de facto made by AI, even though humans formally vote? How can the atrophy of collective judgement be measured and prevented?

**8.5.6. Scalability of PMIP.** The PMIP universal implementability theorem is mathematically sound, but ignores transaction costs, coordination losses, and the problem of attention (attention economy). At what N does the model break down? What is the empirical upper bound of PMIP efficiency?

**8.5.7. The initial bootstrap problem.** How can the "cold start" be overcome — the initial period when N is small and PMIP does not provide substantial resources? What is the minimum critical mass for CSC viability? Empirical data from the current experiment should provide an answer.

**8.5.8. Formalisation of SES as an evolutionary process.** Is it possible to formalise SES in the terms of evolutionary game theory (Maynard Smith, 1982)? Are there evolutionarily stable strategies (ESS) for agents in NFC? What is the convergence rate of SES towards an optimal distribution of resources?

**8.5.9. Legal compatibility of CSC.** Can a CSC function within the existing legal systems of various jurisdictions? DUNA resolves this problem for Wyoming, but how does a CSC interact with the legal systems of the EU (MiCA), Singapore (PSA), and Switzerland (DLT Act)?

**8.5.10. Measurement of social relevance.** SR(p) in the current model is binary (0 or 1). Is it possible to develop a continuous metric of social relevance that accounts not only for the number of SIC participants, but also for the intensity of their interest, their expertise, and the public significance of the problem that the project addresses?

### 8.6 GyberExperiment as a Controlled Socio-Economic Experiment

GyberExperiment is not simply a startup or a technological platform. It is a **controlled socio-economic experiment** — a conscious attempt to test the theoretical predictions of CyberSocium on a real system with real participants and real economic stakes. This section formalises the experimental methodology, its limitations, and its connection with the traditions of experimental economics.

#### 8.6.1. Methodological Tradition: From Laboratory Economics to Field Experiments

Experimental economics as a discipline was founded by Vernon Smith (Nobel Prize 2002) and Daniel Kahneman (Nobel Prize 2002). Smith demonstrated that laboratory experiments allow economic theories to be tested under controlled conditions; Kahneman showed that real behaviour systematically deviates from the predictions of rational choice theory. Esther Duflo and Abhijit Banerjee (Nobel Prize 2019) developed the approach of randomised controlled trials (RCT) for assessing the effectiveness of economic interventions "in the field."

GyberExperiment occupies an intermediate position between laboratory experiment and field study. It possesses properties of a controlled experiment: the system parameters (quorums, thresholds, reward formulas) are defined by the designer and can be modified; results are measurable (on-chain data provides full observability). But it also possesses properties of field research: participants are real people with real motivations; economic stakes are real (GBR has market value); the environment is not fully controlled (external shocks, regulatory changes, technical failures).

#### 8.6.2. Experimental Design

We formalise GyberExperiment as a series of nested experiments, each testing a specific hypothesis of CyberSocium theory:

```
Experimental design GyberExperiment:

  Experiment E1: Verification of PMIP
  ─────────────────────────────────
    Hypothesis H1: individual_cost(i,p) = O(1/|SIC(p)|)
    Observable variables: C(p), |SIC(p)|, individual_cost(i,p)
    Method: regression analysis of the dependence of individual_cost
      on |SIC| for all completed projects
    Counterfactual: comparison with analogous projects
      on traditional crowdfunding platforms
    Minimum sample: ≥30 completed projects
    Expected time: Phase II (2025-2026)

  Experiment E2: Hybrid VP vs. Token-Weighted Voting
  ─────────────────────────────────
    Hypothesis H2: Gini(VP_hybrid) < 0.7 × Gini(VP_token)
    Observable variables: VP distribution, voting
      outcomes, participation
    Method: quasi-experiment — parallel votes
      in two modes on the same cohort
    Counterfactual: voting on the same proposals
      with VP = GBR_staked (without square root)
    Minimum sample: ≥50 participants, ≥20 votes
    Expected time: Phase I-II (2025)

  Experiment E3: SES and evolutionary project selection
  ─────────────────────────────────
    Hypothesis H3: projects with f(p) > f̄ attract more
      resources (participants, GBR) in the next period
    Observable variables: f(p) (fitness), x(p) (resource
      share), Δx(p)/Δt (rate of change)
    Method: panel analysis of all projects
      with monthly observations
    Counterfactual: random allocation of resources
      (Monte Carlo simulation)
    Minimum sample: ≥20 active projects
    Expected time: Phase II-III (2026-2027)

  Experiment E4: Activity Score and motivation
  ─────────────────────────────────
    Hypothesis H4: introduction of Activity Score increases
      average participant activity by ≥30%
    Observable variables: on-chain activity,
      contribution rate, retention
    Method: before/after comparison + diff-in-diff
      (with a control group of participants unaffected
       by Activity Score)
    Counterfactual: period prior to introduction of Activity Score
    Minimum sample: ≥100 participants
    Expected time: Phase I (2025)

  Experiment E5: AI augmentation of governance
  ─────────────────────────────────
    Hypothesis H5: AI summariser increases voter
      participation by ≥20% and informed decision-making by ≥30%
    Observable variables: voter turnout, time to
      review proposal, decision quality (expert assessment)
    Method: A/B test (group with AI summariser vs.
      group without)
    Counterfactual: voting on identical proposals
      without AI summarisation
    Minimum sample: ≥50 participants in each group
    Expected time: Phase II (2026)
```

#### 8.6.3. Advantages of the On-Chain Experimental Environment

The blockchain as an experimental platform possesses unique properties unavailable in traditional laboratory or field experimental economics:

**Full observability.** Every transaction, vote, reward claim, LP Burn, and IPI transition is recorded in an immutable ledger. This eliminates measurement error, characteristic of survey data and self-reported metrics. A researcher can reconstruct the complete interaction history of *any* participant to the second.

**Verifiability.** Any researcher (not only the GyberExperiment team) can reproduce the analysis using public on-chain data. This implements the principle of scientific reproducibility (*reproducibility*) at the architectural level — not as a voluntary commitment of the researcher, but as a technical property of the system.

**Impossibility of retroactive falsification.** Data recorded on-chain cannot be altered after the fact. This eliminates p-hacking, selective reporting, and other forms of scientific misconduct identified in the "reproducibility crisis" of the social sciences (Ioannidis, 2005; Open Science Collaboration, 2015).

**Real stakes.** Unlike laboratory experiments with symbolic rewards ($5–50), participants in GyberExperiment operate with real assets (GBR with market value). This ensures ecological validity — the experimental results reflect real behaviour, not laboratory artefacts.

#### 8.6.4. Limitations of the Experimental Methodology

We acknowledge fundamental limitations of GyberExperiment as an experiment:

**Selection bias.** Participants in GyberExperiment are a self-selected group: people who have consciously chosen to participate in a cryptoeconomic system. They are not a representative sample of the general population. Consequently, the results of the experiment cannot be automatically extrapolated to an arbitrary group of people. Mitigation: as NFC grows and becomes geographically diversified (KPI: Diversity Index), selection bias decreases but is not eliminated.

**Absence of a control group.** A true randomised experiment requires a control group not subjected to the "treatment" (CyberSocium). This is practically impossible: one cannot randomly assign people to "decentralised" and "centralised" groups with identical tasks. Mitigation: quasi-experimental methods (difference-in-differences, regression discontinuity, instrumental variables) allow an approximation of causal identification.

**Hawthorne effect.** Participants who know they are participating in an experiment may alter their behaviour (the Hawthorne effect). In GyberExperiment this effect is attenuated: (a) on-chain data records real actions (not self-reports); (b) as participants become accustomed to the system, the "novelty effect" diminishes; (c) financial incentives (GBR rewards) ensure that rational behaviour dominates over the desire to "make an impression."

**Confounding variables.** External factors (the crypto market, regulatory changes, technical failures) affect results in parallel with the variables of interest. Mitigation: cadCAD modelling allows the separation of endogenous dynamics from exogenous shocks; time series of on-chain data allow the use of event study methodology for the identification of causal effects.

**Ethical considerations.** GyberExperiment involves real people with real economic stakes. This creates ethical obligations: informed consent (participants know that the system is experimental), minimisation of risks (contract audits, Fund Monitoring, Timelock), and the right to exit (a participant may withdraw their stake and leave the system at any time). DUNA as a legal wrapper provides limited liability for participants, reducing personal economic risk.

#### 8.6.5. Open Data and Academic Collaboration

CyberSocium adheres to the principle of **Open Science** — all experimental data (with the exception of private off-chain data of participants, protected under A3) is published in the public domain:

- On-chain data is accessible via BSCScan/block explorer;
- Aggregated metrics (KPIs from Section 9.5) are published daily on DSP;
- cadCAD models and simulations are available on GitHub (AGPL-3.0);
- Experimental protocols (E1–E5) and pre-registrations are published prior to the commencement of the experiment;
- Results of analyses are submitted to peer-reviewed journals (Journal of Institutional Economics, Cryptoeconomic Systems, Journal of Political Economy).

This openness reflects Axiom A2 (transparency): the scientific value of GyberExperiment is determined not only by its practical results, but also by its contribution to the common knowledge of decentralised socio-economic systems. Even if the specific configuration of GyberExperiment proves to be suboptimal, the experimental data will enable other researchers and projects to learn from its experience — in full accordance with the epistemological position of Section 1.6.

---
## 9. Development Roadmap: From Experiment to Ecosystem

The implementation of CyberSocium follows the principle of **progressive decentralization** — beginning with a minimally viable prototype under the coordination of a narrow group of initiators and progressively transferring control to the community as it grows and institutional mechanisms mature. This approach is driven by two factors: first, the bootstrapping problem, wherein PMIP does not yet provide sufficient resources; second, the necessity of empirically calibrating parameters (quorums, thresholds, rewards) prior to their codification in autonomous smart contracts.

Each phase is defined by: (a) priority objectives, (b) key performance indicators (KPIs), (c) transition criteria for the subsequent phase, and (d) risks and mitigations.

### 9.1 Phase I: Foundation (2024–2025)

Phase I constitutes a period of "managed genesis": the creation of the legal, technical, and social infrastructure necessary for ecosystem viability. The paradox of the initial phase lies in the fact that a decentralized system inevitably begins with a centralized act — just as Bitcoin was launched by a single individual (Nakamoto) and Ethereum by a Foundation. CyberSocium acknowledges this paradox and formalizes it through the mechanism of Progressive Decentralization: every action taken by the initiators creates infrastructure that renders their own influence progressively less necessary.

Key dependencies of Phase I: DUNA → bank account → fiat operations → payment of auditors and infrastructure; GBR token → PancakeSwap LP → initial liquidity → economic participation; DSP (MVP) → first projects → formation of SIC → empirical data for PMIP calibration; UnitManager v1 → first rewards → participant incentives → community growth. These dependencies define the critical path and task prioritization.

Special attention is devoted to security auditing: prior to deploying significant funds into smart contracts, they must undergo a minimum of two independent audits — an AI audit (Slither + Mythril + Octane within the AiC pipeline) and a manual audit by a specialized firm. This is not merely best practice, but a consequence of Axiom A2 (transparency): the security of the system must be verifiable by any participant.

```
Priorities:
  — DUNA registration (Wyoming):
    Filing → BOI report → Operating Agreement
    → Bank account → EIN
  — Deployment of Governor + Timelock + Gnosis Safe:
    Multisig 3-of-5 for Core participants
    Timelock delay: 48 hours
  — DSP launch (MVP):
    — Participant profiles (wallet = identifier)
    — Project spaces
    — Basic communication (forums, chats)
    — GitHub integration for Code DAO
  — UnitManager v1: reward accrual
    for initial participants based on a fixed schedule
  — G-Plan v1: basic task management
    with manual activity verification
  — Formation of the community core:
    Core (≥1), ArchDev (≥3), LeadDev (≥5), Dev (≥15)
  — Publication of CyberSocium Foundation v2.0
    as the foundational white paper
  — GBR token launch on BSC:
    BEP-20, PancakeSwap LP, initial liquidity
  — Smart contract audit: minimum 2 independent
    auditors (AI audit + manual)

Key Performance Indicators (KPIs):
  — ≥50 active participants (Activity Score ≥ 20)
  — ≥5 projects at the Discussion/Formation stage
  — DUNA registered and operational
  — DSP functional with ≥100 registered users
  — GBR listed on PancakeSwap with ≥$50K TVL
  — ≥3 publications / presentations in relevant communities
  — UnitManager: ≥100 successful claimReward() transactions

Phase II Transition Criteria:
  — All Phase I KPIs met
  — Contract audit completed without critical vulnerabilities
  — DUNA operational: bank account, BOI, EIN
  — Community has demonstrated sustained organic growth
    (≥10% participant growth per month for ≥3 months)

Risks:
  — Key-person risk: dependence on 1–3 initiators
    Mitigation: documentation of all processes, cross-training
  — Regulatory risk: uncertainty regarding GBR classification
    Mitigation: Howey analysis by a legal firm
  — Low engagement: <50 participants
    Mitigation: early participant programs (enhanced rewards)
```

### 9.2 Phase II: Growth (2025–2026)

Phase II marks the transition from a minimally viable product to a fully functional ecosystem. Whereas Phase I creates infrastructure, Phase II tests the viability hypothesis: is the ecosystem capable of attracting a critical mass of participants (≥500) and generating genuine economic value (≥3 revenue-generating projects)?

The central event is the launch of the GyberNet testnet — the first step toward sovereign blockchain infrastructure. The RPoS consensus (Reputational Proof-of-Stake) undergoes live testing: 10+ validators, thousands of transactions, real DAO votes. Testnet data are critically important for calibrating mainnet parameters (Phase III).

Simultaneously, the first Commerce DAO projects take shape — projects that have completed the full IPI cycle (Idea → Discussion → Formation → Implementation) and are generating real revenue. This constitutes a test of the key mechanism: LP Burn as proof-of-commitment, milestone voting, and revenue distribution (CSC 10%, AG Reward). Each Commerce DAO project is a micro-experiment verifying a distinct aspect of CyberSocium theory.

AiC moves beyond the prototype stage: the AI audit pipeline enters production, and the AI proposal summarizer constitutes the first implementation of Axiom A8 in governance. Empirical data on the influence of AI on decision quality form the basis for RQ8 (Section 10.4).

International community expansion (DSP localization into a minimum of 3 languages) tests the scalability of CyberSocium beyond a single linguistic community — a critically important condition for the global NFC underlying PMIP.

```
Priorities:
  — DSP v2: fully functional platform
    with IPFS storage and end-to-end encryption
    — ActivityPub compatibility for federation
    — DID (Decentralized Identifiers) for identification
    — Verifiable Credentials for skill attestation
  — GyberNet testnet: test network
    with RPoS consensus and 10+ validators
  — First Commerce DAO projects:
    LQD, SAPP, PowerSwapMeta
    — Due diligence process for each project
    — Escrow contracts with milestone voting
  — AiC: formation of research groups,
    commencement of infrastructure work (Circuit 1)
    — AI audit pipeline: Slither + Mythril + Octane
    — AI proposal summarizer (prototype)
  — Reputation system v1: integration of
    G-Plan + UnitManager + DAO voting
    — Automatic on-chain Activity Score
    — Hybrid Voting Power formula in GBRVotingWrapper
  — International community expansion:
    DSP localization (EN, ES, ZH at minimum)
    partnerships with university laboratories
  — Fund Monitoring contract: tracking
    UnitManager remaining balance,
    automatic reward adjustment
  — Bug bounty program via Immunefi

Key Performance Indicators (KPIs):
  — ≥500 active participants (Activity Score ≥ 20)
  — ≥20 projects at various IPI stages
  — ≥3 projects generating revenue (Operation stage)
  — GyberNet testnet operating stably (uptime >99%)
  — GBR TVL on PancakeSwap ≥$500K
  — ≥10 GyberNet testnet validators
  — AiC Circuit 1: AI audit pipeline in production
  — ≥1 academic publication on CyberSocium
  — DUNA annual report filed on time
  — ≥3 Commerce DAO projects approved and funded

Phase III Transition Criteria:
  — All Phase II KPIs met
  — GyberNet testnet: 3+ months of stable operation
  — Commerce DAO: at least 1 project at the Operation stage
  — Hybrid Voting Power tested across ≥50 votes
  — AI audit: ≥100 PRs analyzed automatically
  — Community: ≥500 participants, of whom ≥50 Dev+

Risks:
  — Slow growth: <500 participants
    Mitigation: partnerships with DAO ecosystems,
    grant programs for developers
  — Commerce DAO projects fail to generate revenue
    Mitigation: due diligence, milestone voting,
    cadCAD modeling prior to launch
  — GyberNet testnet is unstable
    Mitigation: extend the testing period,
    engage external consensus auditors
```

### 9.3 Phase III: Scaling (2026–2028)

Phase III marks the transition of GyberExperiment from an experimental platform to a fully operational ecosystem. The key event is the launch of GyberNet mainnet — a dedicated blockchain network based on Reputational Proof-of-Stake (RPoS), which liberates the ecosystem from dependence on BSC and establishes sovereignty over transactional infrastructure in accordance with Axiom A1 (Decentralization). In parallel, GyberComputer is launched — a distributed computing network critically required for the realization of AiC (Circuit 3) and providing the ecosystem with its own computational resources.

At this phase, the four-class DAO taxonomy must demonstrate its viability at scale: Social DAO governs a community of thousands of participants; Code DAO coordinates the development of a distributed codebase; Commerce DAO finances and curates dozens of projects; Economic DAO manages tokenomics under real market dynamics. The empirical data of this phase enable verification of hypotheses H1–H3 from the research program (Section 2.6.3).

A principally important aspect is Progressive Decentralization: the transfer of control from a narrow group of initiators to the community. By the end of Phase III, Core participants must not control more than 50% of voting power, and all critical functions (UnitManager, Fund Monitoring) must operate autonomously, without manual intervention. This constitutes the transition from "managed decentralization" (bootstrap) to "self-governed decentralization" (target state).

```
Priorities:
  — GyberNet mainnet: full-scale network
    with cross-chain bridges (BSC, Ethereum)
    — RPoS consensus with ≥50 validators
    — Single-slot finality (3 seconds)
    — Subsidized gas for DAO votes
  — GyberComputer v1: distributed
    computing network
    — Kubernetes + custom scheduler (Rust)
    — Proof-of-Computation verification
    — GPU nodes for AI training (AiC)
    — Federated Learning infrastructure
  — AiC: first trained models,
    launch of AI services (Circuit 3)
    — GNN Sybil detection in production
    — cadCAD simulation for each Economic DAO
      proposal
    — AI Governance Agents: summarizer + simulator
  — Cross-chain integration:
    BSC ↔ GyberNet ↔ Ethereum bridges (IBC-like)
    Research into TON and Solana bridges
  — Liquid democracy: vote delegation
    within the four-class DAO structure
    — Partial delegation (per DAO class)
    — Automatic re-delegation upon inactivity
  — Formalization of inter-community protocols:
    — Inter-CSC communication standard
    — Pilot partnership with 2–3 external DAOs
  — Storage decentralization:
    — Full migration to IPFS/Filecoin
    — Incentivized storage on GyberComputer
  — Progressive Decentralization:
    — Transfer of Gnosis Safe from Core to
      an expanded set of signers (ArchDev+)
    — Autonomous contracts: UnitManager,
      Fund Monitoring require no manual intervention
    — Automatic on-chain status degradation

Key Performance Indicators (KPIs):
  — ≥5,000 active participants
  — ≥100 projects at various IPI stages
  — ≥10 projects generating revenue
  — GyberComputer: ≥100 nodes, ≥50 GPU nodes
  — AiC: ≥3 AI models in production
  — GyberNet mainnet: uptime >99.9%, ≥50 validators
  — GBR total TVL (all chains) ≥$5M
  — ≥5 academic publications on CyberSocium
  — ≥2 inter-community partnerships with external DAOs
  — Governance participation rate ≥15%
    (of total active participants)

Phase IV Transition Criteria:
  — GyberNet mainnet stable for ≥12 months
  — Progressive decentralization: Core participants
    do not control >50% voting power
  — AiC: all three circuits operational
  — Ecosystem is self-sustaining: project revenues
    cover DUNA operating costs
  — Community has demonstrated capacity
    for self-governance without initiator intervention
```

### 9.4 Phase IV: Maturity (2028+)

Phase IV is not so much a conclusion as a transition to a steady state. GyberExperiment ceases to be an "experiment" and becomes a functioning system — a cybersocial corporation in the full sense of Definition 3.2.2: CSC = ⟨N, R, P, G, M, E⟩, wherein each component of the six-tuple functions autonomously and is verifiable on-chain.

The central objective of Phase IV is to demonstrate that CyberSocium is not merely viable, but surpasses traditional institutions on key metrics: efficiency of resource allocation (Pareto-optimality, measured through cadCAD), fairness (Gini index for GBR distribution < 0.5, key-person risk ≤ 5%), and resilience (resistance to external shocks). These metrics provide a falsifiable test of the theoretical claims advanced in Sections 3–4.

Inter-community economics (inter-CSC protocols) move to the foreground: if CyberSocium proves viable, other communities will inevitably create their own CSCs, and the question of their interaction will become central. Meta-SES (RQ9, Section 10.4) — evolutionary selection among CSCs — is the theoretical framework for this interaction: CSCs with more efficient institutional designs attract more participants, creating selection pressure at the institutional level. This is Darwinian evolution for organizations — but with the possibility of deliberate mutation design (through FRP and DAO voting).

Full realization of Axiom A8 (Cognitive Augmentation) means that AI ceases to be an auxiliary tool and becomes an integral participant in governance: AI agents summarize proposals, simulate consequences, optimize resource allocation, and detect Sybil attacks — all while preserving the principle of division (AI recommends, humans decide). Federated Learning provides on-device inference for all DSP participants, eliminating dependence on centralized AI providers.

```
Priorities:
  — Full autonomy: all critical
    functions governed by smart contracts
    and DAO voting without manual override
    — Gnosis Safe: ≥9-of-15 signers
    — Timelock: all significant changes pass
      through Governor + Timelock
    — UnitManager: fully autonomous
  — Inter-community economics: protocols
    for interaction among cybersocial
    corporations
    — Inter-CSC bridge: resource exchange
    — Meta-SES: evolutionary selection among CSCs
    — Cross-CSC DAO: joint proposals
  — AI governance: full realization of A8
    — AI agents for all four DAO classes
    — AI orchestration of GyberComputer
    — AI predictor of SR for SES
    — AI coordinator of PMIP
    — Federated Learning: on-device inference
      for all DSP participants
    — AI ethics governance through Social DAO
  — Full realization of the eight axioms (A1–A8)
    at the scale of a global ecosystem
    — Each axiom verifiable on-chain
    — Annual axiom compliance audit
  — Influence on regulatory frameworks:
    — Promotion of DUNA-like legislation
      in the EU, Singapore, Switzerland
    — Participation in FATF, MiCA working groups
    — Development of model legislation
      for jurisdictions recognizing DAOs
  — Academic recognition:
    — Publication of results in peer-reviewed
      journals (Journal of Institutional Economics,
      Cryptoeconomic Systems, etc.)
    — Collaborative research programs
      with universities
    — Open datasets for researchers
      in DAO governance

Maturity Metrics:
  — ≥50,000 active participants
  — ≥1,000 projects at various stages
  — GyberComputer: ≥1,000 nodes
  — AiC: ≥10 AI models, ≥3 commercial AI products
  — GBR total TVL ≥$50M
  — DUNA recognized as a model structure
    in a minimum of 3 jurisdictions
  — ≥20 academic publications
  — Governance participation rate ≥25%
  — Gini index for GBR distribution <0.5
    (moderate inequality)
  — Key-person risk: ≤5%
    (no single participant controls >5% voting power)

Vision:
  MacroeconomicDAO demonstrates that
  a decentralized, self-governed,
  transparent economic community
  is capable not only of competing
  with traditional economic institutions,
  but of surpassing them in efficiency,
  fairness, and resilience.

  CyberSocium ceases to be an experiment
  and becomes a validated model —
  not the only one, but one of the viable
  alternatives available to humanity
  for organizing global economic
  interaction.
```

### 9.5 Success Metrics: The Ecosystem KPI System

A roadmap without measurable success criteria is nothing more than a list of aspirations. The present section defines the system of key performance indicators (KPIs) that enables objective assessment of GyberExperiment ecosystem progress and well-founded decision-making regarding phase transitions. KPIs are organized along four dimensions: social (participants and community), economic (tokenomics and finance), technical (infrastructure and security), and governance (management and decentralization).

#### 9.5.1. Social KPIs: Community Growth and Quality

Quantitative community growth is a necessary but insufficient condition for success. CyberSocium, unlike traditional platforms, optimizes not for "number of registered accounts" (a metric easily susceptible to Sybil attacks and statistical manipulation), but for *quality of participation*, measured through activity and contribution.

```
Social KPIs:

  NFC (Number of Full Citizens):
    Definition: participants with MemberStatus ≥ Unit(1)
    and Activity Score ≥ θ_min over the preceding 30 days
    Phase I target: NFC ≥ 50
    Phase II target: NFC ≥ 500
    Phase III target: NFC ≥ 5,000
    Phase IV target: NFC ≥ 50,000

  DAU/MAU ratio (Daily Active / Monthly Active Users):
    Definition: ratio of daily active wallets
    to monthly active wallets on DSP
    Target: ≥ 0.30 (benchmark: Discord ~0.25, Telegram ~0.35)
    Significance: durability of engagement, not "register and leave"

  MemberStatus Distribution:
    Target: pyramidal structure
    Core: 1–3 | ArchDev: 5–15 | LeadDev: 15–50 |
    Dev: 50–200 | Unit: 200+ | None: unlimited
    Red flag: more than 80% of participants at the Unit level
    (= insufficient vertical mobility)
    Red flag: inverted pyramid
    (= status inflation)

  Retention Rate:
    Definition: percentage of participants with Activity Score > 0
    90 days after first transaction
    Target: ≥ 40% (benchmark: DeFi protocols ~15–25%)
    Significance: co-metric for measuring the quality
    of onboarding and the value of participation

  SIC Formation Rate:
    Definition: number of new SIC(p) created per 30 days
    Target: ≥ 3 (Phase I), ≥ 15 (Phase II), ≥ 50 (Phase III)
    Significance: SIC = the atomic unit of collective action;
    without SIC — no PMIP effect

  Diversity Index:
    Definition: Herfindahl-Hirschman Index (HHI)
    by participant jurisdictions
    Target: HHI < 0.25 (no dominance by a single jurisdiction)
    Significance: realization of A6 (inclusivity); geographic
    concentration = risk factor
```

#### 9.5.2. Economic KPIs: Tokenomics Sustainability

Economic metrics measure the sustainability of the incentive system and the ecosystem's capacity to generate and distribute value.

```
Economic KPIs:

  Fund Solvency Ratio:
    Definition: UnitManager.fund_balance / projected_rewards_12m
    Target: ≥ 2.0 (twofold reserve against obligations)
    Red flag: < 1.0 (= obligations exceed available funds;
    trigger for Halving)
    Monitoring: daily, automatically via Fund Monitor

  GBR Velocity:
    Definition: transaction_volume / circulating_supply
    Target: 0.5–3.0 (benchmark: ETH ~1.5–2.5)
    Red flag: < 0.1 (= token is not being used, a "dead" asset)
    Red flag: > 10 (= purely speculative use)

  LP Burn Commitment Rate:
    Definition: number of LP Burn events / number of
    Commerce DAO proposals
    Target: ≥ 50% of proposals with LP Burn (proof of commitment)
    Significance: LP Burn = an irreversible signal of seriousness
    of project intent

  PMIP Efficiency:
    Definition: individual_cost(i,p) = C(p) / |SIC(p)|
    for realized projects
    Theoretical bound: O(1/n)
    Practical target: individual_cost < $10 for projects
    with C(p) ≤ $50,000 at |SIC(p)| ≥ 5000
    Significance: the primary indicator of PMIP operability;
    if individual_cost does not decrease as N grows — the theory
    is not confirmed

  Burn/Mint Ratio:
    Definition: GBR_burned / GBR_minted for a given period
    Phase I target: 0.1–0.3 (supply growth for bootstrapping)
    Phase III+ target: ≥ 0.8 (approaching a deflationary model)
    Significance: tokenomics sustainability requires a balanced
    emission/burn cycle
```

#### 9.5.3. Technical KPIs: Reliability and Security

```
Technical KPIs:

  Smart Contract Security Score:
    Definition: (1 - critical_vulnerabilities / total_audited_functions)
    Target: ≥ 0.999 (zero critical vulnerabilities in production)
    Monitoring: AI audit (Slither + Mythril) at each deployment
    + manual audit every 6 months

  System Uptime:
    Definition: percentage of time DSP + GPROD are available
    Target: ≥ 99.5% (permissible downtime ~44 hours/year)
    Monitoring: health checks every 30 seconds

  Transaction Finality Time:
    BSC (Phase I–II): < 5 seconds
    GyberNet (Phase III+): < 2 seconds (single-slot finality)

  Test Coverage:
    Definition: line coverage for each module
    Target: CONTRACTS ≥ 95%, GPROD ≥ 80%, DSP Client ≥ 70%
    Significance: a baseline requirement for safe iterative development

  Bug Bounty Payout Rate:
    Definition: total bug bounty payouts / TVL
    Target: 0.1–1% of TVL allocated to bug bounty pool
    Significance: economic incentive for white-hat researchers
```

#### 9.5.4. Governance KPIs: Decentralization and Participation

Governance KPIs are the most specific to CyberSocium, as governance is precisely what distinguishes a CSC from an ordinary DeFi protocol.

```
Governance KPIs:

  Voter Participation Rate:
    Definition: unique_voters / eligible_voters for each
    DAO class
    Target: ≥ 30% (benchmark: on-chain governance ~5–15%
    for the majority of DAOs)
    Significance: low participation = de facto oligarchy;
    CyberSocium sets an ambitious target of 30%+

  Gini Coefficient of Voting Power:
    Definition: Gini coefficient for VP distribution after Hybrid formula
    Target: Gini(VP) < 0.60 (benchmark: token-weighted DAO ~0.85–0.95)
    Significance: the primary indicator of Hybrid VP effectiveness;
    if Gini(VP) ≈ Gini(token_holdings) → the formula is not working

  Proposal Diversity Index:
    Definition: Shannon entropy of proposals distribution
    across DAO classes per quarter
    Target: H > 1.5 (out of a maximum of log₂(4) ≈ 2.0)
    Red flag: H < 0.5 (= one class monopolizes
    the governance agenda)

  Nakamoto Coefficient:
    Definition: minimum number of participants capable
    of controlling 51% of VP
    Phase I target: ≥ 3 (= Gnosis Safe multisig)
    Phase II target: ≥ 10
    Phase III+ target: ≥ 50
    Significance: the primary metric of decentralization; growth of
    Nakamoto coefficient = progress of Progressive Decentralization

  Cross-Class Proposal Success Rate:
    Definition: percentage of cross-class proposals
    approved by all affected DAO classes
    Target: 40–70%
    Red flag: < 20% (= inter-class coordination is not functioning)
    Red flag: > 90% (= rubber-stamping, absence of
    genuine deliberation)

  Decision Time (average time to decision):
    Target Social DAO: < 72 hours
    Target Code DAO: < 48 hours
    Target Commerce DAO: < 120 hours
    Target Economic DAO: < 168 hours
    Significance: balance between speed and quality of deliberation
```

#### 9.5.5. Aggregate Metric: Ecosystem Health Score

For operational monitoring, it is appropriate to aggregate the key KPIs into a single composite indicator — the **Ecosystem Health Score (EHS)** — computed as the weighted mean of normalized indicators:

```
EHS = w₁ × norm(NFC) + w₂ × norm(Fund_Solvency) +
      w₃ × norm(Security_Score) + w₄ × norm(Voter_Participation) +
      w₅ × norm(Retention) + w₆ × norm(SIC_Formation)

where:
  norm(x) = min(x / target(x), 1.0)  — normalization to [0, 1]
  w₁...w₆ — weights determined by Economic DAO
  (defaults: w₁ = 0.20, w₂ = 0.20, w₃ = 0.15,
   w₄ = 0.20, w₅ = 0.15, w₆ = 0.10)

Interpretation:
  EHS ≥ 0.80: Healthy ecosystem (green zone)
  0.50 ≤ EHS < 0.80: Requires attention (yellow zone)
  EHS < 0.50: Critical condition (red zone)
  Trigger: EHS < 0.50 for three consecutive days → automatic
  creation of an Emergency Proposal in MacroeconomicDAO
```

EHS is published on DSP in real time (updated every 24 hours) and is accessible via the GPROD API. The transparency of EHS realizes Axiom A2: every participant can view the state of the ecosystem and verify the calculation.

---

## 10. Conclusion

### 10.1 Summary: From Problem to Solution

The present document has set forth the theoretical foundations and architecture of cybersocial economics — a new interdisciplinary field studying the regularities of the transition of the global socio-economic mechanism toward decentralized, self-governed, cybersocial forms of organization.

We have demonstrated that:

1. **The problem is real and systemic:** concentration of economic power, appropriation of data, opacity of governance, and inefficiency in resource allocation are not isolated deficiencies but structural properties of centralized economic institutions ill-suited to the reality of the era of supercommunication. The gap between the technological possibilities of coordination (billions of connected people) and the institutional reality (decisions made by boards of dozens) defines the central contradiction of our epoch.

2. **A decentralized alternative is formalizable:** through the concepts of the cybersocial corporation (CSC), the Principle of Minimal Individual Participation (PMIP), socio-economic selection (SES), the Idea-Project-Implementation model (IPI), and the Fork Resolution Protocol (FRP), we have provided a rigorous description of the mechanisms of decentralized economic interaction. Each of these mechanisms is formalized mathematically, possesses demonstrable properties, and is linked to a specific technical implementation.

3. **Governance can be simultaneously decentralized and efficient:** the four-class DAO taxonomy (Social, Code, Commerce, Economic) with the coordinating role of MacroeconomicDAO demonstrates that different types of decisions can be processed by specialized, optimized mechanisms without recourse to a centralized hierarchy. Hybrid Voting Power (VP = √(GBR_staked) × ActivityMultiplier × StatusBonus) resolves the plutocracy problem inherent in the majority of existing DAOs.

4. **Practical implementation is feasible:** GyberExperiment, its applied ecosystem (DSP, GyberNet, GyberComputer, G-Plan, AiC), and its legal wrapper (DUNA, Wyoming) constitute a concrete, functioning implementation of the theoretical constructs described. Five modules (DSP Client, GPROD, CONTRACTS, Supabase, DDP) provide a full stack — from user interface to blockchain layer.

5. **AI is a precondition for realizing the full potential of CyberSocium:** Axiom A8 (Cognitive Augmentation) and its institutional realization through AiC transform the fundamental limitations of direct democracy — cognitive overload and coordination costs — from intractable problems into engineering challenges. Without AI — an elegant theory with practical scalability limits. With AI — a functioning system at planetary scale.

6. **The mathematical corollary of PMIP — "Millions are Billions" — is not a utopian slogan but a formal proposition:** given a sufficiently large number of NFC participants, the threshold cost of social relevance is overcome for a project of any cost. This means that a cybersocial corporation is, in principle, capable of implementing projects of any scale — without centralization of capital or power.

7. **The DUNA legal wrapper provides a bridge between the decentralized and traditional worlds:** the Wyoming Decentralized Unincorporated Nonprofit Association (W.S. § 17-32, 2024) provides a legal framework within which a DAO can interact with banks, regulators, and counterparties while preserving the internal logic of decentralization. The two-tier treasury (on-chain + DUNA treasury) minimizes regulatory and financial risks.

### 10.2 Architecture of Contributions: What This Work Proposes That Is Novel

For clarity of positioning, we enumerate the original contributions of the present document, distinguishing it from the existing literature:

**Theoretical contributions:**

| Contribution | Novelty | Related works |
|---|---|---|
| 8 CyberSocium axioms (A1–A8) | First formal axiomatic system for CSC design; A8 (Cognitive Augmentation) is an original axiom with no analogues in the literature | Ostrom (8 design principles, 1990), Buterin (cryptoeconomics, 2014) |
| PMIP (Principle of Minimal Individual Participation) | Formal model with proven properties (universal realizability, scale inversion, antifragility) | Benkler (commons-based peer production, 2006), Weyl & Posner (Radical Markets, 2018) |
| SES (Socio-Economic Selection) | Formalization as an evolutionary game with proven Pareto-optimality and evolutionary stability of cooperation | Maynard Smith (ESS, 1982), Page (diversity theorem, 2007) |
| IPI (Idea-Project-Implementation) | Formalization of the project lifecycle as a finite automaton linked to DAO votes | Agile methodology, Stage-Gate (Cooper, 1990) |
| FRP (Fork Resolution Protocol) | Formal conflict resolution protocol for decentralized systems via on-chain arbitration | Kleros (decentralized arbitration), Aragon Court |
| Four-class DAO taxonomy | First DAO typology based on functional differentiation of decision types | Aragon (general-purpose DAO), Colony (task-based) |
| CAS model of CyberSocium | Formal description of CSC as a complex adaptive system with identified attractors and feedback loops | Holland (CAS, 1992), Kauffman (NK model, 1993) |

**Architectural contributions:**

| Contribution | Novelty |
|---|---|
| Hybrid Voting Power | Combination of quadratic voting, reputation, and status in a single formula |
| LP Burn as proof-of-commitment | Liquidity burning rather than staking as a project initiation mechanism |
| Two-tier DUNA treasury | On-chain + off-chain finances under unified DAO governance |
| AiC as institutional AI | AI integrated into the DAO structure with a formalized principle of division |
| MemberStatus hierarchy (6 levels) | Formalized meritocratic hierarchy with on-chain management |
| Reputational PoS (RPoS) | Modified consensus accounting for ecosystem reputation |

### 10.3 Limitations of This Work

We acknowledge the following limitations of the present document:

1. **Empirical verification is absent.** All theoretical models (PMIP, SES, CAS) remain mathematical constructions that have not undergone empirical testing against real data. GyberExperiment is designed as a site for such verification, but results will not be available before the completion of Phase II (2026).

2. **Parameters are not calibrated.** The specific numerical parameters (quorums, voting thresholds, reward sizes, slashing conditions) have been chosen on the basis of theoretical considerations and analysis of existing DAOs, but have not been optimized for the specific GyberExperiment ecosystem. Calibration through cadCAD modeling and empirical data is a Phase II objective.

3. **Scalability has not been proven.** The PMIP universal realizability theorem is mathematically correct but ignores transaction costs, coordination losses, and the attention problem. The effective upper bound on CSC scalability is an open question.

4. **Bootstrapping dependence.** The current phase is characterized by significant key-person risk and dependence on a narrow group of initiators. This is a deliberate compromise of the bootstrap phase, but it creates fragility that contradicts the stated principle of decentralization.

5. **Regulatory uncertainty.** DUNA is a relatively new legal form (the statute was enacted in 2024), without substantial case law or precedents. Its resilience under conditions of regulatory change has not been tested.

### 10.4 Directions for Future Research

The theory of CyberSocium opens several promising directions for academic research:

**Short-term (1–2 years):**

*RQ1: Empirical verification of PMIP.* The central question: is the theoretical O(1/n) convergence property of individual contribution confirmed by real-world data? Methodology: collection of data on all SIC formations in GyberExperiment Phase I; statistical analysis of the dependence of individual_cost(i) on |SIC(p)|; comparison of the observed distribution with the theoretical one. Expected outcomes: (a) confirmation or refutation of O(1/n) convergence; (b) identification of practical deviations from the theoretical model (coordination costs, free-riding, heterogeneity bias); (c) calibration of parameters for the cadCAD model. Publication target: peer-reviewed journal (Journal of Institutional Economics or Cryptoeconomic Systems).

*RQ2: cadCAD modeling of GBR tokenomics.* Construction of an agent-based model of GBR tokenomics using the cadCAD framework (BlockScience). Scenario modeling: (a) organic growth (10% per month), (b) stagnation (0%), (c) crisis (−20% participants), (d) speculative shock (5× increase in GBR price). For each scenario: analysis of the resilience of incentive mechanisms (Activity Score, MemberStatus degradation, LP Burn); identification of critical thresholds; recommendations for parameter calibration. Methodological contribution: an open cadCAD framework for modeling four-class DAO systems, available for use by other projects.

*RQ3: Hybrid Voting Power vs. token-weighted voting.* Quasi-experimental design: parallel conduct of votes on identical proposals in two modes (Hybrid VP and token-weighted) on a cohort of ≥50 participants. Metrics: (a) Gini index of voting power distribution; (b) correlation between stake size and vote outcome; (c) subjective participant satisfaction (survey); (d) Pareto efficiency of outcomes (expert assessment). Hypothesis H1: Hybrid VP reduces Gini voting power by ≥30% compared to token-weighted voting at comparable decision quality.

*RQ4: Effectiveness of AI audit of smart contracts.* Quantitative assessment of the AI audit pipeline (Slither + Mythril + Octane): percentage of detected vulnerabilities (recall), percentage of false positives (precision), detection time vs. manual audit. Benchmark: use of known vulnerabilities from the Immunefi bug bounty database and SWC Registry. Practical question: at what recall/precision level can AI audit replace (rather than supplement) manual audit?

**Medium-term (3–5 years):**

*RQ5: Formal verification of CSC through model checking.* Application of formal verification tools (TLA+, Alloy, SPIN) to verify macroscopic properties of CSC: (a) absence of deadlock in the four-class DAO system; (b) guarantee of FRP termination within a finite number of steps; (c) impossibility of accumulating >50% voting power by a single agent under a functioning Activity Score; (d) preservation of fund solvency under given emission/burn parameters. Methodological novelty: the first application of model checking to the full architecture of a DAO (rather than only to individual smart contracts). Expected outcome: formal safety and liveness guarantees for key CSC subsystems.

*RQ6: A continuous social relevance metric SR(p).* The current PMIP model uses a binary SR(p) ∈ {0, 1}. Research: development of a continuous metric SR(p) ∈ [0, 1] incorporating: (a) the size of SIC(p) as a function of total NFC; (b) intensity of participation (mean contribution); (c) temporal stability (retention); (d) qualitative factors (expert assessments). Theoretical question: are the key PMIP properties (universal realizability, scale inversion) preserved under continuous SR? Practical question: does continuous SR allow for more efficient resource allocation among projects?

*RQ7: Cross-CSC interaction protocols.* Formalization of protocols for interaction among independent cybersocial corporations. Three levels: (a) informational (exchange of project and participant metadata); (b) economic (token exchange, cross-CSC investments); (c) governance (joint proposals, inter-system voting). Formal requirements: interoperability without violation of autonomy; preservation of A1 (decentralization) during interaction; conflict resolution mechanisms at the meta-level. Analogues: IBC protocol (Cosmos), cross-chain bridges, but at the governance level, not only the transactional level.

*RQ8: The influence of AI on the quality of democratic decisions.* Experimental research: measurement of the influence of an AI summarizer and AI simulator (AiC Circuit 1) on the quality of decisions in MacroeconomicDAO. Methodology: A/B testing on two cohorts (with and without AI support). Metrics: (a) time to decision; (b) voter informedness (post-vote test); (c) decision quality (expert assessment after 6 months); (d) participation rate. Ethical question: does the AI summarizer create "filter bubbles" in governance? How can "adversarial diversity" be ensured in AI aggregation of arguments?

**Long-term (5+ years):**

*RQ9: Meta-SES — evolutionary selection among CSCs.* Formalization of an "ecosystem of ecosystems": multiple interacting CSCs compete for participants, resources, and attention. Meta-SES as a second-order evolutionary game: CSCs with more efficient institutional designs attract more participants, while less efficient ones adapt or dissolve. Theoretical questions: (a) does a Nash equilibrium exist in meta-SES? (b) does meta-SES converge to an optimal institutional design? (c) what is the role of "institutional diversity" (analogous to biodiversity)? Connection to the literature: institutional Darwinism (Hodgson, 2002), cultural evolution (Boyd & Richerson, 1985).

*RQ10: Emergent properties of large-scale DAO ecosystems.* Agent-based modeling of an ecosystem comprising hundreds of interacting DAOs (four-class). Questions: (a) do stable patterns of specialization emerge among DAOs? (b) do "DAO clusters" form analogous to Porter's industrial clusters? (c) what is the critical mass of DAOs required for a self-sustaining ecosystem to emerge? (d) is a "tragedy of the commons" possible at the meta-level (among DAOs) and how can it be prevented? Methodology: ABM (Mesa / NetLogo) calibrated against GyberExperiment data.

*RQ11: CSC vs. traditional institutions: a comparative analysis.* The first rigorous empirical comparison of CSC and traditional organizational effectiveness. Metrics: (a) transaction costs (Williamson framework); (b) Pareto efficiency of resource allocation; (c) innovation rate (new projects/year per participant); (d) participant satisfaction (survey); (e) resilience to shocks (observed response to external crises). Design: matched comparison — CSC projects vs. comparable startups/non-profit organizations of similar size. Limitation: GyberExperiment at Phase III stage (≥5,000 participants) is required for statistical significance.

*RQ12: The philosophy of CSC as a socio-economic subject.* Fundamental question: is CSC a "subject" in the philosophical sense? Does CSC possess a moral status distinct from the sum of the moral statuses of its participants? Is CSC, as a unified entity, responsible for decisions made through DAO voting? Connection to the debates: corporate moral responsibility (French, 1984; List & Pettit, 2011), agency of AI systems, collective intentionality (Searle, 1995). Practical question: how does the legal person of DUNA relate to the "moral person" of CSC? Can CSC be a "bearer of rights" in a sense analogous to human rights?

### 10.5 Invitation to Participate

CyberSocium is not a finished solution. It is an experimental platform, a research program, and a working hypothesis — one in which AI is not an external supplement but an integral component that amplifies every theoretical mechanism, from PMIP to SES, from feedback loops to the four-class DAO system.

We invite:

- **Researchers** — for empirical verification of theoretical models, development of new formal results, and critical analysis of the proposed architecture
- **Developers** — for implementing the architecture in code, auditing smart contracts, and developing DSP, GyberNet, and GyberComputer
- **Economists** — for calibrating tokenomics parameters, modeling scenarios, and analyzing the economic properties of the system
- **Legal scholars** — for developing the DUNA legal framework, analyzing regulatory compatibility across jurisdictions, and drafting contract templates
- **AI engineers** — for implementing AiC, developing Sybil detection models, AI audit, and Federated Learning pipelines
- **Designers and UX researchers** — for creating interfaces that lower the cognitive barrier to participation in DAO governance
- **All who are dissatisfied with the status quo** — for participation in ecosystem projects, testing governance mechanisms, and forming a new type of socio-economic relations

To join, use the following channels:
- **GitHub:** `github.com/gybernaty` — code, documentation, issues
- **DSP:** Decentralised Social Platform — direct engagement with the community
- **GBR Token:** `0xa970cae9fa1d7cca913b7c19df45bf33d55384a9` (BSC) — economic participation

> *"The exclusive goal and opportunity of a society is the most effective unification of its participants in the interests of the society itself."*

This principle is not an ideal to be attained in the future. It is an engineering specification that can be realized now. MacroeconomicDAO is the instrument of this realization. And CyberSocium is the theoretical foundation upon which this realization is built.

We stand at the beginning of the path. The theoretical apparatus has been set forth, the architecture has been designed, and the first components have been implemented. What lies ahead is the most challenging part: empirical verification, parameter calibration, attracting a critical mass of participants, and demonstrating that a decentralized, self-governed, AI-augmented economic system is capable not merely of existing, but of surpassing traditional institutions in efficiency, fairness, and resilience.

If this succeeds — GyberExperiment will become not simply a successful project, but a proof of existence for a new class of socio-economic systems. If not — the experimental data will enrich theory and reveal which of our assumptions were mistaken. In both cases, knowledge expands, and the next attempt will be better informed. This is the essence of the scientific method, and this is the essence of GyberExperiment.

### Ethics of Technological Design and Philosophy of Institutions

139. Lessig, L. (1999). *Code and Other Laws of Cyberspace.* Basic Books. [Reissued as *Code: Version 2.0*, 2006]. The first articulation of the "code is law" thesis — software architecture as a form of regulation that defines the space of possible actions.
140. Winner, L. (1980). *Do Artifacts Have Politics?* Daedalus, 109(1), 121–136. A classic essay on the political dimension of technical artifacts; Moses's bridge as an example of the architectural codification of social inequality.
141. Friedman, B., Kahn, P.H. (1996). *Value Sensitive Design: Theory and Methods.* University of Washington Technical Report. A methodology for designing technical systems with deliberate attention to the values of stakeholders; three components: conceptual, empirical, technical.
142. Rawls, J. (1971). *A Theory of Justice.* Harvard University Press. Two principles of justice: equal basic liberties and the difference principle; the "veil of ignorance" as an idealization of the situation of institutional design.
143. O'Neil, C. (2016). *Weapons of Math Destruction: How Big Data Increases Inequality and Threatens Democracy.* Crown. Analysis of systemic bias in algorithmic systems; relevant to the AI components of AiC.
144. Popper, K.R. (1945). *The Open Society and Its Enemies.* Routledge. The argument for institutions that permit the correction of errors; the "paradox of democracy" — can a democracy vote for its own abolition.
145. Sen, A. (2000). *Development as Freedom.* Oxford University Press. The capability approach: the quality of an institution is determined by the ability of agents to expand their capabilities, rather than by the optimality of outcomes.
146. Thaler, R.H., Sunstein, C.R. (2008). *Nudge: Improving Decisions About Health, Wealth, and Happiness.* Yale University Press. The concept of "libertarian paternalism" — designing choice architecture without restricting freedom of choice.
147. Hirschman, A.O. (1970). *Exit, Voice, and Loyalty: Responses to Decline in Firms, Organizations, and States.* Harvard University Press. Three strategies for responding to a deterioration in institutional quality; fork as a realization of "exit" in CSC.
148. Varoufakis, Y. (2023). *Technofeudalism: What Killed Capitalism.* Bodley Head. The thesis of the platform economy as technofeudalism; a radicalization of the critique of digital capitalism.
149. Maturana, H.R., Varela, F.J. (1980). *Autopoiesis and Cognition: The Realization of the Living.* D. Reidel. The theory of autopoiesis — the self-reproduction of living systems; formalization of the concept of a self-referential system, applicable to CSC.
150. Luhmann, N. (1984). *Soziale Systeme: Grundriß einer allgemeinen Theorie.* Suhrkamp. Social systems as systems of communications; functional differentiation — the division of society into subsystems (economy, law, science); CSC as a new functional subsystem.

### Evolutionary Game Theory and Simulation

151. Maynard Smith, J. (1982). *Evolution and the Theory of Games.* Cambridge University Press. The concept of evolutionarily stable strategy (ESS); formal conditions for the stability of cooperation in repeated games.
152. Boyd, R., Richerson, P.J. (1985). *Culture and the Evolutionary Process.* University of Chicago Press. A formal theory of cultural evolution; transmit-or-innovate mechanisms; analogues of SES at the level of cultural institutions.
153. Hodgson, G.M. (2002). *Darwinism in Economics: From Analogy to Ontology.* Journal of Evolutionary Economics, 12, 259–281. Institutional Darwinism; conditions for the applicability of the evolutionary analogy to economic institutions.
154. Kauffman, S.A. (1993). *The Origins of Order: Self-Organization and Selection in Evolution.* Oxford University Press. NK-models, edge of chaos, order for free; the formal basis for analysis of CSC as a complex adaptive system.
155. Axelrod, R. (1984). *The Evolution of Cooperation.* Basic Books. Iterated prisoner's dilemma tournaments; the victory of Tit-for-Tat; conditions for the emergence of cooperation without centralized enforcement.
156. Nowak, M.A. (2006). *Evolutionary Dynamics: Exploring the Equations of Life.* Harvard University Press. The formal apparatus of evolutionary dynamics: replicator equations, mutation-selection balance, spatial games.
157. Schelling, T.C. (1960). *The Strategy of Conflict.* Harvard University Press. The theory of focal points; coordination without communication; applicable to the formation of SIC under conditions of incomplete information.

### Tokenomics and Token Engineering

158. Voshmgir, S. (2020). *Token Economy: How the Web3 Reinvents the Internet.* Token Kitchen. A systematic introduction to tokenomics; token classification; design patterns; the connection between tokenomics and mechanism theory.
159. Zargham, M., Shorish, J., Paruch, K. (2020). *From Curved Bonding to Configuration Spaces.* IEEE International Conference on Blockchain and Cryptocurrency. Formalization of curved bonding as a configuration space; theoretical justification for cadCAD modeling.
160. Rouvière, J. (2019). *Token-Curated Registries 1.0.* Medium. The first formalization of TCR as a curation mechanism; applicable to the Content Registry in DDP.
161. Buterin, V. (2017). *On Medium-of-Exchange Token Valuations.* vitalik.ca. A quantitative theory of utility token pricing; the MV = PQ model for valuing GBR.
162. Samani, K. (2017). *Understanding Token Velocity.* Multicoin Capital. Analysis of the velocity problem; why high velocity of circulation reduces the value of a utility token; connection to GBR staking parameters.

## 12. Appendices

### Appendix A. Glossary

#### A.1. Core CyberSocium Concepts

| Term | Definition | Section |
|---|---|---|
| **CyberSocium** | A new interdisciplinary field studying the patterns of transition of socioeconomic systems toward decentralized, self-governing, cyber-social forms of organization | 1.1 |
| **Cyber-social corporation (CSC)** | A decentralized, autonomous, self-governing economic unit of a new type, defined as CSC = ⟨N, R, P, G, M, E⟩ (participants, resources, protocols, governance, market, environment) | 3.2 |
| **Cyber-social economy** | An economic system in which coordination is performed by protocols owned by the participants themselves, rather than by hierarchical intermediaries | 1.1, 3.3 |
| **GyberExperiment** | The first concrete realization of CyberSocium theory; includes an ecosystem of projects (DSP, GyberNet, GyberComputer, AiC) and a legal wrapper (DUNA) | 4.1 |

#### A.2. Theoretical Mechanisms

| Term | Definition | Section |
|---|---|---|
| **PMIP** | Principle of Minimal Individual Participation: individual_cost(p) = C(p) / \|SIC(p)\| → 0 as \|SIC(p)\| grows. The mechanism for distributing the cost of a project across all SIC participants | 3.4 |
| **SES** | Socioeconomic Selection — an evolutionary mechanism for selecting projects through market and social feedback, formalized as an evolutionary game | 3.5 |
| **IPI** | Idea-Project-Implementation model — a formalized project lifecycle: Idea → Project → Implementation → Operation → Legacy | 3.4.2 |
| **FRP** | Fork Resolution Protocol — a mechanism for resolving disagreements through three phases: discussion → synthesis → separation (fork) | 3.4.5 |
| **SIC** | Social Investment Circle — the set of participants N_SIC ⊆ N_FC interested in the realization of a specific project; forms the financial and resource base of the project | 3.4.1 |
| **AG** | Active Group — a subset of SIC (AG ⊂ SIC) directly participating in project implementation (developers, managers, researchers) | 3.4.1 |
| **NFC** | Full Circle of participants in the cyber-social corporation — the entire set of registered participants in the ecosystem | 3.4.1 |
| **SR(p)** | Social relevance of project p — a threshold function: SR(p) = 1 if \|SIC(p)\| ≥ threshold(C(p)), otherwise SR(p) = 0 | 3.4.3 |
| **CAS** | Complex Adaptive System — a system composed of multiple interacting agents, exhibiting emergent properties (self-organization, adaptation, evolution) | 3.6 |

#### A.3. Architectural Components

| Term | Definition | Section |
|---|---|---|
| **MacroeconomicDAO** | The meta-governance level, coordinating four classes of DAO (Social, Code, Commerce, Economic) through Cross-DAO coordination mechanisms | 4.6 |
| **Social DAO** | A class of DAO for managing social processes: community rules, moderation, event organization, membership. Quorum 5%, 72-hour voting period | 4.6.3, 5.1 |
| **Code DAO** | A class of DAO for managing the technical stack: PR merges, architectural decisions, quality standards. Quorum 10%, 96-hour voting period | 4.6.3, 5.2 |
| **Commerce DAO** | A class of DAO for commercial projects: due diligence, financing, milestone tracking, profit distribution. Quorum 15%, 120-hour voting period | 4.6.3, 5.3 |
| **Economic DAO** | A class of DAO for strategic economic decisions: tokenomics parameters, treasury allocation, cross-chain strategy. Quorum 25%, 168-hour voting period | 4.6.3, 5.4 |
| **Gbr (GBR)** | GyberCommunityToken — the primary economic instrument of the ecosystem. BEP-20 on BSC, supply 10B, address: `0xa970cae9fa1d7cca913b7c19df45bf33d55384a9` | 4.2 |
| **UnitManager** | A smart contract on BSC (UUPS + Ownable + ReentrancyGuard + Pausable + EIP-712), managing participant registration, statuses, and reward distribution | 4.2.3, App D.1 |
| **MemberStatus** | Enumeration of participant statuses: None(0) → Unit(1) → Dev(2) → LeadDev(3) → ArchDev(4) → Core(5). Determines rights, obligations, and reward amounts | 4.2.3 |
| **Hybrid Voting Power** | A combined voting formula: VP = √(GBR_staked) × ActivityMultiplier × StatusBonus. The square root reduces the weight of large holders (anti-plutocracy) | 4.6.5 |
| **Activity Score** | A numerical evaluation of participant activity (0-100), computed from G-Plan data, voting participation, and project contributions | 4.6.7 |
| **G-Plan** | The task management and activity verification system: planning, assignment, tracking, confirmation. Data are used for Activity Score and rewards | 6.4 |
| **DSP** | Decentralised Social Platform — the operating system of CSC: an environment for the IPI cycle, SIC formation, DAO governance, and everyday interaction. Implementation: DSP Client (Next.js 15) + GPROD (NestJS 11) | 4.3, 6.1 |
| **GyberNet** | The specialized blockchain of CSC. Phases I–II: functions are executed via BSC. Phase III+: proprietary blockchain with RPoS consensus | 4.4, 6.2 |
| **GyberComputer** | The community's distributed computing network: hosting DSP, training/inference AI, IPFS pinning. Participants stake GBR and provide resources (CPU/GPU/storage) | 4.5, 6.3 |
| **AiC** | Artificial Intelligence Community — the AI department of the Gybernaty ecosystem. Three circuits: internal (AI for the ecosystem), research (R&D), commercial (AI-as-a-service) | 4.8 |
| **DUNA** | Decentralized Unincorporated Nonprofit Association — the legal wrapper under Wyoming law (W.S. § 17-32, 2024). Provides legal personhood to CSC | 4.7 |

#### A.4. Technical Terms

| Term | Definition |
|---|---|
| **DSP Client** | The client-side component of DSP — the ecosystem's frontend. Next.js 15, React 19, Server Components |
| **GPROD** | Gyber Production Backend — blockchain middleware. NestJS 11, Prisma 6, PostgreSQL |
| **DDP** | Distributed Data Protocol — the distributed data storage protocol (IPFS + Content Registry + ACL). Phase 2 |
| **LP Burn** | A liquidity pool burn mechanism for initiating a project: the initiator burns LP tokens (GBR/USDT), demonstrating proof-of-commitment |
| **Buyback & Burn** | A deflationary mechanism: 2% of Commerce DAO project revenue is directed toward purchasing and burning GBR |
| **RPoS** | Reputational Proof-of-Stake — GyberNet's modified consensus: validator_weight = √(staked_GBR) × (1 + ActivityScore/100) |
| **SAPP** | Socio-App Protocol Package — the DSP extensions standard: code module + manifest + API contract |
| **DID** | Decentralized Identifier — a decentralized participant identifier: `did:gybernaty:<wallet_address>` |
| **VC** | Verifiable Credentials — verifiable attestations (status, skills, participation), signed by the issuing party's digital signature |
| **EIP-712** | An Ethereum standard for typed structured data signatures; used for activity proofs and off-chain votes |
| **UUPS** | Universal Upgradeable Proxy Standard — a pattern for upgradeable smart contracts (EIP-1822) |
| **Gnosis Safe** | A multisig wallet for treasury management: M-of-N threshold signature scheme |
| **Timelock** | An execution-delay contract: after a DAO decision is adopted, its execution is deferred by 24–48 hours for review |
| **cadCAD** | Complex Adaptive Dynamics Computer-Aided Design — the BlockScience framework for modeling complex systems |
| **GNN** | Graph Neural Network — a neural network for graph analysis; used for Sybil detection |
| **Federated Learning** | Distributed training of ML models: each node trains locally and transmits only gradient updates |
| **Soulbound Token (SBT)** | A non-transferable token bound to a wallet. Used for AI-audit attestations and reputation records |

#### A.5. Economic and Legal Terms

| Term | Definition |
|---|---|
| **TVL** | Total Value Locked — the aggregate value of assets locked in the ecosystem's smart contracts |
| **Howey Test** | The SEC's four-part test for determining whether an asset constitutes an "investment contract" (security) |
| **MiCA** | Markets in Crypto-Assets Regulation — the European Union's regulation of crypto-assets |
| **PSA** | Payment Services Act — Singapore's payment services law regulating tokens |
| **DLT Act** | Distributed Ledger Technology Act — Switzerland's law on distributed ledgers |
| **AGPL-3.0** | Affero General Public License — a license guaranteeing code openness, including network use |
| **Quadratic voting** | A mechanism in which the cost of N votes = N². Reduces the weight of large holders |
| **Liquid Democracy** | Delegable voting: a participant may delegate their vote to another participant on a specific issue |
| **Conviction Voting** | A mechanism in which a vote's weight increases over time: the longer a participant supports a proposal, the greater their weight |
